material was presented to the members of a research seminar at the
CREA, whom I thank for their useful comments. My gratitude

also goes to my wife Mette Hjort, whose questions about the theory of mimetic desire were an early impetus to the writing of this
book. Richard Macksey’s support was invaluable, and I am also

Introduction

René Girard is a bold thinker who has won international renown
.for his innovative and far-reaching claims. Many of his contentions amount to a headlong challenge to some of the most predominant and entrenched positions in a number of different disciplines, and in a sense there is not a single branch of the humanities
or the social sciences that is not in some way touched by the implications of his hypotheses. Not surprisingly, the result is that
Girard’s work has drawn a wide range of reactions. Some view his
theorizing as sweeping and reductive. That he is a literary critic
who has ventured into the uncertain terrain of “theory”—and
even worse, of interdisciplinary theory—has not helped to lend
academic legitimacy to the enterprise. At the same time, however,
Girard’s work enjoys a large and impressive international following. Scholars from a number of different disciplines have found his
hypotheses to be of great value and have creatively extended his
analyses in a number of directions.'
It should be noted, however, that those who have taken up

Girard’s project in fact tend to emphasize different aspects of his
work. There are, then, different species of “Girardians,” as well as
major disagreements between them on any number of important
points. For some, what is crucial is Girard’s anthropology, his theorizing about the nature and function of myth and ritual in archaic
social formations. Others focus more specifically on his theory of
the dynamics of social order and disorder, in particular on his emphasis on the role of scapegoating in creating a form of violent
unanimity in social groups. Girard’s speculations about the origin
of human culture are another point of focus.? In literary circles, he
is sometimes known as a poststructuralist critic who has proposed
a general theory of culture as well as innovative readings of a large
xd

xi

* Introduction

corpus of major literary texts, beginning with Greek drama and
moving on through the history of the European novel. Many literary scholars have found it possible to extend his insights in the
analysis of works that Girard himself has never discussed, and
Girard’s approach has also provided a basis for rethinking some of
the central concepts of aesthetics.* There are those who are particularly interested in Girard’s recent analyses of the Bible and of its
role in history.4 Finally, some scholars have been especially interested in the implications of Girard’s concept of mimetic desire and
have found it possible to apply this notion in political philosophy
and in economics.®
That Girard’s work has inspired this variety of developments 1s
in my opinion a very good thing, and the present study cannot
attempt to discuss them all in any detail. It is crucial to understand,
however, that Girard’s fundamental concept 1s mimesis and that all

of his important interpretive and explanatory claims are either directly or indirectly based on this notion. Traditionally, mimesis
has been taken to be more or less synonymous with “representation,” but in Girard’s usage the term embraces a much broader
range of phenomena. Mimesis is said to be a “mechanism” that
generates patterns of action and interaction, personality formations, beliefs, attitudes, symbolic forms, and cultural practices and

institutions. Girard insists that there is an intimate link between
mimesis and desire and that conflict and violence, as well as a num-

ber of pathological and self-defeating behavioral patterns, can be
explained in terms of this link. He claims that the failure to understand the link between mimesis and desire has been a fundamental
blind spot in the various conceptions of human agency with which
the humanities and social sciences have worked. The individual

agent is wrongly thought to desire spontaneously, that is, in a direct and immediate relation to the objects of desire. Contrary to
this view, Girard claims that human desire is mediated, or “mi-

metic.” This means that people desire “according to others,” not in
terms of their own intrinsic preferences. And in Girard’s opinion,
to fail to focus on the basically mimetic structure of human motivation 1s to miss the fundamental bases of human experience. Indeed, Girard goes so far as to claim that it is an increased mimetic

xiii - Introduction

propensity that is the primary factor behind the emergence of specifically human cultures: mimesis produces a sequence of interactions leading to what Girard calls the first “noninstinctual” consciousness, and this experience is the starting point for the sacred
beliefs and emotions held to be the bases of human societies. Moreover, a mimetic system of motivation is supposed to explain the
persistence and dynamics of certain invariant institutional forms as
well as some fundamental features of modernity. The theory of
mimesis is, then, the foundation of the “Girardian cathedral,” as

Jean-Pierre Dupuy has correctly noted.® Yet I would prefer to replace this metaphor with a more modern religious image: in the
theory of mimesis resides the cash value of Girard’s many explanatory notes.
The present study proposes a reconstruction of Girard’s discussions of mimesis. As I understand it, a reconstruction is an analysis

that aims at a clear and schematic presentation of some of the arguments that can be found in a particular body of writings and
involves both an elucidation and a reformulation of the author’s
views. In the case at hand, undertaking such a reconstruction is
not a redundant exercise. I think it fair to say that the uninitiated
reader who turns to Girard’s texts in search of a systematic presentation of the mimetic theory is likely to be disappointed, for what
Girard in fact offers are many highly insightful and far-reaching
general suggestions, as well as a number of provocative readings

and intricate analyses of a range of particular texts. For example, in
his early presentation of the mimetic theory in Mensonge romantique et vérité romanesque (1961; translated into English as Decezt,
Desire, and the Novel, 1966) Girard proceeds by means of detailed

descriptions of the characters and interactions figuring in a collection of major European novels. As a result, the nature of his more
general empirical claims is not entirely clear. Are the motivational
phenomena that he evokes meant to be accurate descriptions of the
depictions of desire and interaction in a particular corpus of literary works? If so, how is that corpus delimited? Does Girard also
want to say that these mimetic patterns are anything but fictional
and that the theory’s domain of application extends far beyond the
domain of literary representations? Girard wants to make the latter

xiv - Introduction

claim, but the extent to which it is supported by the literary evidence is not discussed in the study on the novel. Nor are the literary readings accompanied by any extensive discussion of the
various psychological theories that purport to analyze desire outside a literary context. In Girard’s next major book, La Violence et le
sacré (1972; translated, with the author’s modifications, as Violence

and the Sacred, 1977), the focus shifts to anthropological theory
and to the analysis of archaic cultures. The basic theory of mimesis,
while remaining fundamental to Girard’s entire argument—as
Eric Gans has pointed out—does not receive a lengthy development in this work. Moreover, here and in the following book, Des
choses cachées depuis la fondation du monde (1978; translated into
English as Things Hidden since the Foundation of the World, 1987),
significant parts of the articulation of the mimetic theory take the
form of a polemical discussion of the writings of Sigmund Freud
—to the exclusion of any mention of the relations between the theory of mimetic desire and the large body of pertinent work in nonFreudian psychology. The attentive reader of these and other
works by Girard finds important shifts in terminology and emphasis in the author’s various discussions of mimesis. For example,

Girard’s increasing investment in a hypothesis about the origin of
human culture—his “fundamental anthropology”—led him to
lay greater and greater emphasis on the role of a particular form of
imitation, “acquisitive mimesis,” which he distinguishes from the
mimetic desire of the earlier texts. Yet this distinction 1s less salient
in Girard’s recent book on Shakespeare.
It is fair to say that some readers’ comprehension of Girard’s
ideas about mimesis may suffer because of the essayistic style in
which they are couched. Although much is to be learned from
Girard’s brilliant interpretations of a wide range of important
cultural documents, the hard-nosed reader may question whether
there is a “theory” here at all, if by this is meant an explanatory
system having well-defined terms and an explicit argumentative
structure. Girard frequently asserts that mimetic desire functions
as a mechanism, yet he has never backed this contention by presenting anything remotely approximating a calculus, grammar, or
algorithm describing the putative mechanism’s states and transi-

xv

° Introduction

tion laws. Contemporary linguists and cognitive psychologists
would not typically deem themselves warranted to enjoy this kind
of freedom. Any claim that a type of linguistic competence or cognitive capacity has its basis in some psychological mechanism must
be supported, at the very least, by an attempt to provide a plausible
Operative model of the putative mechanism.” Although it may be
at once inappropriate and premature to apply such methodological norms in the complex domains dealt with by Girard, the fact
remains that the exposition of his basic hypothesis has suffered
from an absence of analytic rigor. As I argue in some detail in
chapter 1, there are important ambiguities in the descriptions of
the most minimal and most schematic elements of the phenomenon of mimetic desire. The result is that there is a significant degree of open-endedness in Girard’s presentation of his views concerning mimetic desire (and a very broad range of disagreement
among his interpreters). The conclusion to be drawn, I think, is
that his writings present a far-reaching and suggestive collection
of general insights and hypotheses but not a theory in any strict
sense.8 For these reasons, a more systematic reconstruction could
be worthwhile.
The reconstruction I propose is at once analytic and selective.
My first and primary goal is simply to clarify some of the basic
ideas about the role of mimesis in human experience. To that end, I
draw distinctions between Girard’s various notions, such as “acquisitive mimesis,” 0 « “mimetic desire,” “external and internal mediation,” “conflictual mimesis,” and so on, and I discuss the relations

between them. In my effort to identify what is unique in Girard’s
intuitions about desire, I have drawn on various sources in the philosophy of psychology and action theory, for I believe that this

important body of work provides concepts that are crucial to a reconstruction of the theory of mimetic desire. I have also found it
useful to relate Girard’s terms and assumptions to empirical and
theoretical work in non-Freudian psychology. Here 1s a domain in
which the specificity of Girard’s insights needs to be recognized,

for Girard has something new to say about a theme that has long
been central to psychology.
Imitation, it has been observed, served as a “simple and sov-

xvi

« In troduction

ereign” explanatory principle in a number of early psychological
theories.? When imitation was not explicitly set forth as the central
psychological concept, its place was often taken by some closely
related notion, such as suggestion, contagion, copying, sympathy,
modeling, or identification.!° Imitation is neither simple nor sovereign, however, as far as contemporary social psychologists are
concerned. They deny that imitation or any other single notion
can serve as a universal explanatory concept in the study of social
influence and interpersonal interaction. Moreover, they assume
that there are distinct types of cognitive and motivational processes at work behind what is vaguely identified as imitation; the
term, then, does not refer to a unique natural kind.
This well-warranted distrust of simple and sovereign concepts
of imitation should not, however, be allowed to obscure the fundamental importance of various imitative phenomena; nor should

we fail to recognize that an impressive amount of research on
imitation has been conducted in the recent past. A vast array of
empirical studies document the presence of imitative phenomena
in a number of domains. To evoke only a few examples, one may
point to work arguing that imitation is an indispensable part of
children’s acquisition of language.!? Various studies emphasize the
importance of imitation in the development of gender identity.}8
Others stress its role in children’s development in general.!4 A large
body of work emphasizes the role of imitation in the learning of
aggressive behavior.!5 Many studies of what is called the “Werther
effect” explore the evidence for the conclusion that an action
as important as suicide may be prompted by the imitation of a
model’s self-destruction in a televised fiction.!6 A broad range of
work focuses on the learning of imitative behavior.!’ Finally,
various studies discuss the advantages of imitation for the imitator.!8 This is but a tiny sampling of research on imitation, but it
should suffice to demonstrate that far from being an outdated
nineteenth-century psychological concept, “imitation” and related
terms continue to play a central role in contemporary psychology.
In such a context, Girard’s hypotheses about the pragmatics of imitative interaction, and more specifically, about the influence of

xvii

« L;ntrod. uction

imitation on motivation, represent a unique and significant contribution that merits a careful examination.
In what follows, I do not seek to evaluate Girard’s contributions

to literary scholarship.'9 I do not ask whether his various readings
of literary works are “correct” or “valuable” when viewed as literary “interpretations” or “appreciations” of those works. I do not
take up the question whether Girard is right in claiming that he has
understood the intentions of Shakespeare and Proust. Nor do I
discuss the question whether Girard’s readings of literature contribute to a realistic knowledge of literary history. Instead, my goal
is to discuss Girard’s readings in terms of their heuristic value. To
grasp the specific nature of this heuristic value, it is necessary to
understand that the discussion is situated in an interdisciplinary
context, a context, moreover, where the goal of literary readings is
to help build hypotheses within a nonliterary research program.
Such is the nature of Girard’s ambitions, and by critically evaluating his work in these terms, we are more faithful to him than are
those who perceive him as merely another clever literary elucidator.
The following study focuses selectively on one aspect of
Girard’s hypotheses at the expense of others. Although I do analyze the “fundamental anthropology” in some detail in chapter 4,
no effort is made there to defend all of Girard’s speculations concerning the origin and dynamics of human culture. An important
facet of Girard’s work that receives only a cursory critical discussion here is the “philosophy of history” implicit in Girard’s work, a
philosophy that I take to be of the “substantive” variety.?° In what
follows, I do make use of some of Girard’s statements about cer-

tain large-scale sociocultural changes that have characterized the
history of Western civilization. Yet Iam not in agreement with him
on several major points. In particular, I find some of his. eschatological themes quite unfounded.?! Intimately connected to
this disagreement is my stance on the theological and religious elements in Girard’s work, for the philosophy of history in question
pivots upon nothing less than a claim about the impact that a truly
divine revelation has had on the human social order. My own approach to religious phenomena can be characterized rapidly as

xviii

* Introduction

Durkheimian in the sense that I assume that explanations of such
matters should be sought exclusively at the sociological and psychological levels of description. This means that I do not need
Girard’s hypothesis that the scientific project is a “byproduct” of
the “subterranean” Revelation being wrought by the Holy Scriptures.22 My assumption is that many of Girard’s original insights
into human interaction and motivation are logically separate from
such theological claims, just as the mimetic psychology does not
depend conceptually on the genetic anthropology.
To conclude this introduction, I shall present a brief outline of

the main steps of my argument. I begin by evoking some of the
ambiguities in discussions of Girard’s concept of mimetic desire
and survey some of the major ways in which this idea has been
construed. My conclusion is that these approaches are incompatible and that none of them adequately expresses Girard’s most valuable intuitions. Turning to theoretical psychology and action
theory, I elaborate aspects of a psychological framework within
which a more adequate theory of mimetic desire can be elucidated.
I discuss the problem of identifying the role played by desire and
other motivational concepts in psychological explanations and
take up the dispute between cognitivist and conativist theories. I
criticize Girard’s misleading rhetorical claim that he reduces all
propositional attitudes to a mechanism of desire, and I demonstrate that, on the contrary, descriptions of complex relations
between desires, beliefs, and intentions are a crucial part of his
analyses.
In chapter 2 I identify the basic elements of a minimal episode
of mimetic desire. A key feature of this analysis is an emphasis on
the desiring agent’s beliefs about the model, for in the approach
proposed here, it is the desiring agent’s “tutelary beliefs” about a
model that effectively generate an imitative form of desire. In an
effort to capture the specificity of Girard’s notion, I contrast his
concept of mimetic desire to the kind of imitative process highlighted by Albert Bandura’s theory of social learning. I point out
that there are some radically different ways in which one may de-

sire “according to the other,” and thus I distinguish between imitative and emulative forms of mimetic desire, a distinction that cor-

xix

° Introduction

responds to Girard’s emphasis on the difference between external
and internal mediation. In the same context, I discuss some of the

thorny issues that surround the problem of individuating the contents, or “objects,” of attitudes of desire.

In chapter 3 I discuss ways in which my definition of a minimal
episode of mimetic desire can be extended to embrace more significant sequences of interaction. I distinguish between mimetic modeling and personal mediation and then focus on Girard’s analysis of
double mediation. Of special interest in this regard are the complex cognitive dimensions of Girard’s model, including his emphasis on the crucial function of certain forms of misrepresentation, which I relate to Alfred Mele’s recent philosophical analysis
of self-deception.
In chapter 4 I turn to Girard’s claims about the specificity of a
primary, “acquisitive” form of mimesis and argue that the latter
should not be conflated with the notion of mimetic desire. I outline Girard’s schematic hypothesis concerning the origin of human
culture and identify its key assumptions, discussing ways in which
it may be criticized and defended in future research. I analyze his
claims concerning the typical interactive scenarios of a violent crisis and its resolution, in both precultural and cultural contexts.

The key topic here is the precise manner in which the different
types of mimesis discussed by Girard may be related to the genesis
and orientation of aggressive and conflictual interactions, and
with this in view I discuss Girard’s concept of acquisitive mimesis
and its relation to the notions of motor mimicry, conflictual mimesis, and mimetic desire. I discuss, but do not pretend to resolve,

the crucial problem concerning how a nonrepresentational form
of mimesis can be thought to generate symbolism and cultural
institutions. Reference to key assumptions and issues in Emile
Durkheim’s theory of religion sets the stage for an examination of
Girard’s treatment of the question of the origin and historical revelation of a fundamental misrepresentation of social process.
In chapter 5 I focus on the central theme in all of Girard’s analyses, namely, the conditions and consequences of violence in human interaction. I contend that there should not be a necessary
link in the theory between mimetic desire and rivalry or violence. I

xx

« Introduction

also raise questions about the mechanistic nature of Girard’s explanations of sequences of violent interaction. This discussion is
focused on his assertions about the mimetic motivation of cycles
of revenge. Reference to a depiction of a cycle of revenge in an
Icelandic saga makes possible a clarification of Girard’s statements
about the mimetic dimensions of feuding, and I discuss the kinds
of cultural elements that have to be in place for a mimetic hypothesis to be plausible.
In short, what follows is a selective interpretation of Girard’s
evocative writings. I do not claim to have exhausted his many
themes and am all too aware of the fact that in relation to his
broad, substantive vision, my efforts may seem rather “pale and
bloodless.” Yet I would be quite content if the present reconstruction succeeded in demonstrating the value of some of Girard’s insights in the context of a scientific literature characterized by epistemic prudence and analytic precision.

Models of Desire

Chapter One

Approaches to
Mimetic Desire

Interpreting the Basic Intuition

In a deceptively simple and far-reaching expression, René Girard
refers to mimetic desire as le désir selon PAutre, “desire according to
the Other.”! He contrasts mimetic desire to desire selon soi, desire

that is a spontaneous and autonomous manifestation of an individual’s inherent wants or preferences. Instead of having an “immediate” relation to his or her objects of desire, the person who desires
muimetically has a “mediated” relation to them, one that takes a detour through a model. Desire, then, 1s said to be “triangular,” for

its most basic structure involves at least three terms: the agent who
desires, the object of this agent’s desire, and the agent who serves
as the “model” or “mediator” of the desire.
In spite of its apparent simplicity, this contrast between mimetic and nonmimetic types of desire raises many difficult issues,
and in what follows I shall be at pains to sort some of them out. Yet
some readers may question the necessity of such a clarification. Is
the idea of desire “according to the other” really so very complicated? After all, various literary critics have found it possible to

apply the Girardian conception of desire to literary works without
engaging in any prior clarification of its terms.” Nevertheless, what
the specific tenets of the mimetic psychology are is not, in fact, so
very obvious, and as I shall demonstrate in a moment, there are on

this score some fundamental ambiguities in Girard’s own writings
1

2 - Models of Desire

as well as in the large body of published literature that surrounds
them.
As a first example, we may evoke one way of understanding the
basic insight conveyed by the notion of mimetic desire, a way that
may seem obvious to some but that does not really stand up to
careful scrutiny. Mimetic desire is often contrasted to “romantic”
myths of the individual’s spontaneity. In keeping with this contrast, it is sometimes assumed that the mimetic theory defines the
desiring subject as a passive entity whose desiring states are fashioned automatically upon contact with an external reality, the desires of others. Yet if the notion of mimetic desire is to be plausible
at all, it must be recognized that mimetic desire involves complex
interactions between the desiring agent and external states of affairs. Individual agency is the effective locus of a spontaneous, and
in some sense selective, process, even if this process is not a matter
of fully reflective and lucid deliberation. The selectivity in question, which is inevitable in any instance of mimetic desire, has several facets. First there is the selection of the model. It should be
obvious that people cannot plausibly be thought to imitate the desires of any human being of whom they happen to be aware. There
must be a singling out of which agent is to function as a model at a
given moment. A second element of selectivity pertains to the determination of the particular states of desire that are imitated. It
should be obvious that people do not have some kind of direct access to other people’s states of desire, all or any of which would be
automatically imitated. This point is implicit in the possibility of
error and illusion: we can imitate models whom we wrongly believe to exist, just as we can adopt desires erroneously imputed to
others—two kinds of cases that play an important role in Girard’s
analyses. Advertisers who pay celebrities large sums of money to
pretend that they avidly enjoy certain products understand this
phenomenon perfectly well, for they assume that the star’s prestige
will be associated, in the minds of the potential consumers, with

the commodities, even though the star’s expression of desire may
be totally insincere. Another sort of selectivity comes into play in
those cases where the model’s desire does have an independent reality known to the imitative agent. What does it mean to say that

3 - Approaches to Mimetic Desire
one person desires “according to” or “the same thing as” someone
else, as in Girard’s remark that “mediation begets a second desire
exactly the same as [parfaitement identique a] the mediator’s” (DDN

7/21)? These notions are anything but simple, and mimetic desire
may in fact involve a selective transformation of the model’s desire.
This last type of selectivity will be discussed in detail below, where
I shall argue that it is particularly important to take it into account
when we try to evaluate some of the claims that Girard has made
about the relation between mimesis, rivalry, and aggression.
Given the selective nature of mimetic desire, in what sense can it

be said that the mimetic psychology differs fundamentally from
romantic conceptions of the individual? It is sometimes taken for
granted that the distinction hinges on assumptions about agents’
ability —or inability—to be aware of the source of their own desires. Jean-Michel Oughourlian, for example, has proposed that
romantic psychologies have two blind spots, for they do not acknowledge the role played by desire in the constitution of the self,
and they fail to understand the role that others play in the formation of desire. In the pathological forms of romantic individualism, this lack of awareness is replaced by a deluded affirmation of
the individual’s ability to desire spontaneously.* This is not, however, an adequate interpretation of the most basic and general intu-

ition behind the notion of mimetic desire. Girard explicitly refers
to cases where the agent of mimetic desire not only is acutely aware
of the sway of this desire but also understands its mediated nature. For example, according to Girard’s reading, Don Quixote has
many illusions, but he does not think he can find the right path in
life without referring to the example set by his model, Amadis
de Gaule. Don Quixote knows that his desires are imitative but

wrongly believes that it is possible and desirable for him to realize
the style of life associated with his fictional model. It is true that in
other contexts Girard insists that in some forms of mimetic desire,

the agent has no knowledge of the role of the mediator in the formation of desire. For example, he asserts that the “spontaneity”
and “freedom” of Dostoevsky’s underground man are a myth because the agent does not understand the influence that the others,
such as the insolent officer and Zerkov, have on his desires (DDN

4. - Models of Desire

256—59/289—92). This is an important point, and I shall return
below (especially in chapter 4) to the issue of the constraints on a
mimetic agent’s self-understanding. I shall argue that epistemic
constraints do not provide an adequate general characterization
of mimetic desire and are not as simple and straightforward as
Oughourlian’s formulation suggests. Such phenomena as selfdeception and distorted and incomplete self-concepts, I contend,
are not the defining characteristics of mimetic desire.
What, then, is the central insight of the mimetic psychology if it
is not a matter of a critique of myths of spontaneity and self-awareness? The fact is that there are many different ways of developing
the notion of mimetic desire, each of which amounts to a specifica-

tion of the underlying dispositions that may be attributed to
agents when we say that they desire “according to the other.” As
these ambiguities surrounding mimetic desire have not been obvious to everyone, it may be useful to evoke some of the different
approaches that exist before I move on to propose my own reconstruction of the concept. In a sense, the interpretations to be
surveyed all try to respond to a single question: What does the
imitative agent really want? In other words, what is the implicit
structure and essential orientation of the mimetic system of motivation? Some of the answers that have been proposed are tendencies within Girard’s own writings, while others are more or less
faithful extrapolations from them.

The Idolatrous Animal

Charles Baudelaire expressed a key intuition when he wrote:
“C’est toujours Panimal adorateur, se trompant d’idole.”> Following this approach, the real goal of mimetic subjects, the secret reason for their witting and unwitting erecting of models, masters,
and mediators above themselves, is a persistent quest for transcendence. Humanity’s sense of finitude, mortality, and imperfection,
its uneasy position between what Blaise Pascal called the “two infinities,” is the existential basis of a universal desire for salvation,

the perverse expression of which is the idolatry of mimetic desire.

5 - Approaches to Mimetic Desire

With a slight change of emphasis, the same interpretation may be
understood as saying that what the mimetic agent desires is the
Being of the Other, wrongly imagined to be superior. If this is the
case, it 1s clear that the theory hardly leaves the mimetic subject’s
desire indeterminate; on the contrary, it is essentially determinate
because characterized by a profoundly religious essence in one version and by a basic ontological or metaphysical preoccupation in
another.®
Certain of Girard’s own formulations support this line of interpretation. The epigraph to Mensonge romantique et vérité romanesque
is a citation from Max Scheler to the effect that human beings must
have either a god or an idol, and throughout the book, the terms

“metaphysical desire” and “mimetic desire” are used interchangeably. We read here that the mimetic subject suffers from an “ontological sickness” and seeks a remedy in futile attempts to acquire
the “being” of the “Other” (the capitalization, which echoes JeanPaul Sartre’s usage, goes unexplained in the 196] text and is subsequently abandoned). The desire for transcendence is presented as a
universal fact of human experience: in the absence of a “vertical
transcendence,” there is the world of a “deviated transcendence,”

where men become gods for each other. A similar emphasis on the
mimetic agent’s quest for “being” reappears in the discussion of
mimesis at the center of La Violence et le sacré: here as well the mimetic subject is said to “lack being.” This is what leads people to
imitate the desires of a model, for the model is someone who seems

to have a plenitude of being (VS 146/204). Should we conclude,
then, that an essential desire for “transcendence” and “being” is the

fundamental orientation of the mimetic agent? As we will see below, these terms hardiy exhaust Girard’s intuitions.

The Uncertain Subject

The key idea here is that the agent suffers from an essential indeterminacy or lack, and the attempt to put an end to this lack is the
guiding motive. In one version, a firm sense of personal identity is
the real goal of the mimetic subject, the consistent object of the

6 - Models of Desire

agent’s various relations to the model, be this model real or imaginary. This subject’s essential problem is that of coming up with an
answer to the question, “Who am I?” As no solution may be had by
peering into the abyss of solitude, it is imagined that the response
is to be found in the eyes of the others. The labels for the real object
of the mimetic quest may vary: some speak of recognition, others
of self-determination and self-esteem. In less cognitive and philosophical versions, this is the “other-directed” subject, desirous,
above all else, of the others’ love and approval.

In another variant, instead of puzzling over the problem of selfidentity, the uncertain mimetic creature puzzles over the nature of
the world, especially the question of its values. The guiding question is not “Who am I?” but “What is to be done?” The mimetic
agent seeks in others the knowledge he or she lacks. Perhaps these
others know which objects and actions are inherently best; perhaps some other person can be found who has a privileged viewpoint. Girard touches upon this idea in the following phrase:
“When modern theorists envisage man as a being who knows what
he wants, or who at least possesses an ‘unconscious’ that knows for

him, they may simply have failed to perceive the domain in which
human uncertainty is most extreme” (VS 145—46/204). And again:
“The human subject does not really know what to desire, in the last
resort. He is quite incapable on his own of fixing his desire on one
object and, on his own, of desiring that object consistently and
relentlessly. That 1s why he is given over to the paradoxes of mimetic desire” (TH 373/478).
Another subvariety of this approach may be identified: André
Orléan’s rationally mimetic agent, who knows that he or she does
not know which choice is best.”? Having found another agent who
makes a choice, mimetic agents copy this choice, not because they
believe that the model certainly knows, but because they think
there is a chance that the model might know. Or again, it may be
that the rationally mimetic agent knows very well that the others
have no real knowledge of the intrinsic value of things and considers that the others are also behaving mimetically. In this case,
the rationally mimetic agent understands that the pragmatic value
of the object—for example, its price on the market—will effec-

&

Approaches to Mimetic Desire

tively be determined by the results of a mimetic interaction that
happens to converge on a particular unanimous decision. In this
manner, rationally mimetic agents enhance their own (subjective)
probability of success. In another variety, the situation is similar,

but the mimetic agents in fact believe that the model possesses the
lacking knowledge. This mimetic agent is mimetic because he or
she believes that the model truly knows something; the model is
thought to have a “secret” knowledge as to what is truly valuable
and desirable.

The Ambivalent

Mime

According to this line of interpretation, which is inspired by a certain reading of G. W. F. Hegel and Alexandre Kojéve, to say that
desire is mimetic is to say that desire is always an improper desire
for the proper, a self-consciousness that seeks itself in the other,
the other being perceived as a self who dispossesses oneself from
oneself. Thus Mikkel Borch-Jacobsen claims that a profoundly ambivalent relationship between self and other is prior to any specific
manifestation of desire, and he posits this ambivalence as the
fundamental intersubjective relation. The essence of this relation is
conflict: “Desire is violence because it is a desire for a being of
one’s own, a desire for self-possession [parce qu’1l est désir Wétrepropre, désir de propriation], and as such, an allergic, murderous desire.”8 This conflictual desiring relation, this ambivalent mixture
of philia and netkos, is said to be prior to any particular objects of
desire; indeed, it is constitutive of, and anterior to, all of the effects

that psychological theories may label as the “objects” and “subjects” of desire. Girard’s error, then, would be his occasional em-

phasis on the role of the object in such rivalries, for at times he
states that conflict is generated by a mimetic convergence on a sin-

gle object.
Girard sometimes comes close to asserting that mimetic interaction is necessarily and essentially an objectless struggle. He has
stated, for example, that the double bind, far from being a matter

of pathological cases, is an extremely common phenomenon, “so

8 - Models of Desire

common that it might be said to form the basis of all human relationships” (VS 147/206—7). At times he asserts explicitly that
mimetic desire is essentially conflictual. Thus Girard affirms that
“mimesis coupled with desire leads automatically to conflict” (VS
146/205). In the same work he states that “mimetism 1s a source of
continual conflict. By making one man’s desire into a replica of
another man’s desire, it invariably leads to rivalry; and rivalry in
turn transforms desire into violence” (VS 169/235). In another

text we find the following characterization of desire: “Desire is the
mimetic crisis in itself; it is the acute mimetic rivalry with the other

that occurs in all the circumstances we call ‘private, ranging from
eroticism to professional or intellectual ambition” (TH 288/408).

The essential ambivalence at the heart of such pursuits is analyzed
as follows: “The subject has no wish to triumph completely over
the rival; he has no wish for the rival to triumph completely over
him. In the first event, the object would fall to him, but it would

have lost all value. In the second event, the object would attain
infinite value, but it would be forever outside his reach” (TH
361/500). Thus the subject’s relation to the conflict with the other

is one of profound ambivalence insofar as no lasting satisfaction of
desire is to be had in either victory or defeat.

The Paradoxical Narcissist

Another manner of interpreting Girard’s basic idea is to say that
the mimetic agent prizes one thing: self-sufficiency. The surest
sign of its lack is to experience a romantic desire or passion for
someone else. Yet the agent inevitably succumbs to such desires,
and their complete absence is a condition that only others can plausibly be imagined to enjoy. Thus, this subject’s model—who is
also the object of desire —is a person thought to embody a successful form of narcissism. Desire is self-defeating because a mimetic
enslavement to such a model guarantees that the subject can never
successfully realize the essential desire for individual autonomy.
Girard writes: “Desire implies a contradiction; it aims at complete
autonomy, at a near divine self-sufficiency, and yet it is imitative.”?

9 - Approaches to Mimetic Desire

In discussing Proust, Girard again adopts such an emphasis:
“Everybody believes that someone else possesses the self he wants
to acquire. That is why everybody experiences desire. . . . Since
every desire secks self-sufficiency, no one really possesses it.”!
My evocation of interpretations of the basic mimetic intuition
does not amount to an exhaustive survey of the extant readings of
Girard, but it should suffice to demonstrate that there are some

very different ways of specifying the essential dispositions of the
agent of mimetic desire. In the light of these major differences, it is
not the least bit self-evident that the term “mimetic desire” has a
univocal and coherent meaning. Does this lead us to the conclusion that the notion is ambiguous and that it should be discarded? I
think not, but I do believe that a mimetic psychology cannot coherently embrace all of these characterizations beneath some simple and sovereign concept of mimetic desire. Instead, some distinctions and subcategories must be provided, and in what follows

I shall be considering how this might be done.
One way to understand the multiplicity of portraits of homo
mimeticus 1s to consider that at least some of them are very good
depictions of different manifestations or subvarieties of mimetic
desire. Girard frequently refers to very different personality formations, attitudes, and strategies as the “stages” of a single mimetic dynamic or process, thereby suggesting that mimetic desire
is not to be conceived of as a uniform and undifferentiated entity.
Yet at the same time, Girard wants to stress the unity of the phe-

nomenon. For example, in his book on the novel, Girard asserts
that the diverse forms of the mimetic triangle are organized within
a “universal structure.” He likens this structure to a falling object,
the shape of which changes as the speed of its descent increases. If
different novelists emphasize different aspects of desire, it 1s because they observe the object at different stages of its fall. The goal
of Girard’s own theory is different: “Moving freely from one to the
other, it attempts to espouse the very movement of the metaphysical structure; it seeks to establish a ‘topology’ of imitative desire”
(DDN 94—95/113). This kind of emphasis on the “dynamics” of
mimetic desire, on the “stages” and “levels” of a single “structure”

10 - Models of Desire
or “mechanism” of desire, is sometimes carried forward into the

later texts. For example, Girard declares that “for me, there is only
one and the same structure in perpetual becoming.”!!
The implications of these claims must be explored in detail in
any thorough confrontation with Girard’s views on mimetic desire. This point has been stressed by Jean-Pierre Dupuy, who acknowledges that Girard’s theory of mimetic desire is not reducible
to a disparate collection of mimetic “figures” insofar as Girard
makes strong claims about a “dynamic” continuity between the
“stages” of mimetic desire. It is necessary, then, to state the precise

nature of the “laws governing the transitions between these different figures.” Is there a “logic of these transitions” as Dupuy suggests?!2 If so, then neither Dupuy nor Girard has presented an ex-

plicit description of this logic. Perhaps the differences between
some of the competing approaches and emphases correspond to a
focus on different moments in the development or logical progression of mimesis. Yet if these differences correspond to aspects or
moments of the same thing, what is it that remains invariant? What
are the basic and indispensable constituents of any example of
mimetic desire? Answers to such questions must be provided if talk
of the universal structure of desire “according to the other” is to
be made clear. And in the absence of this clarification, it is impos-

sible to say anything very precise about the empirical value of the
theory.
If the diverse facets of mimetic desire are to be related to each
other more systematically, it will be necessary to take up the issue
of the relation between the basic psychological concepts and the
specific contexts within which human desire finds its particular
manifestations and inflections. It may be argued that in spite of
their sweeping quality, some of the interpretations of mimetic
desire surveyed above really amount to character sketches or
snapshots of the particular clothing worn by the mimetic agent in
specific cultural contexts. What should be sought at the level of
abstract theory, then, is a formulation of mimetic desiring that is as
neutral as possible in regard to the particular contextual features
with which the motivational structures interact in actual life histories. But this requires that the conceptual models of the different

11 - Approaches to Mimetic Desire

forms of mimetic desire be kept distinct from many of the particular factors that appear in the concrete manifestation of human desire. To claim that the mimetic agent adopts an essentially religious
attitude toward the model, or again, that the imitator desires the

model’s “being,” is to fail in this regard, for this kind of stipulation
amounts to an undue generalization of one type of configuration
of belief, desire, and behavior, a generalization that needlessly
limits the potential empirical scope of the theory.
In short, the “basic intuition” of a mimetic psychology, the idea
that people may desire “according to the other,” could use some
clarification, which is indeed the central aim of the present study.
In view of the chaotic results of the interpretations that identify
the philosophical and world-historical implications of a mimetic
desire that has never been rigorously defined, it is important to
proceed slowly and to spell out what may seem painfully obvious
to some. One manner of exploring the basic intuition behind the
concept of mimetic desire 1s to isolate the differences between this
concept and some of Girard’s other concepts of mimesis. A careful

reading of Girard’s work underscores the necessity of distinguishing between “mimetic desire” and other related terms, such as
“acquisitive” (or “appropriative”) “mimesis” and “conflictual” (or
“antagonistic”) “mimesis” (for my analysis of the latter terms, see
chapter 3). If the word “mimesis” can be employed to designate a
very broad class of behavior and psychological states, then mimetic
desire, which may itself take several distinct forms, must be posited
as a subset of mimesis, a subset to be defined in terms of additional

and more complex criteria or traits. Not all behavior, and not
all desire, then, is mimetic. Girardians who favor an imperialistic

theory of mimetic desire may not like this assumption, but they
should recall that the question of the relative empirical frequency
of occurrences of the two kinds of desire is left open. I see no advantage to wiring into the model the sweeping assertion that all

instances of desire are instances of mimetic desire. Although my
approach in what follows will at first be somewhat atomistic in
that the initial aim will be to construct definitions of individual
states, attitudes, and episodes, the theory of mimetic desire in view

is a theory of personality and of social interaction that can be artic-

12 - Models of Desire

ulated in relation to descriptions of specific sociohistorical contexts. One of the boldest claims that is associated with Girard’s theory is the idea that reference to the psychological mechanisms of
mimetic desire can explain some of the major differences and transitions between sociohistorical and cultural contexts. The status of
that sort of explanatory claim will be a central topic in what follows, and I shall be stressing the difficulty of providing anything
like a rigorous, context-free description of a mimetic mechanism.

Psychological Assumptions

It is clear that some of the most important differences between interpretations of Girard’s ideas derive from the fundamentally dif-

ferent psychological assumptions that readers bring to bear on his
work. In my view, some of those assumptions betray the specificity
of Girard’s intuitions. For example, although it is true that Girard
has developed some of his ideas in a dialogue with psychoanalysis, it strikes me as a fundamental error to attempt to fold his notions back into that sort of approach. To mention only a few basic
points, Girard expressly rejects the Freudian concepts of repression and narcissism, the energetic model of libidinal forces, and the
developmental bias that situates the fundamental and lasting determinants of personality in early childhood. Some of Girard’s terminology and notions may retain a psychoanalytic flavor, but I
leave it to others to show why his work can and should be recast in
terms of some particular version of psychoanalysis. My view is that
there is no chance of faithfully elucidating Girard in this manner,
and little chance of thereby coming up with any superior psychological hypotheses.!8
In what follows I shall adopt a different strategy. My idea is that
the valuable intuitions concerning mimetic desire and interaction
can be situated within the general framework of a psychology that
explains human beings’ purposeful activity in terms of such attitudes as beliefs, desires, and intentions. Before I begin, in the next

chapter, my clarification of the basic elements of mimetic desire,
then, I must first say a few things about how I conceive of this

13 - Approaches to Mimetic Desire

general psychological framework, justifying its application to
Girard’s ideas. To that end I shall present some basic ideas from
work in theoretical psychology and in the philosophy of action.
My goal will not be to discuss all of the outstanding technical
problems in these fields, and even less do I pretend to be in a position to resolve them. I shall assume that a reconstructed theory of
mimetic desire can and should be compatible with significantly
different stances on a good number of the outstanding issues in
theoretical psychology, which is not to say, of course, that it is in-

different in regard to all such issues.
How, then, do intentionalist psychological explanations work,
and what is the place of desire within them? It may be helpful to
begin by presenting a schematic and relatively simple example.
Suppose that I look out my window at home and see my next-door
neighbor outside in his backyard. He lifts an axe and brings it
down hard on a piece of wood that he has positioned on another,
larger block of wood, and the axe head gets stuck. Then I see him
pulling up hard at the axe handle while he holds the piece of wood
down with one foot. In what is referred to as an everyday psychological explanation of this action, I assume that this behavior was
subjectively meaningful to the person who engaged in it, and I try
to understand this behavior in terms of certain mental states,

which may be referred to quite generally as attitudes or reasons. I
assume, furthermore, that I can explain the behavior in terms of
the reasons that guided and brought it about. If I assumed on the
contrary that the person had no attitudes, or that the person’s attitudes were purely epiphenomenal and played no role in guiding
that person’s behavior, then I would have no reason for trying to
attribute the right attitudes to the person or to explain that person’s doings in terms of them. But in attitude psychology, we typically begin with the assumption that we should try to arrive at a
satisfactory explanation of what people do by attributing certain
attitudes or mental states to them. We also assume that such explanations sometimes work. So when I see my neighbor strike the
piece of wood with his axe, I may assume, for example, that he

believes that a blow of this sort has some chance of splitting the
piece of wood into smaller pieces. This is an example of what are

14 + Models of Desire

called the agent’s “instrumental beliefs,” which generally take the
form “performing an action of some type X will or might have the
result of type Y.” It is often assumed that beliefs of this sort alone
do not suffice to explain why someone does something. I must also
conjecture that my neighbor has certain desires associated with
these beliefs, or more generally, I assume that he has some motiva-

tion for wanting to split the piece of wood. He wants to bring
about a certain result, and he believes that an action of a certain

type will achieve this end. The key idea here is that this belief and
this desire together orient—and perhaps even cause—an action of
the relevant type. According to Donald Davidson’s highly influential analysis, I explain what my neighbor did by attributing to him
a “primary reason,” that is, a combination of belief and “pro attitude” that together cause the action.!* All other things being
equal, I may even be able to predict accurately that my neighbor
will do something similar at a future time, on the assumption that
the same desires and beliefs will be operative.
Some philosophers have in recent times denigrated the kind of
everyday psychological attribution evoked in this example, for
they say that this kind of talk is too unreliable and imprecise to
merit the title “explanation.”!5 Even if we reject a global and metaphysical assault on this sort of psychology, it does seem important
to recognize that our intentionalist psychology is highly fallible.
An intentional explanation can fail in at least two major ways. It
fails externally when an adequate grasp of the preferences and beliefs of the actor does not seem to be enough and we want to know
how these attitudes emerged and what external factors shaped that
process.!° We want to go “upstream” to the complex conditions
that led to the agent’s being in a particular situation and equipped
the agent with a particular set of psychological dispositions. An
intentional explanation fails internally when we do not make the
correct attributions. People often attribute the wrong motives and
beliefs to each other and are often simply at a loss as to the attitudes that orient others’ actions; on many occasions, they try to
explain someone’s doings in terms of deep-seated dispositions or
personality traits when in fact the activity in question was not
guided by any such things. The fact that these kinds of shortcom-

15 - Approaches to Mimetic Desire

ings are always possible is not a sufficient reason for rejecting this
sort of approach entirely; on the contrary, it strikes me as a good
argument for the importance of trying to clarify and improve it.
A key problem in the theory of action is how to identify the
ingredients of a successful action explanation.!” An aspect of this
issue that is directly pertinent to Girard’s theory centers on the role
of desire and other motivational terms. In this regard, we may refer
to the debate between “Humean” and “anti-Humean” positions,
or between what are sometimes called the “cognitivist” and “conativist” stances on the role of desire and other motivational concepts in intentionalist psychology.!8 Conativism is in general the
view that cognitive states alone cannot generate action: there must
be at least one motivational state whenever there is genuine action.
This general “conativist” stance is compatible with the view that
beliefs are necessary to action, but the point is that beliefs are not
alone sufficient: at least one of what Davidson has called the “pro
attitudes” is also required. Julien Sorel may know that Madame
Rénal’s hand is there in the dark, within reach, but unless he is

motivated to grasp that hand, he will remain immobile. Strong
conativism, which is sometimes associated with David Hume, 1s

the view that the “passion” of desire is an essential component in
any explanation of an intentional action; desire, then, is a neces-

sary but not a sufficient condition of the latter. More moderate and
weaker versions of conativism can be developed by defining the
necessary motivational state much more broadly, but the essential
thesis is that belief alone cannot suffice to get someone going.
Cognitivists generally contend that although attitudes or states
such as desire may sometimes help to explain action, such states are
neither necessary nor sufficient conditions for action: sometimes
people do something that they do not really desire or want to do,
simply because they believe they should do it. It is coherent to
imagine a Julien Sorel who does not really desire to take Madame
Rénal’s hand but who does so because he does not want to think of
himself as a coward. Cognitivists assert that it is not legitimate to
infer that a person who voluntarily and intentionally performed an
unpleasant action under duress or coercion “desired” to perform
that action. Here we may distinguish between two versions of this

16 + Models of Desire

thesis. A moderate version of cognitivism rejects the strong conativist’s claim that desire is a necessary term in action explanations; a
stronger version of cognitivism rejects the idea that any notion of
motivation at all is necessary to action. In its most extreme form,
cognitivism claims that motivational concepts never contribute
anything to action explanations that cognitive notions could not
contribute. Don Locke, for example, argues that beliefs suffice
for action; in his “sufficient reason thesis,” desire and other moti-

vational states are excluded from the explanation of intentional
action, and motivational research in psychology is said to be
useless,72

Defining Desire and Motivation

Let us take a closer look at this debate over the role of motivational
concepts in action explanations. The key issue here has to do with
the difference between a “cognitive” and a “motivational” state.
How might that difference be explicated so that our questions
about the roles of the two types of attitudes could be answered? To
clarify this problem, let us return to my simple example. Suppose
the neighbor whom I have seen regularly chopping wood in his
backyard all winter long one year does not do so the following
year. The woodpile is still there, and it is still cold outside, and I
have every reason to assume that his fireplace remains in working
condition. How do I explain the difference in the neighbor’s behavior? Clearly, we must look for the relevant differences, which
could have to do with any number of factors. It could be that the
neighbor has had health problems and 1s no longer physically capable of chopping wood, in which case an action explanation

could not tell the whole story, for the simple reason that it cannot
tell us anything about the causes of the agent’s physical incapacity.
But let us assume that the relevant differences in fact have to do
with a change in the agent’s attitudes. We can easily invent a story
in which this is so, and we will begin with one that seems to call for
and support conativist assumptions. So here is what happened:
previously, the neighbor wanted to build fires, and therefore he

17 - Approaches to Mimetic Desire

wanted to chop the wood into the smaller pieces deemed necessary

to that end. But this year, the neighbor does not want to build fires
any more, because his desire to do so has been countered by another, stronger inclination: he now wants to stop contributing to
the pollution of the atmosphere, so he refrains from chopping
wood and building fires. So it is a change in motivation that ex-

plains the difference in behavior. Now, how could a cognitivist
give an equally good explanation of this situation without
bringing in any motivational states? Well, the cognitivist could
contend that it is a belief and not a motivational state that is decisive: first the neighbor believed it would be good to build fires,
then he believed it would be bad, and this change in belief explains
why he stopped. The cognitivist adds that such evaluative beliefs,
along with other instrumental beliefs, suffice to explain action (including refraining, if that is what the neighbor’s behavior is considered to be).
The conativist response to this position is that the cognitivist
has not really explained the two actions without relying on any
motivational notions; instead, the cognitivist has avoided talking

about a change in the neighbor’s desires by talking instead about a
change in evaluative beliefs. This, the conativist claims, is a matter

of relying on a category of attitudes that in fact play a motivational
and not a purely cognitive role. Although it can indeed be said that
the neighbor has a belief about air pollution, if it 1s going to make
a decisive difference, this belief has to involve commitment to a

kind of all-out evaluative assessment. It is not a matter of someone
having beliefs about, or knowledge of, the content ofcertain value
judgments; rather, it is a matter of someone actually adopting a

certain evaluative attitude and of this attitude playing a particular,
motivational role in influencing the agent’s activities. Moreover,
this evaluative belief amounts to a decisive preference for one kind
of state of affairs as opposed to another. According to the conativist, the key point is that the cognitivist has to agree that the
items in the example that have the most explanatory relevance are
those that the cognitivist refers to as the evaluative beliefs, and the
conativist goes on to claim that these states play a motivational and
not a purely cognitive role. But the strength of this contention of

18 - Models of Desire

course depends on our ability to say what it means for an attitude
to play one kind of functional role as opposed to another, which in
turn involves the key differences between motivational and cognitive roles. The claim is that reason explanations work with a distinction between at least two (and probably more) categories of
attitudes that are held to be capable of explaining a difference in
behavior. The two main questions that arise here, then, are (a)
how the distinction between motivation and cognitive items
works and (4) whether it is really necessary to successful action
explanations.
As is well known, it is extremely difficult to give a noncircular
definition of any one motivational term in isolation. Yet explications of one motivational concept couched in terms of other
motivational concepts hardly seem illuminating: it is not informative to say that desire is a species of sensuous longing, which is a
kind of wanting, which is a form of favoring, which is a noncomparative version of preferring.?° Yet what terms can we use to arrest
this progressive slippage of desire and anchor the category of
motivation in something else? It may seem glaringly obvious that
the difference between cognitive and motivational terms surely
must reside in their different relations to action and/or behavior,

yet the nature of this difference is especially difficult to delineate. It
has often been proposed that motivation can be defined in dispositional terms: to say that an agent has a desire for something is to
say that the agent has a disposition that would lead to a certain
behavior under the right circumstances. “John is motivated to
chop wood” is like “sugar is soluble”: John may not be chopping
wood right now, but he has the desire, or rather the disposition, to
do so. But as W. V. O. Quine has observed, in the case of such

terms as “desire” and “want,” we have no rigorous description of
the relevant conditions under which the disposition is actually
manifested— nothing that corresponds to the “when put in water”
clause in the dispositional statement about sugar’s solubility.2!
When an effort is made to specify those conditions, it is tempting
to say that desire leads to action provided that no other, stronger
desire gets in the way. But there is a danger of vicious circularity
here, for we have just included the term “desire” in the action-

19 - Approaches to Mimetic Desire

dispositional analysis of desire. What is more, human beings do
not act on all of their desires (fortunately!), which implies that it
will not suffice to say that motivational states such as desires,
wants, and preferences are dispositions to act.
A modification of the same approach is to suggest that desire

can be defined as the disposition, not to act, but to engage in certain forms of mental behavior, such as planning or some sort of
intending. This move would be supported by the idea that a person’s believing that a state of affairs is or is not the case does not
typically imply that this person is any more or less likely to entertain any particular plan relative to that state of affairs. Attitudes of
desire, on the other hand, do warrant such an inference: when an

agent stands in an attitude of desire toward a situation, he or she is
more likely (at least) to entertain the possibility of acting in some
way relative to that situation (e.g., so as to bring about the actual
existence of that situation). Yet an objection to this approach is
that people may have some desires that they never expect to see
satisfied; they never plan to act in a way intended to satisfy these
desires. If desire is a disposition, it only sometimes results in action, and only sometimes in planning and intending. What, then,

is the minimal behavioral manifestation of the disposition?
The basic problem here is that the functional role of motivational terms cannot be defined in isolation, for this class of terms

acquires its specificity in contrast to the role played by all other
kinds of attributions, for example, attributions of nonevaluative

beliefs. This means that it is systems of wants, rather than any one
in isolation, that give rise to action, and they do this in conjunc-

tion with beliefs.22 The conclusion would seem to be that the only

viable approach to action explanation is one that begins with an
interrelated system of beliefs, desires, and actions. This basic insight was already expressed by G. E. M. Anscombe in Intention, tor
she comments that “the primitive sign of wanting 1s trying to get;
which of course can only be ascribed to creatures endowed with
sensation . . . the ascription of sensible knowledge and of volition
go together.”?3 Other philosophers seem to agree that this general
strategy should be adopted. Richard Brandt and Jaegwon Kim
note that the notion of “want” gets it meaning from its systematic

20 - Models of Desire

link to “impulse,” “pleasure,” and “disappointment.” It is a sign
that one wants something when one experiences pleasure when
one suddenly gets it by surprise—other things being equal, of
course. Robert Audi weaves the concept of “wanting” into a texture of associated feelings, thoughts, and actions.74 Similarly,
Robert Stalnaker says that desire and belief are “correlative dispositional states of a potentially rational agent. To desire that P is
to be disposed to act in ways that would tend to bring it about that
P in a world in which one’s beliefs, whatever they are, were true.
To believe that P is to be disposed to act in ways that would tend to
satisfy one’s desires, whatever they are, in a world in which P (to-

gether with one’s other beliefs) were true.”25 More recently, Michael Smith has elaborated a similar position, couching the functional differences in terms of different “directions of fit” that
characterize relations between types of attitudes and the world.?¢
This kind of functional contrast is, in my view, the best approach to describing how the distinction between motivational
and cognitive terms works. The distinction ultimately rests on the
following implicit assumption: all other things being equal and
given “the same” cognitive states (i.¢., a sufficient, functional sim-

ilarity between them), differences in behavior should be explained
in terms of differences in motivational factors. The contrast is also
supported by the inverse assumption: given “the same” motivational factors (or a sufficient functional similarity between them),
differences in behavior should be explained in terms of differences
in cognitive states. The distinction “works,” then, by providing a

contrastive heuristic that guides our search for pertinent differences when we formulate action explanations. It should be noted
that the distinction is not based on the specific introspective
qualities or phenomenological properties of the two types of attitudes: the claim is not that desire feels one way and belief another,
or that the individual has infallible knowledge of these states.?7
But we have not answered the cognitivist’s second question: is
this kind of dichotomy necessary to successful action explanations,
and if so, why? Cognitivists may wish to propose some candidates
for successful action explanations that involve no such contrast.
A relevant example is briefly discussed by George Wilson, but I

21 - Approaches to Mimetic Desire

hasten to add that Wilson does not introduce this example with the
goal of advocating a restrictive, cognitivist view of action explanation.?8 Wilson imagines a case in which someone intentionally
performs an action without having any positive attitude toward it.
A public speaker has acquired the mannerism of tugging at his earlobe once or twice while giving a lecture. He is fully aware of performing this gesture while he does it. On a particular occasion
while giving a speech, the speaker knowingly raises his hand to his
ear, in order to tug at the earlobe, yet he is wholly indifferent toward this action and has no genuine motivation in favor of it. “It is
just something that he now and then does, and, after all, what is

the harm in it?” Wilson goes on to suggest that it intuitively seems
a genuine possibility that a man could perform an action X in
order to Y “without caring a bit about whether he Y’s or not.”29
Thus the “in order to” that is the mark of intentional, purposive
action 1s maintained in the absence of a motivational attribution.
But I think a conativist can respond that the example is not in fact a
plausible case of an adequate action explanation. First of all, the
example is a case of something that borders on subintentional behavior, and Wilson admits that the gesture may be performed “impulsively.” But let us reason about this gesture as if it were an instance ofintentional action. In that case, we need to grant that the
public speaker believes that he can either tug at his earlobe or not
tug at it. What needs to be explained 1s why he acts on the former
possibility, and to do that, we need to invoke some sort of motivation, albeit a rather thin one. There may be some sense in which
the man is “indifferent” to the gesture—that is, he does not believe it especially valuable, important, or desirable—but if we are

to explain the repeated, intentional gesture as purposive action
and not as the result of some purely mechanical, subintentional
process, we must also say that there is a sense in which he is not
fully and perfectly indifferent to it: for example, we might assume
that he favors it slightly because he thinks it grants him some small
release of tension when speaking in public.
The point, then, is that the agent’s effective favoring of one action as opposed to another is only explicable in terms of some kind
of effective evaluation, preference, or inclination, all of which are

22. - Models of Desire

properly motivational terms. To see this, let us return to the case of
the neighbor who has left off chopping wood. We try to explain
this in purely cognitive terms, and the analysis seems promising
when we find out that the neighbor recently read a lot of articles
about the ecological situation, including one that focused on the
role played by smoke from homeowners’ fireplaces. My neighbor
believed what he read, and this new belief acquisition is clearly an
important factor in the change in behavior. What else do we need?
But what about the possibility that my neighbor believed what he
read in the article but did not care enough to want to refrain from
building fires? That case is possible, and significantly different
from the one in which this belief led to a change in behavior. The
advent of this belief —even though it is a strong, evaluative sort of
belief in the goodness or badness of some action—is not alone
enough to explain the difference (all other things being equal). It
only becomes sufficient when we ascribe to this event the properly
motivational role of generating the agent’s intention to refrain
henceforth from fire building and the related activities.
Has the conativist won the argument with the cognitivist? I
think that the case against the extreme version of cognitivism has
indeed been made, for it seems clear that some successful action

explanations do work with the kind of contrast between motivational and cognitive terms that I have evoked. We cannot really
imagine successful action explanations where no such dichotomy
is Operative; in other words, there are no viable models of agency

involving only cognitive states or only motivational ones. Another
argument in support of this thesis, which I shall not develop here,
would suggest that it is precisely the contrast between these two
categories of interacting attitudes that is necessary to the various
important concepts that may be grouped beneath the rubric “motivated irrationality” (a good example being “wishful thinking”)
.3¢
The arguments that I have just presented militate in favor of
Girard’s strong emphasis on the role of desire in orienting human
activity. But it is important to note at this point that the arguments
I have been presenting against cognitivism do not necessarily support any of the stronger versions of conativism, and indeed, that
has not been my intention. I turn now to a discussion of what I

23 - Approaches to Mimetic Desire
take to be the valuable intuitions behind a cognitivist stance on
action explanation, for I believe that these intuitions are an important corrective to some of the prevalent excesses of conativism.
The debate between strong and moderate versions of conativism
leads quite directly to a discussion of the different senses of “desire.”3! Two very basic senses may usefully be identified: on the one
hand, “desire” is understood to designate some kind of sensuous
or passionate longing for some state of affairs; on the other hand,
there is the broad sense of desire as a very general kind of motivational state: “whatever tends to move the agent to act” (other
things being equal).3? “Desire” in this latter sense is synonymous
with “want” or “preference.” These two senses of desire must be
kept in mind if we want to avoid conflating strong and moderate
versions of the conativist thesis. The strong claim is that desire, in
the sense of appetitive or sensuous longing for something, is a necessary element in action. Yet it is possible to attack this rather extreme idea by pointing to the many cases where people hardly
seem to be motivated by any such urges. For example, one may
never have a genuine desire to take out the garbage yet may be
motivated to do so on a regular basis. In my view, the only way a
conativist can deal with such examples is by shifting from the narrow to the broad sense of “desire,” arguing that some kind of want
or preference has to be present to motivate action. The conativist
goes on to say that the deliberative process will be endless unless
some overarching desire—1in the broad sense— puts a stop to motivational regress. Dutiful agents must desire being dutiful more
than they desire the other possibilities they contemplate, for otherwise they would never form any decisive, present-directed intention to act on one belief as opposed to another. Soldiers may very
well believe that they should be dutiful instead of seeking to save
their own hides, but this belief cannot be determinant and produce
action unless the soldiers have a decisive desire to do what they
believe they should do, as opposed to something else.
The moderate conativist’s notion of “desire,” then, which is said

to be a necessary (but not sufficient) precondition to action, is a
generic motivational state, a rough-grained concept that involves
no fine distinctions between the varieties of what Davidson has

24 - Models of Desire
called “pro attitudes,” such as needing, wanting, wishing, prefer-

ring, longing for, lusting after, and so on. In my view, it 1s only this
sort of moderate conativism that survives the cognitivist’s criticisms, for many perfectly plausible action explanations work with
evaluative beliefs and preferences instead of any of the “hotter”
motivational terms (“desire” in the sense of lust, longing, or blind

motivational force). In what follows, I shall often use the term
“desire” in the broad sense, partly because I believe that Girard’s
own use of the term emerges out of a philosophical tradition that
employs it to evoke an all-encompassing motivational concept.*? It
should be kept in mind that this usage is not meant to imply that
there is never a conflict between an agent’s different desires, or that

there is no difference between an agent’s strongest desire (in the
narrow sense) and that same agent’s overall motivation, which also
embraces the agent’s values and preferences. Desire alone, even the

agent’s strongest desire, does not yield the agent’s preponderant
motivation.*4

Desire and the Subject

Some of Girard’s readers may be quite surprised that one could
even begin to entertain the idea of a rapprochement between his
views and cognitive approaches to human agency, and their conclusion may be that I have chosen the wrong sort of psychological
framework. After all, one of the central and most explicit thrusts of
Girard’s thought is his rejection of myths ofindividual autonomy,
rational control, personal lucidity, and so on: far from being a
“philosophy of consciousness,” his theory insists on the role of desire, and moreover, a type of desire that is a source of blindness,

conflict, and delusion. Girard’s theme is conation, not cognition:
he explicitly claims that he wants to break with a philosophical tradition that conceives of mimesis as representation, and not as desire and interaction. Such an ambition leads him to employ expressions in which “desire” is made the determining condition of a
very wide range of behavior. For example, Girard writes that “far

25 - Approaches to Mimetic Desire
from being unconscious in Freud’s sense and only appearing in its
true form in our dreams, desire not only observes but never stops
thinking about the meaning of its observations. Desire is always reflection on desire” (TH 328/460, Girard’s emphasis). In another
passage Girard asserts flatly that “desire is, finally, the first to acquire a knowledge about itself that it finds unbearable.”35 As we
read on, it would seem that “desire” (and the mechanism that
Girard associates with it) is “responsible” for all sorts of activities,
including a wide range of seemingly intentional actions and cognitive attitudes: desire “knows,” “misunderstands,” “forgets,”
“deceives itself,” and so on. In other words, Girard sometimes de-

scribes desire as if it were what pulled the strings of the human
marionette, and this string puller would seem to be a kind of homonculus equipped with whatever cognitive faculties are required
for complex feats of reflecting, learning, and forgetting.
I believe that Girard’s emphasis on the characteristics of human
desire and his particular analyses of them are extremely important
and offer many precious insights. Yet I must at this point present
my reservations about his apparent tendency to reduce any number of different psychological states, and most notably the full
range of attitudes, to the control of a single entity called “desire”
(sometimes the reduction is even more extreme: not only are states
of belief and intention said to be controlled by desire, they are said
to be nothing more than manifestations and effects of an Omnibus
Named Desire). The question of the status of such allegories is
