directly posed in Des choses cachées depuss la fondation du monde
when Oughourlian quite aptly poses Girard the following question about his use of the term “desire”: “You are saying that ‘desire’
does this and that. . . . Would you not agree that you are tending
to give desire a false identity? [Ne pensez-vous pas que vous étes en
train ahypostasier le désir?|}? (TH 303/428). Girard’s response,
which is also his most explicit statement on the issue, reads as
follows:
If desire is the same for all of us, and if it is the key to the system of relationships, there is no reason not to make it the real “subject” of the struc-

26 + Models of Desire
ture—a subject that comes back to mimesis in the end. I avoid saying
“desiring subject” so as not to give the impression of relapsing into a psychology of the subject. (TH 303/428)

In my view, this is a wholly inadequate justification for a usage
that, in its autonomizing of an “entity” called “desire,” in fact carries strong idealist connotations and effaces many important psychological distinctions— distinctions that Girard himself consistently and carefully makes at other points in his analyses. Desire
should not be understood as a disembodied allegorical figure, but
as a word that can be used to classify certain kinds of psychological
states. On any plausible nonidealist account, the term “desire” can
only be held to refer to some invariant features of the “states” or
“conditions” of a living organism having physicochemical instantiations. At a higher level of abstraction, one could refer to
certain invariant features ranging across the desiring states of a collection of these organisms, but it is important not to lose sight of
the fact that this is an abstraction, a classification of invariant fea-

tures of distinct states of affairs in the world. In this sense alone is
there a basis for a unified concept of “desire” that could refer truthfully to a single kind of situation. Yet various thinkers would contest the unity of desire, and Girard has not demonstrated or proven
that all particular states of desiring are in some strong sense “identical” or that there exists a single “structure” to which all such phenomena can be related. To say that there is never more than a single
and selfsame desire is very rash, a bit of bluster that can only come
between the cautious reader and the real value of Girard’s insights.
Moreover, this kind of rhetorical flourish is not supported by
what Girard himself rightly goes on to say about the important
differences between the behavioral phenomena that one may associate with desire, on the one hand, and a range of different psychological states, on the other. Some of the crucial distinctions between types and stages of mimetic desire drawn by Girard hinge
precisely on the agent’s ability or inability to have reliable beliefs
about aspects of the motivational processes and intersubjective situations in which the agent is involved, which implies that a dis-

Qk * Approaches to Mimetic Desire

tinction between desire and belief is already implicit in Girard’s
theorizing. A central example is his “law of the psychological circle,” which has to do with a kind of blind spot that is typical of
those involved in “internal mediation” (DDN 73—74/90—92). In
developing Girard’s notion of double mediation (see chapter 4), it
is certainly necessary to refer to the desiring agents’ beliefs, for
Girard explicitly links levels of blindness and awareness, concerning self and other, respectively, to the distance between subject and

mediator. Girard insists again and again on what the desiring
agent (and in his shorthand, desire itself) understands and misunderstands, forgets and remembers, expects and assumes. Nor is his

emphasis on these cognitive states compatible with the idea that
they make no difference to the course of desire. Not only does
Girard emphasize the idea that desire is always reflection on desire
but he grants this reflection a decisive role. Desire is influenced and
oriented by this reflection: “From the basis of this reflection, it
takes its direction and, from time to time, modifies its own struc-

tures. Desire is a strategist and it alters its aim, if I can put it like
that, as a function of what it has learned about itself” (TH 328-—
29/460-—61). It is quite clear in these phrases that the beliefs and
other intentional attitudes attributed to the processes of mimetic
desire are not the least bit epiphenomenal in Girard’s view, for they
are said to determine the very course of desire: desire observes,
interprets, and draws false conclusions, and it is these conclusions

that serve as the basis of ulterior states of desire and whatever actions are linked to them.
To follow the complexities of Girard’s analyses of mimetic desire, it is necessary to adopt a psychological framework that allows
us to distinguish between the roles played by motivational and
cognitive items. In spite of some of the formulations that I have
just criticized, many of Girard’s analyses are already squarely situated in the kind of psychology that I have been advocating, for he
consistently tries to explain agents’ activities in terms of meaningful relations between the propositional contents of their attitudes
and reasons. This is as it should be, for attempts to abandon this

approach run contrary to some of the most basic and uncontrover-

28 - Models of Desire

sial features of our framework of psychological understanding. Believing that a certain situation is the case is surely not the same
thing as having a desire that would be satisfied should that situation be factual. The existence of cases where one believes something to be true without desiring either the reality or one’s knowledge of it runs contrary to the idea that all beliefs are simply
“overdetermined” by our desires. Belief and desire interact, but
this does not mean that they are simply synonymous or that the
one is reducible to the other: desires typically imply certain beliefs,
but the fact of having a certain belief does not imply desire in the
same way. Consider as well some of the differences between desires
and intentions. Having formed an intention effectively to do
something at the present moment is not the same thing as, or reducible to, having a desire to do this thing. For example, I can have

a strong desire to do something without having yet formed the
intention to act on that desire. Indeed, we do not in fact act on all
of our desires, nor do we even intend to act on all of them. These

remarks only begin to suggest the complexity of our usage of notions of desire, belief, and intention, but they should suffice to re-

veal the inadequacy of any account in which all of the explanatory
weight is placed on any one of these terms.
If these arguments are accepted, what becomes of Girard’s critique of the “psychology of the subject,” with its excessive confidence in the role of the unified and masterly ego? Does an insistence On an intentionalist psychology imply a return to the
much-despised category of the subject? I think not. Particular
agents and collections of them can be identified in terms that make
no claims whatsoever about the unity and autonomy ofconsciousness, or the Subject in some strong, idealist sense.*° It is then possible to proceed to the task of analyzing such agents within the
framework of attitude psychology. Reference to states of belief,
desire, and intention need not engage us in any unwanted assumptions about the agent’s lucidity, mastery, self-knowledge, ideal rationality, perfect coherence across time, identity, and so on. Moreover, in discussing the ways in which mimetic desire is related to

beliefs, we may adopt a fairly common distinction between two
different ways of ascribing mental states to an agent. One kind of

29 - Approaches to Mimetic Desire

ascription is dispositional: here it is a matter, for example, of propositions that a particular agent is disposed to believe, without it
being assumed that this agent is aware of all of these beliefs at any
particular moment. The second type of belief ascription is a matter
of attributing a focal or “occurrent” belief to the agent, who is said
to have a particular belief at a given time, in the sense that this
belief corresponds to what the agent is consciously thinking or
aware of at that point in time.%7 This distinction is important in
the context of an analysis of mimetic desire. If, as I shall argue at
greater length below, mimetic desire is a type of desire that
emerges in interaction with certain kinds of beliefs, it is important
to understand that the beliefs in question need not always be focal
to have a role within the agent’s motivational processes and that an
emphasis on the agent’s intentionality does not wed us to some
overly rationalistic model of human beings. To stress a point that I
made earlier, the psychology of propositional attitudes is not reducible to a phenomenology of consciousness based on “qualia” or
the notion of “what it feels like.”38
I have just argued that the theory of mimetic desire belongs
within an intentionalist psychology where states of desire (and
other motivational processes) would be held to interact with be-

liefs and other attitudes in the explanation of behavior. Thus I shall
not be asking how Girard’s insights concerning imitation overturn
or completely replace everyday psychology, but how these insights
may be put to work in improving our typical intentional explanations and understandings of people’s behavior. In support of this
claim, I point to Girard’s own consistent reliance on the language
and assumptions of such a psychology, as well as to various other
independent reasons why this is the only viable direction in which
the mimetic theory could be developed.*? I develop such arguments in the next chapter. Yet Girard’s readers will know that there
is one aspect of his work that would seem to fit in poorly with the
kind of psychological framework that the present reconstruction is
advocating, namely, his emphasis on a primitive, nonrepresenta-

tional form of mimesis. A discussion of that tendency in Girard’s
work will allow me to articulate my understanding of his various
concepts of mimesis in much greater detail, for as I said earlier, it is

30 - Models of Desire

not the case that Girard works with only a single concept of imitation. In chapter 4, I return to this issue and discuss the primitive

form of mimesis that should be distinguished from the concept of
mimetic desire. But first I turn in the next chapter to the problem
of identifying some of the basic elements of a minimal definition of
mimetic desire.

Chapter Two

Elements of

Mimetic Desire

From Desire to Mimetic Desire

What are the differences between desire and mimetic desire? If
mimetic desire is desire “according to the other,” it would seem
obvious that a first factor that must be added to the elementary
situation of desiring in order to characterize a basic episode of
mimetic desire would be the desiring agent’s belief that someone
else desires something. Thus Girard writes: “At the origin of a
desire there is always the spectacle of another real or illusory desire” (DDN 105/124). We may refer to that someone else as “the
model,” but it will be useful later on to introduce the notion of the

“mediator” as well. It is important to emphasize Girard’s stipulation that the desiring agent’s belief about the model can be false.
The belief about the model may not refer truthfully to any real
agent, or in cases where this belief does refer to an existing agent, it
may not refer truthfully to this agent’s states of desire. The agent of
desire may wrongly believe that the model wishes to acquire or
maintain a purely imaginary social distinction, and may mimetically desire the same thing.
Although there must be a belief to the effect that the model has
a certain desire, the mimetic agent need not make any direct comparison between his or her own desires and the other's. The latter
stipulation is included because the theory of mimetic desire must
allow for two rather different possibilities: on the one hand, cases
31

32 - Models of Desire

in which the desiring agent does not believe that his or her desires
are oriented by beliefs about a model’s desires; on the other hand,
cases in which there is such a belief. We do not want to stipulate
that the desiring agent necessarily believes that his or her own desire and the model’s desire are the same.
More explicitly, a first difference between mimetic and other
states of desire is that in the former, the emergence of desire is
linked to the agent’s holding of a belief belonging to a certain class.
These beliefs may be labeled the mimetic agent’s “attributions of
desire,” as it is a matter of the desiring agent attributing a state of
desire to some “model.” But what is a model? First of all, the

model figures in the beliefs of the desiring agent. We assume that
the individuals, relations, situations, and events figuring as the

contents of mental attitudes may or may not refer accurately to a
real state of affairs or course of events (the model could be supernatural, nonexistent, a collective or composite subject, a memory
or phantasy of the agent’s former self—the latter case being especially important, for example, in Proust’s writings). We do not
even want to stipulate that the desiring agent necessarily believes
in the literal reality of the model. It is possible that a mimetic agent
could know very well that the model is a fictional character and
that the novelistic events never literally took place and yet believe
that events of the sort evoked by the story either could or should
transpire and be inspired to desire “according to” the fictional
other.
So far I have required the mimetic agent to have a belief about a
model and about that model’s desire. Yet the mimetic agent’s own
desire for the object must somehow be linked more firmly to the
belief that the model desires it. The issues can be clarified by means
of a schematic illustration. Suppose we have the following situation. An agent, A, has had, just prior to a point in time, t, at least
four beliefs, namely, beliefs to the effect that

. some other agent B desires S,
. some other agent B desires S’,
. some other agent C desires S, and
HN
PW
. some other agent D desires S”

33 - Elements ofMimetic Desire
(where S, S’, and S” designate significantly different kinds of situations desired). Now, suppose that we want to declare that at t,
agent A has a mimetic desire for S, and that (1) was the attribution
of desire that played the crucial role in the formation of A’s mi-

metic desire for S. Wnat makes B a model for A’s desire for S? This
is the problem of motivating the desire-belief link in the basic mimetic episode, for we want to specify that the belief plays an effective role in the generation of desire, and we want to say under what
conditions this would typically take place.
The point to grasp here is that it is not necessarily the case that
someone only has one other person in mind and can only conceive
of one desire that this person has. To say, however, that someone

desires a specific something “according to” the desire of a particular person is to stipulate that one particular model concept, and
one particular attribution of desire, was linked to (or indeed generated) the imitative desire that emerged. In the example sketched
above, this means that A desires S because A believed (1), and not
because A believed (3), although the latter could also generate a
mimetic desire for S. Also, A’s mimetic relation to model B could

also have contributed to the creation of a situation where A desires
S’ mimetically. How is the process where (1) plays an effective
role, and the others do not, determined? What conditions would

typically tend to exclude the other possibilities? Why doesn’t A’s
belief (4) lead A to have a mimetic desire for S’’?
A literary example may be used to illustrate this problem. In Le
Rouge et le now, Julien Sorel attends a ball, and as he moves slowly

through the crowd that throngs the salon of the magnificent hdtel
de Retz where the ball is being held, all of the fashionable young
men around him are exchanging words of praise for a single
woman, who is proclaimed by one of them to be the “queen of the
ball.” There seems to be a total unanimity that Mademoiselle de La
Mole’s coquettish charms are infinitely superior to those of all of
the other women, and so much so that “only a prince” could be
worthy of the “sublime Mathilde.” Yet Julien is not convinced. He

experiences no desire for Mathilde at this point in the story, in
spite of the fact that he is highly aware of the other men’s unanimous and avid desire for her. Instead of hearing their praise of

34 - Models of Desire

Mathilde and then suddenly succumbing to an overpowering
mimetic desire for her, he merely thinks to himself, “Since these

puppets find her so remarkable, she merits being studied. I will
understand what these people take perfection to be.”! Why, then,
does the “spectacle” of the young men’s desire fail to awaken
Julien’s passion? Why does he not spend the rest of the evening
pursuing her? (Instead, bored with everyone else, she follows him
around, trying to overhear his conversations.) The most obvious
way to respond to this question is to emphasize Julien’s comment
that those who voice their admiration for Mathilde are “puppets.”
They are not models for his desire, either individually or collectively, because he lacks the requisite kind of beliefs about them and
indeed holds their preferences in contempt, as something to be
studied in the manner of a sociologist studying the alien ideology
of some strange class of people.
When we apply this insight to our analytic schema, this means
that we must start adding in further stipulations about agent A’s
beliefs concerning agents B, C, and D. Perhaps some features of
what A thinks about B make the latter into a model; given other
circumstances, the agent’s beliefs about D would have led to a desire for S’’, in spite of the fact that two others were thought to
desire S. So we rephrase our question as a question about the difference, for the desiring agent, between any number of beliefs
about other agents’ desires and the kind of belief that serves as a
belief about a model ofdesire. What makes the difference? The most
plausible approach to this difference is based on the assumption
that the content of some kind of additional belief, or set of beliefs,

is what serves to make an agent into a model for some desiring
agent (which is not the same as saying that the agent must foreground the idea that the other “is a model”).
In what follows, I will call these beliefs “tutelary beliefs,” my

goal being to specify their function and typical contents in episodes of mimetic desire. This approach corresponds to at least
some of Girard’s views on the problem. First of all, his analyses
consistently presuppose—when they do not state outright—the
idea that it is the imitator’s beliefs about the model that in one way
or another qualify the model as model in the imitator’s mind. This

35 - Elements ofMimetic Desire
point is rather obvious; to present but a single example, we may
cite the following: “[The disciple] admires and respects the model;
if he had not done so, he would hardly have chosen him as model
in the first place” (TH 290/315). Girard is eloquent about the
many things that a model can represent to those who are in the
grips of mimetic desire. In their eyes, the model has a godlike quality and appears to be a unique and superb source of orientation. In
many of Girard’s descriptions of the inception of a mimetic desire,
it is clearly the prestige that surrounds the model that leads to the
‘first “selection” of this person as a source of desires to be imitated.

If this much is accepted, a crucial outstanding question concerns the viability of subsuming such beliefs beneath a single
rubric. This was precisely what was done in each of the basic approaches to mimetic desire surveyed in chapter 1, where in each
case a single quality or characteristic of the model was made the
basis of the “fundamental” mimetic orientation. Yet in my view,
each of those approaches restricted the content of the tutelary beliefs in an unrealistic manner. Here we encounter the difficulty of
keeping the concept of tutelary beliefs from betraying the explanatory value of the model. For example, one could suppose that the

agent’s mimetic orientation makes perfect sense once we realize
that agent A thinks that B embodies Sei, while A reckons poor C
and D among the lowly Sezendes! Isn’t A’s choice obvious in the
light of such a belief? The example shows that in some applications, this approach can slide into interpretations of mimetic desire that obscure the very real differences between particular manifestations of the phenomenon. By this I mean to say that it does

not seem like a good idea to attribute to all mimetic agents a belief
(or set of beliefs) having an essentially philosophical content. In
my view, the mimetic hypothesis should not attribute to everyone
a single essential desire underlying all actual states of desire. It is
reductive and dogmatic to assert that the ultimate object of all desires is some philosophical object such as “the Good,” “Being,”

“autonomy,” “spontaneity,” or “originality” or some psychoanalytical object such as “the mother,” “the breast,” “primary narcissism,” “care-taking,” or “the phallus.” The mimetic hypothesis
might also include the rationally mimetic economic agent studied

36 - Models of Desire

by Orléan, an agent that has the most minimal tutelary beliefs
about the model: the agent only wagers that it may be pragmatically useful to follow a particular model’s example, without really
believing that this model has truly superior knowledge or being.?
Girard himself tended for a long time to adopt a “philosophical” approach to this issue insofar as he privileged an existentialist
way of talking about the imitator’s beliefs about the model. The
imitator was consistently cast as the subject of “ontological sickness,” which meant that the person prone to mimetic or “metaphysical” desire was thought to be driven by a hopeless quest for
“being.” Yet some of Girard’s other statements break with this tendency and emphasize instead the diversity of the contents of tutelary belief. For example, a passage that refers to the “metaphysical”
and “ontological” dimensions of desire goes on to explain this
usage as follows:
By invoking the notion of metaphysical desire, I am not in any way giving
in to metaphysics. To understand this notion, we have only to look at the
kinship between the mimetic structure we have discussed and the part
played by notions such as honour or prestige in certain types of rivalry
that are regulated by society: duels, sporting competitions, etc. These notions are in fact created by the rivalry; they have no tangible reality whatsoever. Yet the very fact that there is a rivalry involving them makes them
appear to be more real than any real object. (TH 296—97/321)

In this and many other contexts, Girard insists on the illusory nature of the imitator’s beliefs about the model, but he does not as-

sert that any one master term can sufficiently characterize the sum
total of these beliefs. The word “prestige” in its multiple senses
sometimes replaces the previous insistence on the belief in the
model’s superior “being” and “self-sufficiency.” Yet it should also
be observed that in a recent publication, Girard explicitly embraces the earlier terminology of “metaphysical” desire: “Words
like ‘being’ and ‘ontological seem pompously philosophical in the
context of flighty adolescents, yet they cannot be avoided. Being is
what mimetic desire is really after” (TE 43).
In my view, the basic approach that involves an emphasis on the

37 - Elements ofMimetic Desire
role of the imitator’s beliefs about the model is correct. However,

this approach must be applied carefully, which means avoiding a
tendency to limit the belief in question to a content that is in fact
only appropriate to one particular cultural context or to one aspect
of mimetic desire. A theory of mimetic desire is necessarily committed to making claims about the typical orientations of mimetic
agents, but if this theory is to have a broad descriptive scope, as
well as a genuine explanatory capacity, these claims must be very
carefully construed. The problem with the interpretations of the
“essence of homo mimeticus discussed in chapter 1 is that they fail in
this task by insisting on one historically specific type of motivational orientation (¢.g., certain social scientific conceptions of rationality, the romantic individualist’s concern with spontaneity,

the existentialist’s interest in Being, and a strangely pessimistic
Hegelianism). Clearly it will not do to furnish our agent with a
precise set of preferences about the possible objects of desire, as in
the utilitarian picture of the imitator. The specificity and interest
of the concept of mimetic desire reside precisely in not equipping
the human motivational system with a preestablished program
that specifies in detail a set of preferred states, built-in systems of

“rewards” and “incentives,” or an overarching mechanism that es-

tablishes incentives through “reinforcement.” A utility-driven imitation may very well have its own sphere of pertinence but should
be kept separate from the notion of mimetic desire, which is different insofar as it identifies an essentially comparative formation of
desire and not simply another orientation toward an item in a list
of possible goals that range from the satisfaction of basic appetites
to a sense of self-efficacy.
But what of the strategy that consists in equipping the mimetic

agent with some criteria for the “choice” of the model? A fully intentional choice is clearly ruled out: the agent does not form an
intention to pick a particular model. We do not want to propose
that the determination of the model is necessarily the result of a
focal inferential process, yet beliefs about the potential models
could still figure among the necessary conditions for the activation
of a process that results in the effective selection of a model, a selec-

tion that is implicit in the fact that the agent desires in one way and

38 - Models of Desire

not another. But here we encounter our question once more: what
would those beliefs be?

Tutelary Beliefs
In attempting to answer the question of the nature of tutelary belief, one becomes acutely aware of the problem of the psychological theory’s relation to the specificities of particular historical formations and personal histories. How is it possible to arrive at a
conception that is flexible enough to be able to correspond to the
diversity of specific beliefs that may effectively motivate one agent
to adopt another agent, or a concept of another agent, as a model?
In some cases, such words as “reverence,” “esteem,” and “admiration” seem appropriate; in others, “attractiveness,” “seduction,”

and “power” would be more likely to describe the operative beliefs; in others still, the actual beliefs would involve notions of

“purity” or “saintliness.” There are simply many specific kinds of
beliefs involved when people come to have a deep confidence in
some model’s “superiority,” or perhaps more generally, in the kind
of hierarchical difference that is often described by Western novelists and by Girard himself as idolatry or as “deviated transcendence.”
One aspect of the issue can be clarified by examining a distinction proposed by some attribution theorists in empirical social
psychology. I have in mind the distinction between “entity attribution” and cases where another person’s preferences are viewed
as merely subjective opinions. Following this jargon, if I think
your preference for a certain variety of whiskey is peculiar to your
own strange taste buds, then this is not a case of “entity attribution.” The latter term would be used if I thought, on the contrary,
that you had discerned some objective qualities of the drink. Does
it help to say, then, that mimetic desiring involves so-called entity
attribution? The idea would be that the mimetic model’s preferences are not viewed by the mimetic agent as merely “subjective”
or relative. Thus the attribution in question involves a belief about
the model’s ability to identify objective truths, such as which state
of affairs is truly worthy of preference. Thus we could conjecture

39 - Elements of Mimetic Desire

that in many cases, the tutelary belief would involve the mimetic
agent’s view that the model’s desire corresponds to an “intrinsically superior choice.” Or again, the abstract statement would be
that the model is the agent who is thought to know the “true
value” of things. Yet this assumption may not be appropriate in all
contexts: we can imagine imitators who are fairly cynical about the
truth of their model’s choices but not about other features of the
model’s superiority. For example, I might not think that your preference for this brand of whiskey is grounded in objective proper~ties of the drink, but I might deem your overall style to be quite
genuine and admirable, so I might want to drink the same sour
mash whiskey that you do. Insofar as this kind of example seems
plausible, the implication is that we do not want to include the
notion of intrinsic value as a necessary part of the belief about the
model. Also, to the extent that a particular model, serves over time
as a consistent mediator for an individual agent, the agent’s relation to this mediator could very well involve an attachment to the
perceived individuality and singularity of the mediator. The mediator’s judgments are valued as unique, creative, different, but not

necessarily as “true” or intrinsically right.
To restate the problem, we want to say that the imitator’s belief
about the model’s desire plays a crucial role in the motivation of the
mimetic agent’s own state of desire. We also want to say that this can
only be true when another condition is satisfied, namely, that the

imitator has a tutelary belief that helps to motivate the nondeliberate selection of the model, perhaps in a nonconscious inferential
process. As the nature of this tutelary belief is highly contextbound and involves reference to cultural and personal histories,

it is difficult to give a precise and abstractive characterization of
its essential contents. Yet if nothing is said to characterize those
contents, the notion is vacuous and the explanation runs in a circle: What motivates the selection of the model? Tutelary beliefs.
Which beliefs are tutelary? Those that motivate the selection of
the model.
One negative stipulation of the contents of tutelary belief runs
as follows. Roughly, it is a matter of adding in a “not-imitatingmy-desire” clause: if anagent A believes that agent B’s desire for S

40 + Models of Desire
is a mimetic desire for which A is the model, then B cannot be A’s

model for a desire for S. Note that this clause does not say that A
could not end up desiring S by other means. Nor does it say that
A must believe that B’s desire is fully “spontaneous” or “autonomous” (e.g., agent A believes that B embodies a case of realized
narcissism). In some cultures, that a master himself had a master 1s
no disqualification in the disciple’s eyes; what the disciple will not

accept is a master who takes him as master. It should be noted that
the clause does not rule out reciprocal modeling or double mediation, that is, cases where A is the model for a B who also happens to

be A’s model. The clause in question does imply that the latter phenomena will involve significant constraints on the agents’ awareness of their real situation, but this is what we want in the model. I

return to these patterns of mimetic interaction in chapter 3.

Yet as I have already said, the “not-imitating-my-desire” clause
is only a negative condition and does not adequately characterize
tutelary beliefs. Let us explore some possible schematic formula-

tions of the latter. Tutelary beliefs could typically take the following form, where M stands for the model:
(1) M knows which x is the best (or a superior) kind of X.

This is the version just evoked in the discussion of psychologists’
“entity attributions,” and I suggested that it was overly restrictive.
One can imitate a model’s desires without believing that this person really has the kind of knowledge that (1) attributes to that person. Perhaps the following version would be less restrictive:
(2) M knows which x will (typically) yield y.

Here the agent’s tutelary belief attributes to the model what is essentially knowledge of aproduction rule: to get y, do x. This sort
of basis for imitation has been strongly emphasized by a number of
theorists, and it is a central intuition of the “social cognitive the-

ory” proposed by Albert Bandura.* The main idea is that of a “utilitarian imitator,” for whom

imitation is a means

to an end.

Equipped with a set of preferences and incentives, this agent ob-

41 - Elements of Mimetic Desire

serves the consequences of others’ actions and then copies those
actions that result in outcomes that he or she deems rewarding.
This is often a highly successful strategy because watching others
and then engaging in selective imitation allows the agent to learn
without engaging in a costly process of trial and error. Utilitarian
imitators already know what they want, and what they seek in
observing others is know-how linked to particular rewards and to
a general goal of self-efficacy. It seems clear to me that Girard’s
analyses of mimetic desire are not based on these assumptions.
-Whereas Bandura equips his agent with a motivational system that
then serves to prompt imitation along with other forms of behavior, Girard explores a way in which imitation influences motivation. Bandura’s imitator has certain desires but is uncertain how to
satisfy them; Girard’s mimetic agent is uncertain what to desire,
but this uncertainty 1s conjoined with a tendency to believe in the
certainty and self-directness possessed by some other agent. Thus
in Girard’s hypothesis, the social rewards serving as the implicit
and explicit goals of a mimetic relation to a model are not secondary to some other set of rewards or incentives already sought by
the agent. The orientation toward the other is secondary in Bandura’s theory, and it follows that an agent who already possesses
sufficient know-how and self-efficacy would no longer imitate a
model. In Girard’s view, the orientation toward the other is pri-

mary, and even the goal of self-efficacy is ultimately understood as
a particular stance taken in relation to others.
Formula 2, then, corresponds to Bandura’s approach, because
the tutelary belief focuses the agent’s interest on the particular
task, not on the model’s personal qualities. To transform this particular case into a very general tutelary belief about the “prestige”
of a particular model, one might alter (2) to yield the following
variant:
(2’) Mis the person who knows, and can successfully use, an effective means to ends of type Y.

So when the imitator wants to get a particular something, he or
she thinks of M and does what he or she thinks M would do in that

42, - Models of Desire

situation, because M is perceived as being generally effective in
this domain of activity (and perhaps others). This sort of formula
may express a genuine insight into the kind of attitude that helps
people to single out someone to imitate on some occasions, but it
seems to me that that insight is not a particularly Girardian one. To
see why, one need only reflect that the Girardian imitator does not
already know what specific ends he or she wants to achieve and
hence does not merely survey the world of others in search for
someone who displays the requisite skills. What the Girardian imitator wants is not only a way to achieve certain ends but a model
of what is worth doing and pursuing. In Weberian jargon, the
agent of mimetic desire is engaged in Wertrationalitdt and a form
of charismatic authority, and not just Zweckrationalitat. To account for the difference, one might propose the following typical
schema for a tutelary belief:
(3) M is someone who knows what ends are worth pursuing.

Yet (3) brings us back in the direction of a tautologous formulation of the contents of tutelary beliefs: tutelary beliefs are beliefs
that inform the believer that someone’s desires are worth copying.

But such beliefs seem rather uninformative if their content is
nothing more than (3). Yet the basic idea does have a Girardian
flavor, and that is why many of the attempts to specify the orientations of homo mimeticus insist on the model’s general prestige, not
on his or her particular doings and competencies. What matters is
not what the model is thought to do or to know but what the
model “is,” which when said rather bluntly renders the “metaphysical” understanding of the source of mimetic desire:
(4) M has the Being I lack.

I have already objected that this approach wrongly transforms all
those who are under the sway of mimetic desire into existential
philosophers. Although such a formula describes a relation between two agents, it lacks a dimension that is crucial, in my view,

43 - Elements ofMimetic Desire

to the mimetic intuition. What is needed is better captured by the
following formula:
(5) Mis the kind of person I would like to be.

Tutelary beliefs, then, presuppose and are filled in by beliefs in the
existence of kinds of persons that are at least implicitly and partially ranked in the mind of the mimetic agent. Some of these kinds
will correspond to culturally designated “roles” or types (e.g., the
chivalrous knight, the dandy, etc.); others will be more idiosyncratic composites of such types. The agent adopts the model’s desires in order to become the kind of person the model is perceived
to be, the assumption being that the individual in question is a
sufficiently good, if not the best or the only, example of the type.
But is there not a regression here? How does one get one’s general

idea of which kind of person one wants to emulate, and how is it
determined that a particular individual has the necessary traits?
The negative clause proposed above (“not-imitating-my-desire”)
is a partial response to that question: I do not want to be the kind
of person who would imitate me. Conversely, I might want to be
the kind of person who is indifferent to me. Other ways of supporting a belief of type (5) would point to the various kinds of
evidence that one could have, in particular sociocultural contexts,

of an individual’s embodiment of the virtue or distinction required
by the kind in question. In other words, we attach to (5) another
explanatory clause:
(5') Mis the kind of person I would like to be, because M is q, has
r, knows s, and has t, where q, r, s, and t are typical characteris-

tics of a superior social kind.

The variables would be filled in with whatever people take to be
the tangible signs of the model’s superiority: high birth, wealth,
beauty, manifestations of passion or fervor, evidence of individual
well-being, unique talents or exploits, enjoyment of admiration or
popularity, and so on. The particular tutelary belief that fills in (5)

44. - Models of Desire

would be supported, then, by a chain or cluster of other beliefs and
perceptions that, in the context, appear to justify and confirm it. A

crucial point implicit in (5) and (5’) is the idea that the desiring

agent does not already believe himself or herself to equal the model
in the embodiment of the virtue or distinction in question: the
model is the kind of person I would like to be, implying that I am not
now such a person, my proof being that I lack some or all of the
attributes listed. The tutelary belief involves the agent’s awareness
that the model possesses—at least for the moment—a hierarchical
difference in relation to the agent. One might also add that typically the agent also believes that the model has this same superiority in relation to some significant set of others, if not in relation
to everyone.
Perhaps reference to a literary illustration will make these abstractions appear somewhat less lifeless. Julien Sorel, I noted, does

not imitate the desires of the young “puppets” at the ball. Although these young men all objectively enjoy a superior social status in relation to M. de La Mole’s secretary, the fact that Julien

does not presently believe this social difference has any “substance” means that it does not suffice to make these men his models. They may outrank him in some public scale of status, but they
are just puppets and he has no desire to become like them. In order
to see what is required to change the situation, it would be informative to turn to a case where Julien’s beliefs about another person

do qualify that person as a model for him. Examples are easy to
find. For example, one paragraph explicitly tells us why Julien is
fascinated by the chevalier de Beauvoisis, so much so that he “almost courts him.” The key phrases run as follows: “The combination of self-respect, mysterious importance, and youthful fatuousness enchanted him. For example the chevalier stammered a bit
because he had had the honor of spending a lot of time with a very
noble man who had this defect. Julien had never found united in a

single being an amusing ridiculousness and a perfection of those
manners that a poor provincial fellow must try to imitate.”5
Julien lacks the status enjoyed by de Beauvoisis and knows it.
He knows that in order to justify being associated with Julien, de
Beauvoisis has circulated the rumor that Julien is actually the fils

45 - Elements ofMimetic Desire

naturel of an intimate friend of the great M. de la Mole—and not
merely the son of a provincial carpenter. In Julien’s mind, evidence
of the chevalier’s external social distinction combines with a perception of his unique personal qualities, a mixture of perfect aristocratic manners, self-esteem, and /e ridicule. The latter may seem

incongruous as a quality capable of motivating Julien’s admiration
but should be understood as contrasting in Julien’s mind with his
acute sense of his own social vulnerability: while Julien knows that
he must himself constantly anticipate the thoughts of others and
avoid appearing ridiculous, he imagines that the chevalier’s distinction and self-esteem are so secure that he can permit himself to
be ridiculous, even fatuous. What is more, the chevalier’s self-love

is such that he can display, and not hide, a fatuous imitation of the
defect of a great man. In this case, there is no clause to the effect

that the model must be perceived as being strictly nonimitative;
only if the chevalier were to imitate Julien, or other inferiors,

would his mimicry be perceived as a flaw potentially serious
enough to disqualify him as a model. Finally, the chevalier’s manifest attitude toward Julien combines interesting features: he displays his sense of his own social superiority by letting Julien know
that he will only associate with him after having circulated a lie
about Julien’s birth, but at the same time, this very lie holds out the

possibility of Julien’s social advancement, an advancement that
Julien passionately desires. Clearly, the particular episode of mimetic desire in which Julien imitates the chevalier is part of the

larger history of Julien Sorel’s ambition. By pointing to the role
that the tutelary belief plays in identifying the chevalier as a model
for him, I do not presume to have explained Julien’s motives ab ovo.
For this episode of mimetic desire to be possible, Julien has to be
equipped with any number of preferences, values, longings, and

ideas. We know, for example, that none of the priestly models that
he has had ample occasion to observe was ever shrouded in the
kind of tutelary beliefs that he is capable of holding in regard to
other kinds of individuals— beginning with his youthful infatuation with the image of Napoleon. That Napoleon should emerge
as the salient model for an ambitious young man in Julien’s context
and social situation is hardly mysterious, but why is he ambitious?

46 - Models of Desire

Why does he dream and read instead of working diligently at the
factory? There will be questions “upstream” of any mimetic episode, some of which may be difficult to answer. But this should not
be taken to imply that the notions introduced above can have no
genuine explanatory value when applied to a particular situation
“downstream.”© We are not after a covering law for agents’ individual global trajectories, and it is hardly surprising that we find
no context-free formula that explains why an individual has come
to hold one set of tutelary beliefs and not another. (I return to this
topic in chapter 5.)
Does (5’) constitute an adequate, albeit blank, description of
the most general and typical form of a tutelary belief? The key assumption would seem to be that people believe there are different
kinds of people; moreover, people believe there are hierarchical
differences between these kinds. Furthermore, it is assumed that

people sometimes do not consider themselves to belong to the
kind they “would like to” and that they find themselves taking a
special interest in those who do. Mimetic desire would then seem
to be this fixation on a model whose desires are thought to manifest a hierarchical difference between self and other. The specific
nature of this difference, however, and the qualities and actions
that embody it vary massively from context to context. One can
easily imagine a situation in which imitating the stammer of an
older man would disqualify someone from being worthy of imitation. Consequently, in any particular explanation of a case of mimetic desire, the blank formulation of the tutelary belief would
have to be filled in with a more detailed and fine-grained description of the contents of the beliefs and desires. Yet in the process,

additional complexities not yet hinted at will be encountered.

“Cet obscur objet du désir”

Above I suggested in passing that the semantic content of a desire
(its “object”) can be characterized as a situation, that is, as the state
of affairs that, if it were to become actual, would satisfy the desire.”

Thus at a particular point in the story, Julien Sorel desires to have

47 - Elements of Mimetic Desire

Madame Rénal allow him to hold her hand as they sit and converse

with Madame Derville in the twilight. The content or object of his
desire is the situation where this is indeed what takes place. In this
example, the situation desired has various elements. Note that if
this situation is to become a fact, both Julien and Madame Rénal

must perform certain types of actions: Julien has to muster the
courage to undertake the bold and transgressive action of reaching
for her hand, and she must yield to this advance, and in a manner

that gives the physical contact a certain kind of significance. Ac‘cording to this manner of construing the “object of desire,” the
content of a desire 1s not “objectival” in the sense of involving primarily or in the first instance a physical or concrete object—the
“object” of Julien’s desire is not simply that the woman’s hand remain in his grasp, and one can easily imagine a Sartrean example in
which the hand would be momentarily surrendered but Julien
would nonetheless determine that the type of situation he desired
had not been realized. As the text indicates, Julien reckons that the

holding of hands must count as an advantage that he has acquired
for himself, which means that certain attitudes and dispositions on
the part of Madame Rénal figure as essential parts of the object of
Julien’s desire.8

The content of a desire, then, may be described as a situation,
and moreover, as the situation that would realize the desire should

this situation become actual. In describing the situation that specifies the content of a particular desire, we must take into account

the terms and viewpoint of the agent who has the desire. As
Dretske has usefully emphasized, attitudes of desire share something of the opacity that is typically associated with attitudes of
belief. Thus, although we know that Jocasta is the mother of Oedipus, the fact that Oedipus desires Jocasta does not warrant us to

draw the conclusion that Oedipus desires his mother. As soon as he
learns that Jocasta is his mother, Oedipus no longer desires her in

the same way, if he desires her at all.? In describing the objects of
desire, it is crucial to allow that an agent can desire, not one particular state of affairs, but a kind of state of affairs. In other words, in

speaking of types of situations as well as of particular situations, we
conceive of the desire’s content as an abstract schema or model that

48 - Models of Desire

may contain one or more variables. Thus it is not always a matter
of a desire of the sort “I desire to own this particular automobile
here and now.” There are also cases such as “I desire to own an
automobile someday,” or “I desire to own an automobile now,”
or “I desire that someone somewhere own an automobile.” As
Dretske puts it, “Our capacity to distinguish one object from another sets an upper limit on the kinds of objects that can be objects
of desire.”!° Similarly, if we wish to respect the opacity of desiring
attitudes, our attribution of the contents of desire must corre-

spond to the type of situation intended by the person who has the
desire.
For example, suppose someone is oblivious to the difference be-

tween Bordeaux and Burgundy wines: this person not only cannot
taste the difference between them but has never even heard of the
distinction and deems the different shapes of bottles arbitrary or
meaningless. Yet this person does think there is a difference between French wines in general and wines from other countries and
on a particular occasion in a restaurant wants to be served some
French wine. In such a case, we are right to ascribe to that person a

desire for French wine but not for a specific variety of French wine,
even though the wine that he or she consumes may turn out to be a
specific vintage, say, a Chateau Mille Secousses 1980. If I attributed to this person a desire for that particular bottle, I would get

the content of his or her desire wrong, just as I would be mistaken

if I said that the person desired any kind of wine whatsoever. With
this kind of example in view, Dretske suggests that a desire can be
satisfied by something that was not the object of desire.!! Thus,

although our uninformed restaurant goer was satisfied by the
Mille Secousses that the waiter served, it seems wrong to say that
this was the content ofhis desire. It could seem to follow from this
observation that it is a mistake to say that the content of a desire is
the situation that, if it became actual, would satisfy the desire. It

might be preferable, then, to say that the object of a desire is whatever would “realize” the desire, where “realize” refers to the be-

coming factual of the desire’s semantic content, the proviso being
that we define this situation in terms that attribute the degree of
particularity or discernment appropriate to the person and context

49 - Elements of Mimetic Desire

involved in this particular episode of desire. This point may seem
somewhat arcane to some readers, but I will now show that these

matters make a very real difference when we begin to analyze what
it means to say that one person conceives of a desire “according to”
another person’s desire.
What indeed is meant by the claim that in cases of “triangular
desire,” two people desire “the same thing”? A closer look reveals
some important ambiguities here. The problem can be clarified by
means of yet another schematic example. Let us suppose that an
‘agent, A, is disposed to desire mimetically in relation to another
agent, B, who is A’s model, which means that A has the right kind

of tutelary beliefs about B, namely, some belief that fills in (5')
above. Let us now assume that A and B are both members of a
committee. B is the present chairperson, but B’s term is up and
there must be a new appointment. According to the rules governing the organization in question, B is eligible for reappointment.
Now, A knows that B would like very much to be chairperson for
another term, and it is this knowledge that is going to play the role
of the mimetic agent’s attribution of desire in this particular instance of mimetic desire. In other words, A’s knowledge that B

wants to be the leader again is the belief that will have an effective
role in the formation of A’s desire. What, then, is the content of A’s

mimetically conceived desire? What situation would satisfy A’s desire? Does A desire the situation (a) where B is renamed chairperson of the committee, thereby satisfying B’s desire, or (0) where A
becomes chairperson of the committee, thereby thwarting B’s desire? Obviously if A desires (a) there could be cooperation between A and B, whereas if A desires (0), there is a potential for
conflict, if we assume that the rules stipulate that there cannot be a
sharing of the chairperson’s role.
Here we have a crucial issue for the theory: do we want to say
that both (a) and (4) correspond to examples of mimetic desire or
that only one of them does? It seems to me that we have to say both
if we are not going to restrict the scope of the theory severely.
Clearly, within actual cultural contexts (such as social systems having very stable hierarchical value schemes and institutions) there
are many examples of subordinates whose desires are, through a

50 - Models of Desire
process of modeling or social learning, formed in ways analogous
to (a). There is no compelling reason why a theory of mimetic
desire should rule them out as a matter of principle. Nor does there
seem to be any evidence for saying that even in such cases, a fundamental ambivalence toward the model is present—or that an emulative desire to supplant the model is lurking in the wings, being

somehow repressed more or less effectively. In that direction lies
psychodynamic theorizing, with its circular positing of unconscious aggressive drives, killer instincts, or a universal “will to
power.” But if these arguments are accepted, the often-discussed

tendency of mimetic desire to generate rivalry all of the time is put
in doubt; under a definition that embraces cases of both types (a)
and (4), mimetic desire does not necessarily or automatically generate rivalry or even a potential for it. It is not synonymous with
“emulation” or “the desire to surpass the model.”
What would seem to generate rivalry is a type of desire in which
the agent wants to appropriate for his or her own exclusive possession what the model is thought (or known) to desire in a similar
way. In my view, we want to say that this type of conflictual desire
is a subset of mimetic desire. Girard tends to stress the link between mimetic desire and rivalry, but he often refers to cases of
“external mediation,” where desire is at once mimetic and non-

competitive. The competitive subset of mimetic desire could be
specified in terms of a class of desires defined in terms of their content, that is, a set of types of situations that involve relations that

exclude any possible cooperative sharing or harmonious coincidence of goals between agent and model. In other words, if both A
and B desire an S that belongs to this class, then it is ruled out that
both A’s and B’s desires could be realized at the same time. Desires
belonging to this class are not externally imposed on the agent but
are generated by the specific mode in which the agent imitates the
model’s desire, a mode that can be labeled “emulative.” Moreover,

a subset of tutelary beliefs will correspond to this class of mimetic
desires. This possibility is allowed for in (5), “M is the kind of
person I would like to be,” for the fact that someone “would like”

something does not mean that he or she believes that the thing is
possible, or even that this particular want enjoys any motivational

51 - Elements ofMimetic Desire
priority. On the other hand, “would like to be” can read as “would
like and in fact hope to become.” The person in the grips of the
emulative variety of mimetic desire would like very much to become the kind of person exemplified by the model, and the ensuing
episodes of imitation typically are part of a project aimed at precisely that goal.
There is a moral here about the supposed “identity” of two
agents’ desires in a triangular situation.!2 An attitude of desire is
not in the third person; it is someone’s attitude toward a situation.
‘For that reason, it is not entirely accurate to say of B’s desire in our
example that its content 1s the situation where “B is renamed chairperson.” Instead, the situation in question involves a relation,
from B’s perspective, between B and a state of affairs. More accurately, the situation desired should be described as “I am renamed

chairperson,” with B speaking. The same insight must be applied
to our two different versions of A’s mimetic desire “according to”
B. The version in which what A wants is to supplant B as leader
clearly foregrounds A’s first-person relation to the object of desire,
which 1s described as “I become chairperson, supplanting B,” with
A speaking. In the version in which what A wants 1s to help B retain the post, the object of desire might best be described as “B
remains chairperson with my help.” Such remarks hardly seem illuminating, but they do serve to clarify what is meant by the “identity” of the objects of two agents’ desires. Being renamed chairper-

son and becoming chairperson for the first time are not identical
situations. Similarly, if B wants to see himself in the seat of power

for another year, and A wants to sit at the feet of an idolized leader,
is it really appropriate to speak of a strict “equivalence” of their
objects of desire? Obviously not. At most, one can speak of a similarity between the contents of A’s and B’s desires. What we can say
is that whether A’s mimicry of B’s desire is emulative or not, A still
desires a situation that is schematically similar to the situation desired by B. The two agents “anchor” this schema quite differently,
just as A can mimetically desire according to B’s desire in radically
divergent ways. But this schematic similarity does not entail an
equivalence of desires.
The issue becomes even more complex when we leave behind

52 - Models of Desire

the kind of relatively simple case that has just been evoked. For
example, in some cases we must consider the content of a mimetic
desire based on a desire that is embedded within another attitude
of desire, as in the following structure: Agent A mimetically desires some situation S “according to” agent B, where A believes
that what B desires is an S’, the content of which is that agent C
desire some S". Is the content of A’s desire S “equivalent to,” or
only schematically patterned after, the contents of S’ or S$"? Examples can be conceived of where the appropriate response varies. For
example, B is in love with C and desires that C have some desire in
which B figures: B wants C to want B to invite her out. Taking B as
model, A in turn conceives of a desire for C, but the content of this
mimetic desire, S, could in some cases be rather different from that

of B’s desire S': A wants C to want A—and not B—to invite her
out. But what of the kind of “voyeuristic” mimetic desire of a Pandarus (which Girard has sometimes discussed)? Here A would mimetically desire that B’s desire for S’ be satisfied, which would

mean that A would indeed want C to desire what B wanted her to
desire, so that the contents of the embedded desires in S and in S’

would be equivalent. Yet surely A’s desire for S and B’s desire for S'
are not of the same type! We can also imagine cases where the contents of both the embedded desire and the attribution of desire
would be of the same type. For example, B desires S’, the content
of which is that C want to wear a certain dress, and A mimetically
desires S, the content of which is that she want to wear that dress.

Additional complexities would be introduced in cases where the
content of C’s desire in S"’ includes essential reference to the desires of B or A.18
There is a more general lesson here about the puzzling question

of the contents of attitudes of desire. The necessity of emphasizing
the desiring agent’s beliefs about the model’s desires should not
be misunderstood as a way of detaching the processes of mimetic
desire from all forms of concrete interaction, thereby making the
concept part of a purely individualistic psychology. When the
agent’s concept of a model is actually of a real agent, the relation
between the agent’s attitudes and those of this agent must be taken
into account 1n providing an adequate description of the mimetic

53 - Elements ofMimetic Desire
episode. Although it would simplify matters greatly if we could do
SO, it is not a good idea to equate the mimetic agent’s beliefs and
desires with external states of affairs; nor is it wise to divorce them

from external situations entirely. Instead, we must describe the
very complex relations that may obtain between the two, relations

that sometimes recall those of the metamorphic thieves in Dante’s
Inferno: “due e nessun Pimagine perversa parea [both and neither
the perverse image seemed]” (25.77—78). Strictly speaking, the
contents of the model’s and imitator’s desires are not equivalent
“because even when the mimetic agent actually knows what the
model really desires, the agent’s mimetic desire transforms and
adapts its contents to that agent’s own situation. Girard relies on
this nonequivalence of the situations desired whenever he discusses emulative desire: X’s desire to steal Y’s seat is never “equivalent” to Y’s desire to keep his seat. I have argued that the transformation need not have a conflictual or rivalrous orientation: X’s
desire to help Y keep his seat is also schematically similar, but not
equivalent, to Y’s desire to keep his seat.!4
To drive this point home, let us examine Girard’s discussion of
what he presents as a fairly common mimetic configuration. Two
young people do not manage to declare their mutual love because
both sense that it is dangerous to be the first to do so. Why? Girard
writes:
The desire that speaks first puts itself on display and, as a result, can become a mimetic model for the desire that has not yet spoken. The displayed desire runs the risk of being copied rather than reciprocated. In
order to desire someone who desires us, we must not imitate the offered

desire, we must reciprocate it, which is vastly different. . . . If Benedick
spoke first and Beatrice took his desire as a model, she might reorient her
desire toward herself in imitation of Benedick’s desire; she would prefer

herself to him. (TE 80-81)

Here the feared “copying” of desire to which Girard refers does
not involve anything like a simple equivalence of the two situations desired. When someone hears another person say “I love
you” and translates this into “I love myself,” the “copy” is unfaith-

54 + Models of Desire

ful. Girard seems to be reasoning that the anticipated danger resides in a “copying” that in fact transforms the content of Benedick’s utterance, extracting from his “I love you” something like “T
believe you desirable,” which, when given an egotistical twist by
Beatrice, becomes a source of self-love. In another plausible case,

such an utterance might be interpreted as evidence of the speaker’s
lack of good judgment and hence as a reason for not reciprocating.
But in neither of these two cases would it be a matter of a faithful
copying or imitation of the initial desire—unless, of course, that
initial desire is not a desire for reciprocal affection but something
predatory, such as the desire to make an erotic conquest. The dan-

ger that Beatrice may “prefer herself” stands prior, then, to any
danger that she may tendentiously “copy” or exploit Benedick’s declaration. Imitation may exacerbate romantic rivalry, but it does
not generate it ex ni/ilo or convert two desires for mutual love into
two desires for conquest. The young lovers’ hesitation 1s more simply explained by pointing out that both of them are uncertain
about the other’s response and are afraid that a declaration will not
be reciprocated, which could happen for any number of reasons
having nothing to do with the fact or timing of the utterance.
Also, it is important to keep in view the possibility of a happier
scenario. If in saying “I love you,” what Benedick expresses is his
desire for a situation in which he and Beatrice enjoy their mutual
love, then the copying of that desire by Beatrice is hardly to be
feared by him. She might very well hear his declaration and then
express her own desire for a romantic union. If he has good reason
to think her inclinations lie in that direction, he need not withhold

his declaration. He might even reckon that when suitably made,
his declaration could help to inspire in her a desire for romantic
reciprocity. These sorts of events may be rare in the great literary
texts, but they can be observed from time to time in the world.

Internal and External Mediation

In the preceding discussion, I tried to avoid translating Girard’s
basic intuition into termis appropriate to only one type of back-

55 - Elements ofMimetic Desire

ground of sociocultural beliefs and practices, or again, to one kind
of personality formation or deep-seated existential attitude. Even
the apparently schematic “means-end” rationality framework of
utilitarian psychology is an overly limited approach to the diversity of the phenomena with which mimetic desire may be associated. What Is precisely interesting and promising about the concept of mimetic desire is the plasticity or flexibility it allows one to
attain in the description of a motivational system that remains
_Open to contextual determinations. At the same time, however,

the theory of mimetic desire is meant to identify invariant patterns
and processes in a manner that a purely contextualist or culturalist
approach cannot. The model seeks, then, to combine an emphasis

on positing dynamic invariants and constraints with an emphasis
on the theory’s openness to contextual determinants.
Yet the reader may be skeptical about a concept that is so essentially protean. On the one hand, mimetic desire is said to involve

an agent’s emulative relation to another person, a relation that is
inherently conflictual: the other’s desire is at once a model and an
obstacle, with rivalry being a likely result. On the other hand, another type of mimetic desire is said to involve an agent’s hierarchical relation to another person, a relation that is essentially one of
subordination: the imitator perceives the model as a master or a
ruler and is said to desire quite spontaneously the other’s satisfaction. Is this not a matter of seeking to explain two rather different
things in terms of the “same” kind of desire? What does the theory
of mimetic desire have to say about the difference? When queried
along these lines, Girard once responded that it was the “culture”
that was the determinant condition of such differences.!5 This
seems to be the right general approach to take. In the terms that I
have proposed above, the difference between imitative and emula-

tive desires would be linked to the two different kinds of tutelary
beliefs that the desiring agent may have about the model. One belief posits a model who is superior, and whose superiority is in one
way or another placed out of reach. To desire following this figure’s desire is not to seek to equal or surpass but to subordinate
one’s wishes and endeavors to those of the model. There are many
examples in many cultural spheres, especially in those where re-

56 - Models of Desire
ligious beliefs serve to ground social differentiation in a transcendent, extrasocial source. Girard elaborates on this theme, for exam-

ple, in his discussion of Shakespeare’s conception of “Degree” (TE
165—66). The other constellation of tutelary beliefs about the
model also involves a notion of the model’s superiority or hierarchical difference, but this advantage is not perceived as being
permanent. It is instead taken as a strategic difference to be overcome if possible. The other’s perceived difference is a spur to actions that have the goal of diminishing this difference, or more
frequently, of maintaining the relation of inequality while reversing the roles. This is the world of envy. A superb illustration is
provided by Girard’s analysis of the conspirators’ mimetic relations
to Caesar in Shakespeare’s play (TE 185-99).
Thus it is possible to flesh out the tutelary beliefs so as to render
the motivational scenarios more credible; in reference to more specific contexts, such descriptions can involve a great deal of nuance
and detail. It is in such terms that Girard specifies his concept of
the “distance” between the mediator and the subject, and with it,

the distinction between “internal” and “external” types of mediation. Girard writes that this distance cannot be defined in terms of
physical space: “the distance between the mediator and the subject
is first of all a spiritual distance.” He goes on to imply that the
distance may be “social” and “intellectual.” Yet this is too vague:
given that such a belief would be expressed very differently from
one sociohistorical context to the next, it is impossible to provide a
specific and detailed description of the “distance” between model
and desiring agent in these terms. The same is true of the related
subdistinction between “endogamic” and “exogamic” forms of
“internal” mediation. Yet the theory does provide definitions of
the agent’s relation to two different types of models, and we can
link these two relations functionally to different kinds of beliefs
about the model. Thus “external mediation” involves a desiring
agent's relation to what may be called a “hierarchical model.” “Internal mediation,” on the other hand, involves an agent’s relation
to an “obstacle model” in an emulative form of desire. In this manner we express the distinction between internal and external mediation precisely in the terms called for by another of Girard’s

57 - Elements ofMimetic Desire

clauses, which is surely the most essential, for he states that in the
case of external mediation, “no rivalry with the mediator is possible” (DDN 9/23). Elsewhere in the same text, Girard similarly

equates a decrease in the “distance” between model and imitator
with an increase in “equality” that exacerbates rivalry: “The increasing equality—the approach of the mediator in our terms—
does not give rise to harmony but to an even keener rivalry” (DDN
136—37/160).

Elements of Mimetic Desire

I am now in a position to recapitulate this analysis in the form of
some basic definitions of terms necessary to the description of a
minimal episode of mimetic desire, the hope being that these definitions could facilitate the analysis of more complex cases in subsequent discussions. Readers who dislike this sort of analytic exercise
are cordially invited to skip this section. The schematic notation
will not be used in the rest of this work.
First we designate three types of situations. The most abstract is
the basic episode of mimetic desiring, defined as follows: There
must be an agent, A, whose state of desire has as its content the
realization of a state of affairs or course of events, S. At some point

prior to this attitude of desire, and perhaps concurrent with it, the
desiring agent A had a belief to the effect that a model, M, desired
a similar situation S. This latter belief 1s the mimetic agent’s attribution of desire. At the time of desiring S (and perhaps for some
time prior to this moment), agent A has another belief that qualifies
the agent M as a model whose desires (or at least some of them) are
in some sense worthy of imitation. This 1s the mimetic agent’s “tutelary belief.” The essential content of the latter is specified as follows: “M is the kind of person I would like to be because M is
eras
, and does
.
Some or all of these Nene are filled in os attributes that in A’s
mind are evidence of M’s hierarchically superior difference in relation to A. Furthermore, A’s tutelary belief about M generates A’s
desire for S, possibly in a nonconscious manner (meaning that A

58 - Models of Desire

need not be aware of the role these conscious beliefs play in the
formation of A’s desires). Here is an informal definition incor-

porating these clauses:
Given agents, A, B,C, . . . , in a spatiotemporal location; given

abstract types of situations, S, S’, S”,. . ., which are the possible
objects of an agent’s intentional attitudes, such as belief and desire;

given a type of belief, M, instances of which are concepts of “models,” M, M’,M”,. . .; given, finally, a type of belief, T, instances of
which are T, T’, T”, ... , where these are tutelary beliefs that

qualify an agent as a model, a basic episode of mimetic desire requires the following:
. Att), A believes that M desires S.

4. A’s beliefs under (1) and (2) jointly generate A’s desire
under (3).

This minimal “episode” of mimetic desire is itself an abstract type
of situation that can be concretized in various ways. Two subvarieties of this situation can be specified, namely, “emulative

desiring” and “imitative desiring.” Thus we have the following
terms:
ABSTRACT TYPES OF SITUATIONS, S, S', S”,. . . , serving to clas-

sify other abstract types of situations, or PARTICULAR SITUATIONS 1626°85) 7
