59 - Elements ofMimetic Desire
Given this setup, we can specify some basic elements of any episode of mimetic desire as follows:

A: the agent who experiences the state of mimetic desire
Iw: the state of mimetic desire

S': the abstract type of situation that is the content of A’s desire
S: the abstract type of situation that is the content of M’s desire in
A’s attribution of desire to M
M: A’s model concept, the agent to whom A attributes a desire
for S
B: (when applicable) the real agent to whom A’s model concept
refers
T: A’s tutelary belief that qualifies M as A’s model
“Emulative desire” is defined as a case ofbasic mimetic desire in
which the desiring agent’s relations to the model and to the object
of desire are of the sort discussed above as “internal mediation.”
An example was A’s desire to supplant B as chairperson. Basically,
this variety of mimetic desire requires that A’s desire for S’ and M’s
putative desire for S could not in principle both be realized because S and S’ are mutually exclusive situations. Moreover, although A has a tutelary belief serving to qualify B as model, this
belief does not in A’s view entail the impossibility of the success of
A’s emulative desire: whatever the form of “hierarchical superiority” attributed by A to M in this belief may be, it is not a matter
of A’s belief in an “abyss” separating A and M. More precisely, A
believes that the emulative desire in relation to B has some possibility of being satisfied.

Chapter Three

Patterns of
Mimetic Interaction

Expanding the Framework
The focus of my discussion of mimetic desire so far has been extremely narrow. Although my proposed definition of a minimal
episode of mimetic desire may help to lend a certain amount of
rigor to the discussion of this concept, it hardly corresponds to the
broad range of Girard’s analyses, which embrace personality structures as well as dynamic patterns of interaction. And that sort of
breadth is clearly a necessary feature of any adequate theoretical
model of mimetic phenomena.!
In what ways, then, does the theory of mimetic desire need to
be developed? Girard’s psychological insights typically have to do
with the ways in which people’s lives are shaped by significant and
longstanding relations to a single model. We need to distinguish,
then, between highly punctual and transient imitative relations
and a different and more significant sort of mimetic relation to a
model or a “mediator.” Moreover, an emphasis on the latter will
lead to the analysis of typical interactional patterns that may be
associated with mimetic desire. Girard’s most extensive and detailed discussions of mimetic desire indeed focus upon complex
sequences of interaction involving a number of agents.

Girard’s emphasis on patterns of interaction can be understood
by situating his assumptions in relation to the entrenched opposition in psychology between the proponents of “trait” and “situa60

61 - Patterns ofMimetic Interaction

tion” theories.” Trait theorists typically assume that the consistency of an individual’s behavior can be analyzed in terms of the
traits or invariant features of that person’s personality or “character,” these features being exemplified both in publicly observable
behavior and in self-descriptions. A major polemic began, however, when the trait theorists’ belief in the relative consistency of
personal traits was challenged.’ The consistency of the person was
said to be an illusion having its source in the observer, whose dogmatism, sampling biases, instrumental attitude, and so on, were

held to be the ultimate bases of the invariant traits wrongly attributed to individuals. Situationalists contend that behavioral consistency is manifested across situations, not persons, for consistent
behavior 1s elicited by situations instead of being generated by invariant internal features of the individual personality. This kind of
criticism of trait theory is not based on some kind of belief in the
irreducibly particular and irregular nature of behavior, that is, a
total rejection of nomothetic psychology in favor of idiography;
rather, situationalists believe along with trait theorists that behavior exhibits lawful variation, yet they expect to find a different sort
of regularity, namely, one linked to a variation of the situation.

Various proponents of an “interactional” approach have rejected the opposition between “trait” and “situation” theories as a
false choice, their claim being that psychological regularities are
the product of the interaction of a broad range of variables, including both situation and personality traits. The basic insight here
was already expressed in Kurt Lewin’s formula, B = f(P, E), which

specifies that behavior is a function of the person and the environment, but this formula is probably best described as a broad
heuristic and not as a theory.* The interactional thesis may be understood, then, as the view that person and situation are interdependent; but in another version, it would be a matter of reference

to a combination of independent person and situation factors. The

former position makes a great deal of sense, for it is unclear in what
terms a taxonomy of objective “situations” is to be constructed so
that they may then be correlated with behavior. Most of the significant “situations” that one can readily imagine as having some kind
of impact on behavior cannot be described in terms of objective

62 - Models of Desire

spatiotemporal boundaries nor even in some set of invariant social
or cultural terms; instead, reference must be made to the agent’s
relation to the “situation” in question, in which case the situation’s
role as a fully independent variable is vitiated.
Girard is clearly an “interactionist” in the sense that he describes
the development and manifestation of personality primarily in
terms of the agent’s ongoing interaction with other agents.> Girard’s stress on “patterns of interaction” and on systematic relations reveals a strong affinity to Bateson (1972), a common source

being cybernetics and systems theory. Girard’s stress on this point
is so great that he has chosen to speak of an “interdividual psychology,” a neologism that underscores the refusal to acknowledge the
existence of personal essences prior to the reality of interaction.
Nor is the Girardian psychology a differential one at a fundamental level: the assumption is that in certain important respects there
is an essential sameness to human individuals (whence his emphasis on the theme of “the doubles”). The terms of this “sameness”

need, I think, to be stated very precisely, and at times there may be
a blurring of normative and descriptive categories in such passages. Girard certainly does not say that all personality structures
are at all times the same: the structures of “the coquette”’s attitudes and typical actions are not identical to the structures of everyone else’s, for otherwise, why speak of “the coquette” at all? Yet
certain attributions of differences to this type of personality are
identified as illusory (e.g., the rejection of any belief in a coquette’s
or anyone else’s successful and superior narcissism), and it is in relation to these attributions that the thesis of an essential identity of
“the doubles” finds its first meaning. The point to be underscored
here is the interactionist emphasis of the mimetic theory, which
moves away from the reified traits approach without having recourse to the theoretical fiction of objective situations directly
serving to motivate behavior.
In developing the theory of mimetic interaction, then, it is necessary to proceed in several directions at once. Discrete episodes of
mimetic desire need to be described in terms of the individual
agent’s momentary attitudes, but these episodes in turn need to be
related to more extensive and more significant segments of a life

63 - Patterns ofMimetic Interaction

history, segments having a certain “narrative” coherence for the
agent. Moreover, a developmental perspective is crucial insofar as
the individual's particular relations to others are in part motivated
by attitudes developed earlier on as a result of interactions with
other individuals. Such long-term processes of interpersonal mediation obviously begin in childhood. Although Girard frequently
criticizes the Freudian tendency to assume that all models are parents and parent surrogates, it is obvious that caretakers are the
human infant’s first partners in mimesis. Even if we agree with
Girard—as I think we should—that other individuals may, at later
stages in life, play decisive roles, it remains necessary to study

the sociocultural conditions under which the role of parental modeling varies. One may well ask, for example, what kind of personal
and cultural conditions are likely to make parental modeling
an overarching influence on the individual or, on the contrary,

only one episode among others in the ongoing formation of

personality.

From Episodic Modeling to Personal Mediation
As a first step toward developing the mimetic theory, I propose a

broad distinction between discrete episodes of imitation and a situation in which an agent has a more significant mimetic relation to
a single model or “mediator.” Punctual modeling, then, would be
distinguished from personal mediation. Some such distinction is
clearly implicit in Girard’s analyses of cases where the agent is
strongly focalized on a particular model, and this kind of focalization is also what distinguishes an atomistic variety of imitation
from processes instrumental in the formation and transformation
of the larger dispositions one may associate with “personality” and
“patterns of interaction.” Thus I think the distinction is crucial to
the theory and needs to be developed. The terminology is to some
extent arbitrary. Freudians would probably claim that what is at
stake in the distinction is the difference between imitation and
identification, but I think it best not to adopt a term that is so heavily charged with the meanings granted to it in a host of conflicting

64. - Models of Desire

Freudian constructions, the status of which is open to question.
On this point I follow Bandura’s trenchant survey of the literature
on “identification”:
Except for the common assumption that identification involves wholesale
incorporation of personality patterns, there has been little agreement
among its proponents on how the construct should be conceptualized.
.. . Not only is there little consensus of how imitation should be distinguished from identification, but some theorists assume that imitation produces identification, while others contend, with equally strong conviction, that identification produces imitation.®

In what follows, I will reserve the term “mediator” for cases where

the mimetic agent’s relation to another person is more significant
and more enduring than an instance of punctual imitation. “Significant” and “enduring” are, of course, rather vague notions, but

the intuitive basis of the distinction being proposed can be evoked
by pointing out that typically a child’s relation to its caretakers 1s a
very good example of the kind of significant, long-term modeling
that we have in mind; but of course, parent-child relations are not

so very easy to define and do not exhaust the category, and we need
to provide some more explicit criteria if the proposal is not to be as
vague as the notions of “identification” just criticized.
It may be useful to give examples that illustrate the basic intuition behind the distinction that defines the two extremes of a
broad spectrum of types of imitation. If someone chooses to purchase a particular brand of toilet article more than once as a result
of an imitative relation to a particular actor who appears in television advertisements, this actor should hardly be called the agent’s
“mediator,” although the performer clearly does serve as the referent of the agent’s model concept. If, on the contrary, someone is
led to adopt certain basic political attitudes and make crucial professional decisions as a result of the influence of a particular leader,
then we may speak of that leader as having served as a “mediator”
for the agent. In both cases there is an imitation of another person,
but the differences between these cases may be more important
than the similarities.

65 - Patterns ofMimetic Interaction

Yet what is the nature of these differences? Let us evoke another
example. In acquiring a skill such as driving an automobile, it is
necessary to attend to what others say and do, to model one’s own
performance after the implicit and explicit norms that determine
the difference between competent and incompetent driving. An
effective way to acquire such a skill is to imitate some particular
person who plays the role of instructor. This kind of imitation is a
matter of learning to perform a number of different actions that
together make up the complex competence labeled “knowing how
‘to drive.” Now, if an agent learns to drive from some one person,
does it follow that this person is the agent’s mimetic model or mediator? We can plausibly argue that more than one desire is effectively imitated; in watching the model, the agent learns that it is
desirable to have the hand brake on when parking on a hill; that he
wants to make sure that there is no car in the other lane before
changing lanes, and so on. He may also acquire a preference for
standard or automatic shift and perhaps even an expressive “style”
of driving, such as big brother’s tendency to take risks while overtaking, and honking aggressively when another driver does not see
that the light has turned green. There is clearly a lot of imitation
going on in this very basic and unexciting example of learning. But
is the bundle of mimetic episodes involved in learning how to
drive from someone a case of having a mediator? Is the overall sequence “significant” enough? The example can be pushed in either
direction: in some cases driving is a matter of personal mediation
and in others it is not. Imagine a case where learning to drive is
anything but a robust example of personal mediation. In this case
all of the motivational dispositions imitatively acquired through
interaction with the driving instructor pertain uniquely to the
basic competence required for driving. In other words, no other
objects of desire from any other sphere of activity are involved.
Moreover, no basic attitudes or preferences are added or altered,
especially not any important attitudes pertaining to the agent’s
sense of his or her personal identity or self-concept. Finally and
perhaps most important, the person taking the course in driving
has not been drawn to this particular instructor because of some
significant tutelary belief about that individual; on the contrary,

66 - Models of Desire

the student’s belief that this person is a qualified driving instructor
alone suffices to motivate imitative learning.
Let us now imagine a contrasting example of someone for
whom the learning experience is an instance of personal mediation. Suppose a teenage boy learns to drive from an older friend,
who also serves as his model in other activities, such as how to

dress, how to dance, and how to produce a gentle fade with a six
iron. In learning to drive, the young man forever abandons a number of former pleasures and believes that getting a driver’s permit
involves an explicit and major change of personal identity. For the
teacher and learner alike, driving is a supreme pleasure; cruising
about town is viewed as an important end in itself as well as a
means to other ends. Moreover, the older friend is believed to be a

special and particularly qualified teacher, and more generally, the
kind of person that he would himself hope to become.
It is possible to extract from these illustrative examples some
basic criteria that would typically correspond to the differences between punctual imitation, isolated episodes of mimetic desire, and

personal mediation. Punctual imitation can be motivated by any
number of “thin” beliefs about the model; for example, the imitator may simply take a chance that copying the other person’s behavior could be an effective means to a given end and has no other,

more substantive reason for imitating that particular person. I
have suggested above that in all cases of mimetic desire, the tutelary belief that qualifies someone as a model to be imitated involves,
on the contrary, the belief that this person is, for various reasons,

“the kind of person” that the imitator would like to be. In other
words, in any episode of mimetic desire, the imitator has both a
real and an ideal conception of self and perceives someone else as
corresponding to the ideal in some significant way. In respect to
that ideal, the other’s desire is deemed worthy of imitation.
Such requirements are not met by my typical example of someone who learns to drive from a driving instructor. The imitator
may very well aspire to be the kind of person who drives very competently, and this for any number of reasons, but the imitator does
not long to be the kind of person who gives driving lessons for a
living. Copying someone’s preference for a certain type of cosme-

67 - Patterns of Mimetic Interaction

tics could be another example in cases where it does not entail that
one has any more generalized longing to become the type of person that this individual represents. A purely episodic instance of
mimetic desire, however, would require that the imitative desire be

‘

motivated by a more substantive belief about the model. Fully
fledged “personal mediation” would correspond to cases where
this kind of mimetic modeling comes to have a longstanding significance for the individual’s self-conception. Such is the case in the
example of the teenager’s relation to his older friend. The young
man’s consistent and longstanding imitation of his older friend is
motivated by his belief that this fellow embodies a range of virtues
that are together fully definitive of the kind of identity he would
like to create for himself. And it is this direct and enduring relationship to an idealized self-concept that is at the heart of personal
mediation, which is thereby quite different from the purely instrumental relation to a person from whom one borrows relatively
trivial means to ends that one has already determined for oneself.
One may, it is true, look to a genuine mediator in search for effective means to one’s ends. Moreover, these means need not range

across any great variety of activities. In Girard’s analyses, it 1s often
the case that the mediator is followed in primarily one sphere, that
of romantic desire, yet this one sphere 1s firmly linked in the imitator’s mind to a global conception of self. This latter notion, then, is
the key to the difference between instrumental imitation, episodic

modeling, and mimetic mediation. Given the importance of the
notion in this context, it would be appropriate to specify in more
detail what is meant by a “self-concept.”

The Self-Concept
The notion that needs to be developed here does not require a solution to the various thorny issues surrounding the question of
personal identity. In other words, we need not try to distinguish
between an “agent” and a “person” by requiring the latter to possess a “transcendental apperception” or some other manner of successfully unifying his or her various experiences or recollections.

68 - Models of Desire

Instead of looking for the correct formulation of the unity or identity relation at the heart of a normative concept of personal identity, my aim is to single out a particular type of belief figuring in an
intentional agent’s various mental states (which may or may not
have the kind of unity that would be stipulated by the winning
theory of personal identity, should there ever be one). At the same
time, the idea of the self-concept is complicated and resembles
philosophical discussions of personal identity because it will not
suffice to say that the beliefs in question are simply any secondorder beliefs of an agent A where A happens to figure in the belief.
It makes sense to say that any such belief is at least potentially pertinent to a self-concept that an agent A could have, but it does not
make sense to say that the self-concept of A is reducible to the sum
total of such beliefs. Rather, the belief must involve the agent’s attempt to arrive at a reflective unification, not of the sum total of his
or her experience, but of some privileged subset of the agent’s beliefs,
actions, and preferences.” The self-concept that I have in mind is,

then, an agent’s momentary and perhaps deluded answer to the
question “Who am I?” and not a well-founded and consistent response to the question “What have I experienced?” Thus it differs
from the basic approach to personal identity initiated by Locke
and developed by Anthony Quinton, Paul Grice, and others.’
What must be required of a belief for it to figure as an instantiation of the kind of “self-concept” being invoked above? We do not
need to require that it be accurate nor that it be consistent with
other self-identifying beliefs held at other times by the same agent.
We do, however, need to require that it be in some sense nontrivial

and nontautological. The response to “Who am I?” takes the form
of reference to some set of definite descriptions, not the tautological form of the proper name, or that of the transcendental I = I.
Here I agree with Ernst Tugendhat’s critique of the tradition according to which the particularities of the word “I” are thought to
be the key to self-reflection and to the identification of a person.?
More generally, I think it useful to take up the analysis of the logic
of “knowing who” questions proposed by Steven Boér and William Lycan.!° These authors stress the privileged role of definite

69 - Patterns ofMimetic Interaction

descriptions in responses to such questions and point out that the
“regress” of “who” questions is only stopped by the limiting criteria implicit in the questioner’s “project” relative to the person
whose identity is in question. There is no exhaustive description of
a person, but limited descriptions may serve as adequate responses
to the question “Who is N?” when the question is motivated by a
particular project in relation to N.
This observation is useful when we turn to the issue of the selfconcept, for we may employ it in attempting to specify the nature
- of the “privileged” subset of beliefs about self that make up a “concept” of self. Thus we can say that a self-concept is a response, not
to any and every possible question of the form “Who am I?” but to
questions of that form that are motivated by a certain type of project in relation to oneself. What type of project? As stated above,
our requirement is that the belief must involve the agent’s attempt
to arrive at a reflective unification, not of the sum total of his or her

experience, but of some privileged subset of the agent’s beliefs, actions, and preferences. But what criteria define this privileged subset? I do not think it will be possible to say anything terribly specific on this issue without sacrificing the general validity of the
answer; to say, for example, that the agent’s self-reflection is motivated by a desire to know his or her own “substance” or “essential
identity” is to make too many assumptions about the belief structures supposedly shared by all human agents. A more neutral
phrasing is to be preferred. Thus we could say that the self-concept
arises in an effort to provide a summary of those beliefs, actions,
and judgments that are most “salient” and “characteristic.” And
most frequently, this search will be further delimited by reference
to some particular issue or domain. Thus we would call a particular belief about oneself an instance of the self-concept when it
arises in response to a “Who am I?” question; moreover, this question is concretized in the following form: “In regard to some particular domain of activity, what are my salient beliefs, actions, and
judgments?” The summary provided in response may furthermore

be motivated by particular normative and epistemic goals, such as
questioning the consistency of the beliefs and actions or holding

70 - Models of Desire
them up for evaluation in regard to some other real or ideal performance (e.g., that of an idealized mediator). Clearly, a lot of different kinds of projects are contained within the more general project
of arriving at a summary description of one’s salient beliefs, actions, and preferences.
To sum up, we will say that an agent A’s belief is an instance of
A’s self-concept if the belief serves, for A, to provide a summary
description of some of A’s salient beliefs, actions, and judgments.

This description is developed in reference to some particular domain of activity deemed by the agent to be of special importance,

and thus it is not necessary for A to see the description in question
as representing a global or exhaustive account of all of A’s experience and doings. Yet it is stipulated that A view the subset as
characterizing a central or significant facet of A’s beliefs, actions,

and judgments. Typically, this means that the self-referential beliefs have to do with A’s understanding of one or more of A’s intrinsic ends or life themes, or perhaps with the issue whether A is
successfully realizing these (1.e., the beliefs could focus on the appropriateness of A’s actions in relation to a particular end).
Emphasizing intrinsic ends is, however, but one way to satisfy
the requirements of the “central or significant” clause. (Note that
the “self-efficacy” conception privileged by some contemporary
social psychologists represents one way in which an agent may engage in a self-interrogation. My point here is more general in that
we do not assume that the most significant and most important
instances of “Who am I?” questions all have the hidden form, “Am
I efficacious?”)"! A schematic example ofa self-concept may help to
clarify what is meant. It occurs to Chang Guangxi while visiting a
neighbor that truly reverent people make generous votive offerings at the familial shrine, and he is incited to ask himself whether

he has consistently done so in the past, as well as whether he is

intending to do so in the future. In response to this self-query,
Chang Guangxi thinks that he has not in fact been as diligent as he
could and should have been, and he also thinks that this fact is im-

portant to his life. This belief, then, is an example of a nontrivial
self-concept.

71 - Patterns ofMimetic Interaction
Mimetic Relations and Interactions

We may now develop the analysis of mimetic phenomena involving significant relations and interactions between at least two desiring agents. Although the distinction is implicit in my argument,
I will not try to provide here a precise definition of the difference
between “interactions” and other relations between human agents.
One could in this context evoke the Weberian intuition whereby
social interaction requires that the agents in question have a “sub- Jective orientation” toward each other.!? It may be interesting to
note in passing that by Weber’s own admission, imitation is a phenomenon standing “on the indefinite borderline of social action”
because an agent’s imitation of another agent’s action does not entail that the imitator has the requisite sort of “subjectively meaningful orientation toward” this other.!3 Even less does it entail that
the two agents have a reciprocal meaningful orientation toward
each other, for the model or mediator may or may not be aware of

the imitator’s existence and may or may not act in function of the
other’s anticipated behavior.
Girard’s analyses of mimetic relations embrace a broad spectrum of cases, ranging from an individual’s or group’s mimetic relation to a model who does not reciprocally orient himself or herself to the imitators, to the intensively interactive configuration
that Girard calls double mediation. Instead of seeking to establish
taxonomic distinctions within this spectrum, I shall undertake to

provide careful descriptions and analyses of some of the most important configurations emphasized by Girard. This approach will
at the same time allow me to highlight certain differences between
forms of mimetic desire as we move along the spectrum ranging
from mimetic relations to mimetic interactions.
I shall now provide a first approach to what is one of Girard’s
most unique aid original insights, namely, his discovery of the
general interactional pattern known as double mediation. At the
most general level, double mediation is any situation in which two
agents serve reciprocally as each other’s models or mediators.

Various factors must be taken into account before this basic insight

72, - Models of Desire

can be translated into an adequate description of the phenomena
associated with double mediation.
To begin, Girard’s analyses often focus more specifically on
cases where the mimetic desires in double mediation are of the emulative variety. Cases of reciprocal imitative (nonrivalrous) desiring are conceivable and no doubt very “healthy,” but this is rarely
what Girard has studied under the rubric double mediation. Instead, for Girard, the world of double mediation is a world of internal mediation, that is, a world where agents no longer have any

firm conviction in the unassailable externality and transcendence
of models. Social ideologies of stable and long-lasting hierarchical
differentiation have been vitiated, giving way to a world where
rivalry and competition, far from being restricted by social norms
and systems of belief, are widely encouraged and promoted. In
Girard’s terms, this is the movement from a world of external me-

diation to a world of internal mediation. It should perhaps be reiterated that the distinction in question is not determined by the
physical presence or absence of the mediator within the desiring
agent’s sphere of existence. Such a factor may be important to the
description of actual situations but is always secondary to a more
fundamental fact, one that concerns the kind of beliefs that the im-

itator possesses in regard to the mediator or model. To grasp this
point, we need only recall Girard’s remark to the effect that although Don Quixote is almost always within close physical proximity to Sancho, the latter never dreams of emulating his master,
who forever remains Sancho’s external mediator (DDN 9/23).14

The world of internal mediation is a sociocultural context in
which a profound crisis has disrupted the kinds of imaginary social
differences and barriers that constitute the apparent stability of a
Sancho’s relation to a Don Quixote. This is a world of acquisitive
individualism and “upward mobility,” a world where equality is
held forth as the yardstick of all justice and legitimacy, indeed as
the very regulative ideal and vanishing point of social life, while at
the same time a perpetual quest for personal distinction is the predominant emblem and lifetime burden of the successful and “welladapted” individual. Thus the difference between external and internal forms of mediation corresponds directly to the historical

73 - Patterns ofMimetic Interaction

emergence of certain ideologies of equality, ideologies that reveal
the arbitrary and illusory bases of the institutions and differentiating concepts that formerly erected barriers between agents. Thus
Girard writes: “The increasing equality—the approach of the mediator in our terms—does not give rise to harmony but to an even
keener rivalry. Although this rivalry is the source of considerable
material benefits, it also leads to even more considerable spiritual

sufferings, for nothing material can appease it. . . . When he emphasises [szc] the vicious circle in which the passion for equality is
.trapped, Tocqueville reveals an essential aspect of triangular desire” (DDN 136—37/160).
Although this factor sometimes is not sufficiently emphasized
by Girard’s interpreters, his analyses of double mediation must always be understood as being inscribed within this particular sociohistorical framework, that is, one defined in terms of a radical

overturning of the ideology of hierarchy, on the one hand, and the
emergence of a contradictory ideology of equality and competitive
individualism, of leveling and distinction, on the other.!> For this

reason, the theory of mimetic desire is not an individualistic psychology or a social psychology of small group dynamics. Even less
is it a philosophy of “self and other.” On the contrary, the theory
of mimetic desire 1s intimately connected to a conceptualization of
the predominant cultural and social institutions of the modern
world. A characteristic passage in which Girard stresses the interrelation between sociocultural factors and the analyses of individual psychology reads as follows:
As we have pointed out, only our society can unleash mimetic desire in a
number of different domains without having to dread an irreversible escalation in the system, the runaway defined by cybernetics. It is because of
this unprecedented capacity to promote competition within limits that
always remain socially, if not individually acceptable that we have all the
amazing achievements of the modern world—
its inventive genius, and so
on. The price for all of this is perhaps not invariably the aggravation
beyond all bounds, but certainly the democratization and vulgarization,
of what we call neuroses, which are always linked, in my view, to the reinforcement of mimetic competition and the “metaphysical” aspect of the

related tensions. . . .

74. - Models of Desire
In a world where individuals are no longer defined by the place they occupy by virtue of their birth or some other stable and arbitrary factor, the
spirit of competition can never be appeased for once and for all. Indeed it
gets increasingly inflamed; everything rests on comparisons that are necessarily unstable and insecure, since there are no longer any fixed points of
reference. The manic-depressive has a particularly acute awareness of the
state of radical dependence that people occupy vis-a-vis one another, and
the lack of certainty that results. As he sees that everything around him is
image, imitation, and admiration (tmago and imitare derive from the same
Latin root), he passionately desires the admiration of others. He wishes
for all mimetic desires to be polarized around himself, and he lives
through the inevitable lack of certainty—the mimetic character of what
develops—with a tragic intensity. The smallest sign of acceptance or rejection, of esteem or disdain, plunges him into dark despair or superhuman ecstasy. (TH 307—8/432-—33)

Reciprocal Mediation
I shall now present a schematic description of a minimal episode of
reciprocal mediation. Given two agents, A and B, at time t, neither

A nor B is the other’s mediator or model (but neither has any beliefs about the other that could prevent this from happening). At
t,, agent A somehow rightly or wrongly comes to believe that B
desires a particular object of desire, S. Agent A then mimetically
desires S in turn. Let us temporarily call the interesting variant in
which B, at t,, does not in fact desire $, DM,, as opposed to DM,
which is a case where B at t; does desire S. Now, at t,, A behaves in

a manner such that A’s desire for S is known to B at ty (or, say, t3).
B’s knowledge at t; of A’s desire for S then serves in the formation
of B’s mimetic desire, at ty, for S (with A, of course, as B’s media-

tor). At ts, B in turn behaves in a manner such that B’s desire for S
is known to A. In the case of DM,, this means that A’s false belief
that B desired S is replaced by a true belief about B’s desire for S. In

the case of DM), A has new evidence for believing that B desires S.
(Let us note in passing that it is imprecise in most cases to say that
A’s false belief “becomes true” as a result of this process: if A
wrongly believes at t, that B desires S, and if B does desire S at ty

75 - Patterns ofMimetic Interaction

and A knows it, it does not follow that A’s first belief was any less
false. It is correct to say, however, that A’s false belief has, through

an interactive process, contributed to the realization of a situation
where B comes to desire S and A knows it. But note as well that A’s
false belief at ty is only “self-fulfilling” given the satisfaction of certain conditions, namely, those under which B is somehow disposed to take A as his or her model; we would generally point to
the requisite tutelary beliefs about A in delineating these conditions.) The process of double mediation need not stop at ts. At t¢,

. A renews his or her efforts to have S, which only adds further confirmation to B’s views concerning his mediator’s inclinations. Thus
we have a cycle that would tend to reinforce the agents’ respective
desires, all other things being equal.
Now, what is interesting about this general type of interactive
sequence? Although at first glance the scenario just introduced
may seem unremarkable, it is in fact the basic schema of a complex
and rich model having a surprising generative capacity. I will make
some broad claims along these lines before settling down to a
more methodical demonstration. Very generally, two features may
be identified. First is the emphasis on the strongly systemic quality
of the relation, for as Girard points out, it is a matter here of the
kind of informational feedback process that cybernetic approaches
find at the heart of complex communicational and interactive systems. I will demonstrate this in more detail in a moment, but it

should be obvious that we have here a case of strong reciprocal
determination (a kind of Wechselwirkung) between the agents.
Moreover, the system is not static, for in certain of its possible
sequences, states emerge that are qualitatively different without
having been intended by any of the parties concerned. This leads
us to a second very general point: we have here an analysis of an
intersubjective dynamic that refers consistently to the agents’ cognitive states, including not only their interpretations of their own
situations and actions and those of the other parties but also the
agents’ ongoing conceptions of the interactive system as a whole.
The intersubjective reality of the interaction, however, is not reducible to either of these subjective viewpoints nor to the sum
total of the two.

76 + Models of Desire

The analysis, then, is both systemic and cognitive. In regard to
the latter, what is of particular interest is the emphasis on the generation and role of certain forms of misrepresentations within the
system of interaction. Girard’s analysis of double mediation is,
properly speaking, “mythogenetic” insofar as it describes a systematic emergence and reinforcement of states of misunderstanding (if
not delusion) in the agents. Not only does the process generate the
latter, but it engenders certain forms of self-defeating behavior as
well. Girard thus proposes a model of a kind of subjective irrationality without having recourse to the arbitrary positings of
death drives or any similar constructs. Finally, the claim is made
that the theoretical model of double mediation has a special explanatory value in relation to certain specific varieties of rivalrous
and aggressive interaction. This is not equivalent to the more general claim that imitation and modeling can be the vehicles for the
spread of aggressive forms of behavior—a claim that would in no
way be unique to Girard. Bandura, for example, writes that “modeling influences play an especially important role in the rapid contagion of aggression.”!6 Rather, the claim is that a particularly pernicious type of cycle of aggression is successfully modeled in the
theory of double mediation.
In what follows I shall unpack and evaluate these broad claims.
I begin with the cognitive elements of the system before moving
on to the larger issues of its genesis of self-defeating and aggressive
interactional patterns. I take up the particular claim about a strong
link between mimetic interaction and aggression, and more specif-

ically, cycles of revenge, in chapter 5.

Mimests and Misrepresentation

To what extent does the theory of mimetic desire in general, and
the theory of double mediation more particularly, depend on assumptions about the necessity ofthe mimetic agent’s misrepresentation or unawareness of certain key factors of his or her situation?
An answer to this question 1s suggested by the fact that a key term
in Girard’s discussions of mimetic desire is méconnaissance, which

77 + Patterns ofMimetic Interaction

may be translated variously as “misreading,” “failure to recognize
or to appreciate fully,” “misrepresentation,” and so on. Girard
speaks of desire’s “false conclusions,” of its “forgetting,” its “primordial error,” and of its “inability to recognize” certain realities
(TH 329/461). He asserts that there is a logic proper to the méconnussance engendered by mimetic phenomena. In the context of a
discussion of Freudian theories, he proclaims that the failure to
recognize the true nature of mimesis is “le véritable inconscient [the
real unconscious]” (VS 189/260, Girard’s emphasis).}7 In a striking
- passage, Girard expands upon the strange combination of knowledge and error, lucidity and forgetting, at work in certain advanced stages of double mediation: “Desire is always using for its
own ends the knowledge it has acquired of itself; it places the truth
in the service of its own untruth, so to speak. . . . The idea of the
demon who bears light is more far-reaching than any notion in
psychoanalysis. Desire bears light, but puts that light in the service
of its own darkness” (TH 304/429).
Is it possible to distinguish between the different kinds of
méconnassance that are at once a condition and a consequence of
mimetic interaction? It may be useful to recall here that in the most
general category of mimetic desire proposed in chapter 2, it is not
stipulated that the self cannot be aware of its own desires or of the
role of mimesis in the formation of those desires (which is not the
same as claiming that the self can attain some kind of total selfknowledge). Nor does it seem a good idea to make the total lack of
any significant moments of awareness of the mimetic bases of desire part of a normative (or descriptive) concept of normality. In
some cultural contexts, it may be highly normal to be aware that
one’s self-concept is largely determined in relation to some other
person, such as an elder, teacher, parent, religious leader, or dzrec-

teur de conscience. Is there any sense in which cases of external mediation necessarily or commonly involve a form of méconnaissance?
An answer to this question will put us in a better position to isolate
the specificity of the misunderstandings and distortions at stake in
internal (and double) mediation. Now, although this is quite out
of keeping with Girard’s tendencies, I think that we want to say
that no delusion or distortion is necessarily part of every case of

78 - Models of Desire

external mediation. Let us recall that following our analysis, only
two beliefs necessarily figure in a minimal episode of mimetic desire, namely, the belief about a model’s desire for a particular object
and a tutelary belief, which qualifies this agent for the desiring
agent. Given that either of these two beliefs can refer falsely to a
real state of affairs, either can be the source of a misrepresentation
on the part of the desiring agent. Moreover, one could also identify a second type of cognitive constraint within mimetic desire by
pointing to the agent’s inability (or consistent failure) to have an
accurate awareness of certain other aspects of his or her situation
—not a méconnaussance but a manque de connaussance, or lack of belief in regard to a particular issue or reality.
A first question, then, concerns the necessity of falsehood 1n the

imitative agent’s two beliefs about a model or mediator. I think we
want to assume that an agent can have a true and justified belief
about another agent’s state of desire for an object, and so we allow
that the first belief can be a relatively undistorted form of knowledge.!8 What about the tutelary belief? Many of the cases referred
to by Girard certainly involve an agent’s credence in the superhuman qualities of the model, who is wrongly granted a difference or
prestige, a form of being or knowledge, having no basis in reality;
as Proust’s narrator puts it, the other is thought to “withhold the
secret of truth and beauty.”!9 Could there be a tutelary belief that is
not erroneous? The question hinges upon many far-reaching assumptions about the status of value judgments and the evaluation
of the rationality of preferences, but I think it is possible to establish, at least as a limiting case, that all beliefs qualifying another
agent as a model need be neither false nor unjustified. The purely
strategic calculations motivating the preferences of Orléan’s rationally mimetic agent could serve as an example, for such an agent
makes a cognitive best bet in a situation of uncertainty.
It could be argued, however, that as soon as genuine tutelary
beliefs are in question, the agent’s beliefs necessarily lack any sort
of robust epistemic warrant. Such an argument could be based on
a normative relativism to the effect that no such thing as an intrinsically valuable state of affairs really exists. Following such arguments, the “ought” cannot be grounded in the “is,” and value is

79 : Patterns of Mimetic Interaction

reducible to the preferences of organisms for one state as opposed

to another. It would follow that it is impossible to have any knowledge about which state of affairs is really intrinsically best; there

could be knowledge about one’s own and others’ preferences as to
the “kind of person” one wants to be, but to think that some model
has the secret to the question of value is an essential misrepresentation of the situation. It would follow as well that the uncertainty
about the value of things, an uncertainty that is said to be an important basis for mimetic desire, would arise quite spontaneously
~ as soon as agents recognized the truth that judgments of value have
no real basis in matters of fact.
I will neither develop nor seek to refute the latter line of argumentation here. Even if we were to accept this normative relativism, it is not clear in any case that it necessarily follows that we

want to attribute a fundamental méconnaissance to all desiring
agents who believe that a mediator embodies a certain type of social or personal virtue. In some cases, the cultural context and
framework of beliefs is such that to speak of the relativity of value
is to import a philosophical problem that can only result in a distortion of the description of the conditions of desire. This said, it
may be useful to make one further observation about the typical
form of the tutelary beliefs supporting external mediation. Frequently these beliefs have to do with an agent’s acceptance of a
permanent hierarchical difference between self and other. Such beliefs can effectively function to legitimate and sustain institutionalized forms of domination and privilege, and thus any sociopolitical ideology dedicated to a norm or ideal of social justice
based on some understanding of equality will tend to reject the
conceptual bases of external mediation. Yet the shift to internal
mediation does not necessarily mean that the beliefs in hierarchy
are totally eradicated. As we will see below, at least one form of
internal mediation involves a tendency to generate and strengthen
the agents’ misrepresentations of each other. What should be contrasted, then, is not a world of stable hierarchy and a world of

equality but worlds where personal identity is part of a relatively
stable system of social differentiations, on the one hand, and
worlds where identity is caught up in a complex and difficult pro-

80 + Models of Desire

cess of social comparison, unguided by unanimous and stable
standards.?°
Moving on to the second type of cognitive constraint on mimetic desire, we now ask whether there are any important facts or
states of affairs of which the desiring agent cannot become aware
in the case of external mediation. My view is that the theory should
include the possibility of an agent who has a second-order belief
that his or her own state of desire 1s linked to his or her own belief
about the model’s desires. The mimetic agent can know that she
believes in the model’s prestige and can know that this beliefisthe
reason why she desires the same thing the model desires. Is such an
awareness also possible in the case of internal mediation? We have
defined the latter in terms of the agent’s emulative relation to a
model, which means the agent wants to replace or supplant the
model in relation to some particular object of desire. There is no
logical reason why such a desire could not exist alongside a belief
in the model’s absolute supremacy or superiority, or even a belief
that the origin of the desire is the agent’s own model attribution.
In other words, the agent of internal mediation need not believe
that his or her own desire is anterior to the model’s desire; nor need

this agent be unaware of the structure of the desiring relation.
Thus at a purely formal level, the cognitive constraints are the same
as in the case of external mediation. But there is something misleading about this conclusion, particularly if we reflect on what
was said about the ideologies of individualism, equality, and competitiveness characteristic of the world of internal mediation. The
strictly formal possibilities of our most general model of internal
mediation do not capture the typical configurations that are emphasized in the majority of Girard’s analyses, which focus on the
special case of double mediation. Let us proceed, then, to a discus-

sion of the cognitive constraints characteristic of the latter.

Cognitive Constraints and Double Mediation

I shall begin with a case of reciprocal or double mediation. By definition, we have here a situation where both agents desire a similar

81 - Patterns ofMimetic Interaction

object and where it is impossible for both of these desires to be
successfully realized. Now, what do the two agents know about the
situation? Each is aware of the other’s desire, and each has a tute-

lary belief qualifying the other as a model. In what follows, we
will assume that the tutelary belief is initially a rather minimal one,
and not a far-reaching and profound belief in the absolute and permanent superiority of the model. At this point we have a situation
in which each agent has at least the following cognitive states: (a)
recognition that the other desires an S; (4) recognition that self
~ desires a similar S’; (c) recognition that S and S’ are mutually exclusive situations; and (d@) at least a minimal tutelary belief that
qualifies the other as model. What other misrepresentations, states
of awareness, and lacks of awareness, then, can be added to such a

situation to produce a variety of different outcomes? A survey of
Girard’s discussions of double mediation suggests that the following list of possible cognitive states should be taken into account. A
“+” mark indicates an occurrent or focal belief held by the agents;
a “—” mark indicates a constraint that determines the absence of
the belief or recognition mentioned (which is different from having an occurrent belief in the logical negation of the belief marked
“+”). In the following list, the term “belief” is used to refer to

false beliefs, while “recognition” is reserved for true beliefs.

+ Belief that other desires S
+ Recognition that self desires S’
+ Recognition of mimetic basis of own desire for S’

This list pertains to a particular moment in the interactional sequence of reciprocal mediation, a moment when neither agent has
defeated the other in the attempt to satisfy the emulative desire. In
the sequence sketched above, this was the moment t; or ts, the moment when both agents are aware of the other’s desire, and the
moment when both have the occasion to interpret the other’s resistance to the satisfaction of selfs desire for S’. Obviously, the sequence does not normally end here, and the possible scenarios will
be discussed in a moment. But much remains to be said about the
possible configurations at t;. For the moment, we will simplify the
analysis by making the arbitrary assumption that the agents’ beliefs are perfectly symmetrical: if an item in the list applies to A,
then it applies to B. Clearly, in a second stage of analysis this assumption will not hold. In either case, a first question concerns the
compatibility of the different kinds of beliefs in the list. A second
question has to do with the impact that these different beliefs, and
different combinations of them, will likely have on the agent’s subsequent desiring states and action. Girard is interested in the ways
certain of these beliefs are privileged, as well as in how they in turn
tend to lead to certain pathways of interaction, including the reinforcing of self-deception, rivalry, and self-defeating behavior.
First, let us look at the question of compatibility. It would be
tedious to make an exhaustive list, but a few observations are in

order. Quite trivially, the following pairs are incompatible: (4)
and (6); (4) and (5); (8) and (10); (6) and (7); and so on. Ob-

83 - Patterns ofMimetic Interaction

viously, (1), (2), and (3) obtain in all cases, so the question is

which other items will figure in particular scenarios. In cases in
which both agents are lucid about the mimetic nature of their desires, (4) and (7) will hold; should they also be aware that the

other is imitating them, (8) and (11) obtain as well. The configuration that Girard considers to be far more important, indeed fundamental, can be called the “disciple’s situation.” The disciple knows
that he or she is imitating the model, and thus (4) and (7) obtain.
Also, the disciple attributes an essential superiority to the model,
~ which rules out any belief that the model could be imitating the
disciple (9). This can often be linked to the disciple’s view that the
model’s desire is spontaneous or nonmimetic (10). Here we encounter what Girard calls the fundamental error of the disciple,

and of mimetic distortions in general. The cognitive items just
mentioned conjoin to support the model’s inferences, the most important of which is a particular misinterpretation of the model’s
opposition to the full realization of the disciple’s desire. Thus the
disciple’s situation involves (13), (14), and (16), which means that
instead of seeing that the rivalry follows from the reciprocal imitation that defines the situation, the rival makes erroneous inferences

about the model’s motivations and relation to the disciple, as well
as inferences about the implications for the object’s value.
Now, what has just been said is crucial to Girard’s reading of the
patterns of double mediation and probably identifies what he sees
as the most basic and common form of méconnaissance, one that

will in turn generate quite far-reaching effects. In a nutshell, it is a
matter of the disciple’s or imitator’s inability to see that the person
taken as model can in turn imitate the disciple. As a result, the disciple consistently misunderstands the model’s behavior as well as
the rivalry that is inscribed within the situation. Girard discusses
this typical pattern in the following passage:
The model-disciple relationship precludes by its very nature that sense of
equality that would permit the disciple to see himself as a possible rival to
the model. The disciple’s position is like that of a worshiper before his
god; he imitates the other’s desires but is incapable of recognizing any
connection between them and his own desires. In short, the disciple fails

84. - Models of Desire
to grasp that he can indeed enter into competition with his model and
even become a menace to him. If this is true for adults, how much truer it

must be for the child experiencing his first encounter with mimetic desire!
(VS 174—75/242)

But why insist in this manner on the point of view of the disciple?
Girard’s response is as follows: “As for the model, no matter how

self-sufficient he may appear, he invariably assumes the role of disciple, either in this context or another. From all indications, only
the role of disciple is truly essential—it is this role that must be
invoked to define the basic human condition” (VS 147/206—7). In
what follows, I follow Girard’s lead in focusing on the perspective
of the disciple, or in other words, the perspective of an agent

whose desires are formed in connection with beliefs about other
agents thought to be hierarchically superior in one way or another.
I am less sanguine, however, about the possibility of identifying
in these or any other terms something called “the basic human
condition.”
At this point, I have only described the initial stages of a typical
pattern of double mediation. What happens next? Here we must
return to Girard’s emphasis on the process of feedback, which en-

genders new effects. Of particular importance is the way in which
the disciple’s initial beliefs are altered by a misunderstanding of the
model’s motives: “Desire refuses to understand why the model
changes into an obstacle, but it sees clearly that this change always
takes place. A phenomenon as reliable as this really must be taken
into account” (TH 327/458). In this regard, Girard writes:
The value of the object grows in proportion to the resistance met with in
acquiring it. And the value of the model grows as the object’s value grows.
Even if the model has no particular prestige at the outset, even if all that
“prestige” implies— praestigia, spells, and phantasmagoria—is quite unknown to the subject, the very rivalry will be quite enough to bring prestige into being. (TH 295/417)

In terms of our list of the cognitive items that may figure in a sequence of reciprocal mimetic interaction, the disciple’s “primordial error” may be described in terms of the retroactive transfor-

85 - Patterns ofMimetic Interaction
mation of the initial configuration of beliefs. More precisely, (1)
and (2) are affected; not only does the disciple now have further
evidence for thinking that the model desires the object in question

but the disciple can infer from the model’s resistance that this
desire is strong. At the same time, the basic tutelary belief that
qualified the model as model is strengthened. Why? In thinking
about this question, we must remember that in the situation being
described, the disciple cannot say to himself that in manifesting an
insistent desire for the object, the model is being propelled by the
- disciple’s own prior manifestation of desire. Thus the model’s
demonstration of desire is read by the disciple first of all as revealing aspects of the model’s character. The particular attributions
will, of course, vary from situation to situation, but such traits as
“force,” “spontaneity,” “strength of the will,” “persistence,” “selfassertion,” and so on, provide a reliable indication of the kind of

attributions that could be expected in a world where the ideology
of acquisitive individualism is the overall framework of evaluation.
To shift to another register, we could say that the disciple tends to
think that the model is being “active,” not “reactive,” in striving
for the object. For example, in Proust, what fascinates the erudite
and worldly Swann is Odette’s “fleeting, elusive, and cunning

will.”21
The resistance to the disciple’s desire is not understood by the
disciple as resistance to the disciple, but as yet another manifestation
of the model’s spontaneous and immediate relation to the object.
At the same time, the disciple can draw certain conclusions about

the implications of the master’s attitude toward the disciple, and
more generally, about the hierarchical relation between them.

(These conclusions are identified, quite schematically, by [18],
[21], and [22] in our list.) According to Girard, this means that the

disciple will tend to interpret what has happened in terms unfavorable to his or her own self-image:
Even if he holds himself to be persecuted, the subject will necessarily ask
himself if the model has not got some perfectly good reasons for denying
him the object. An increasingly weighty part of himself will carry on imitating the model and, by virtue of this fact, will take the model's side,

86 - Models of Desire
secretly justifying the hostile treatment he believes he is undergoing at the
hands of the model and interpreting it as a special condemnation that he
probably deserves. (TH 296/418)

A similar passage includes some additional nuances:
The disciple feels compelled to make some sort of choice between himself
and the model; and it is perfectly clear that he will choose in favor of the
model. . . . The disciple feels guilty—though of what, he cannot be sure
—and unworthy of the object of his desire, which now appears more alluring than ever. (VS 175/243)

In short, the contention is that in at least one typical scenario of
reciprocal mediation, the result is a reinforcement of at least one of
the agent’s desires for the object (14), as well as a transformation or

strengthening of the agent’s beliefs about the model of this desire
(1), but also (18), (21), and (22). Moreover, the process is iterative, so that the consequences of one stage become the starting
point of yet another cycle: “the elements of the system react upon
one another; the prestige of the model, the resistance he puts up,
the value of the object, and the strength of the desire it arouses all
reinforce each other, setting up a process of positive feedback”
(TH 295/417). The result is the agent’s fixation on the model in
question. It 1s easy to see that under these conditions, a situation of
reciprocal modeling could engender either one of two much more
significant types of mimetic interaction: a case where an agent A,
caught up in the process just described, comes to take an agent B as
a mediator; or again, a case where both A and B take each other as
personal mediators. The theory, then, suggests a certain way in
which an agent’s fixation on another agent can transform the latter
into a mediator by means of a process that reinforces and develops
the imitator’s beliefs in the other agent’s qualities and superiority.
Girard insists upon this point in the following passage:
In so far as the mimetic process comes to a conclusion, the model of desire
is transformed increasingly into an ontological model. The more the value
of the object increases, the more this object comes to appear as being

87 - Patterns ofMimetic Interaction
linked to a superiority in being
—a superiority that, in the final analysis, is
that of the model itself. To sum up: once again desire tends to relinquish
the object and become fixated on the model itself. (TH 333/465—66)

If we understand this passage’s emphasis on the desiring agent’s
concern for a superiority of being as merely one way to characterize the kinds of beliefs that may support a relationship of hierarchical difference, it describes perfectly well the way in which modeling and a more significant and binding relationship of personal
- mediation may develop. The particular object of desire and the relation to this particular model are no longer viewed by the desiring
agent as mere means to other, more important ends and come to
be seen instead as intrinsically desirable; the model may be deemed
unique, and there is no question of looking elsewhere; finally, the
outcome is thought to be of crucial importance to the desiring
agent’s own self-definition.

The Self-Defeating Agent

Until now, our retracing of Girard’s analyses of a typical pathway
of reciprocal mimesis has focused primarily on the complex cognitive conditions involved in the first moments of the interaction.

But Girard’s theory does not stop at this point but moves on to
discuss the possible scenarios that follow upon particular outcomes of the rivalry that was occasioned by the two agents’ mimetic convergence on a single object of desire that cannot be
shared. The agents’ interpretations of these outcomes have important consequences for what follows. And it is in these terms that
Girard is able to define some typical syndromes that amount to the
characteristic cognitive, affective, and interactional patterns of
certain formations of mimetic desire.
Focusing still on the perspective of the disciple—the fundamental perspective, in Girard’s view—let us assume that the kind
of process described above has taken place. So that we have an
agent who desires a particular object and is engaged in rivalry with
a mediator who has a particular prestige in the disciple’s eyes. The

88 - Models of Desire
disciple’s beliefs and awareness, then, are as described above, that

is, as including at least the following cognitive features in addition
to the basic elements of personal mediation: (4), (7), (9), (10),

(13), (14), (16), (18), (21), (22). We may now consider the consequences of some of the possible outcomes of the rivalry, such as
the disciple’s failure to realize his or her desire and the contrary
case where the master for one reason or another steps aside, making possible the disciple’s victory. Girard insists that for the agent
of internal mediation, the typical impacts of these two possible situations are by no means equally important: whereas defeat can
serve to fuel the cycle that was already set in motion, victory is
most likely to be a source of disappointment. If the model’s resistance contributed strongly to the disciple’s belief in a hierarchical
difference, the model’s failure to resist, or failure to resist success-

fully, might be followed by a radical collapse of the model’s prestige, which in turn would give rise to a search for a new model. In
one of Girard’s earliest and most evocative images of this turn of
events, the man who fails to find a treasure under any of the stones
he has overturned ends up looking for a stone that he is too weak
to lift and invests all of his hope and energy in a futile attempt to
overturn it (DDN 176/203).

Some readers may be skeptical at this point, for Girard’s parable
may seem to echo the kind of pessimistic maxims that one associates with Schopenhauer (the only pleasure is the absence of pain,
etc.) Or various romantic dicta about the essential futility of desire.
But such conclusions are premature. It would be a mistake to universalize the irrational and self-defeating patterns that Girard describes, but it would also be an error to rule them out in advance.

Rather, we should attempt to confront the difficult questions
raised by Girard’s hypothesis. How could such a process come
to pass? What strange psychological processes, conscious and
nonconscious, must be at work for the agent to fall into such a
profoundly self-defeating and self-deluded pattern of behavior?
Is it really coherent to suppose that people are capable of spontaneously converging on a course of action that is essentially a matter
of selecting only those models that are the most formidable obstacles to the satisfaction of their desires? Must there be a contra-

89 - Patterns ofMimetic Interaction

dictory or paradoxical form of self-deception at work here? Can
Girard’s model really make such a disposition plausible without
making assumptions that are at least as mysterious as the death
drives and “compulsions to repeat” that play a similar role within
various Freudian doctrines? After all, Girard himself has stated

that the real unconscious has to do with the mimetic agent’s tendency to make models into obstacles and obstacles into models.
Girard supports his claim by describing a fundamental ambivalence in the implicit orientation of the emulative agent’s de~ sires. As we have already specified, the emulative agent imitates the
desires of a rival thought to enjoy a hierarchical superiority. It is
this belief in the hierarchical difference that essentially sustains
and promotes the self’s desire for the object desired by the other.
Should self win out over the rival, and should this victory be interpreted as a sign of a previous overestimation of the model, then it
may seem to follow that the object never really had the value that
was wrongly ascribed to it. Such a deflation of the object’s value
may also find independent justification in the perceived discrepancy between the anticipated satisfaction to be had in its possession and the real effects of the victory. Following this scenario,
then, should the agent win out over the rival, the object is won but

loses its value. The desire for it is extinguished, and a state of affairs
the satisfaction of which was formerly of great importance to the
agent becomes a matter of indifference.
Let us turn now to the other horn of the desiring dilemma.
What if the agent cannot win the object? This course of events has
the major disadvantage for the desiring agent of indefinitely
postponing the moment of the desire’s satisfaction, but it has the

strange advantage of sustaining, and indeed of progressively fortifying, the belief in the value of the object, which becomes more

and more precious. Moreover, as long as there is hope, the desiring
agent need never recognize that the intensification of desire is purchased at the price of settling into a position in which desire can
never be successfully realized— if by success we mean a satisfaction

of the desire that is not joined to the painful experience of the disappointment that accompanies the object’s sudden deflation. But
why would this disappointment necessarily accompany the satis-

90 - Models of Desire

faction of the desire? This point is not based on any assumptions
about the inherent features of the objects of desire in question (it 1s
not a matter of a pessimism that presents its depreciation of life’s
joys as a form of realism). Rather, the disappointment is “Proustian” in that it is analyzed as the experience of a discrepancy between two states of affairs, the one anticipated, the other actual.

Thus Girard writes:
For victory to change anything in the fate of the subject, it must come
about before the gap has started to widen between all that possession can
offer in the way of pleasure, satisfaction, enjoyment, and so on, and the

increasingly metaphysical aspirations that are brought into being by the
misconceptions of rivalry.
Ifthe gap is too wide, possession will be such a disabusing experience that
the subject will put all the blame for it upon the object, not to mention the
model. He will never blame desire as such, or the mimetic character of

desire. Object and model are both rejected with disdain. But the subject
sets off in search of a new model and a new object that will not let him
down so easily. From this point, desire seeks only to find a resistance that it
is incapable of overcoming. . . . The pursuit of failure becomes ever more
expert and knowledgeable, without being able to recognize itself as the
pursuit of failure. (TH 297/420)

In Girard’s analysis, then, the self-defeating orientation of a particular formation of mimetic desire is to be understood as the unintended consequence of what is in fact an intention to succeed.
But this intent to succeed has as its object only a particular variety
of success, a goal that is in fact unattainable because it is conditioned by contradictory imperatives. If the desire’s objects were
not prized as a result of false beliefs about a rivalrous mediator,

then its disappointment would not be necessary; but once this

inflation has occurred, the only possible result is disappointment
or the deferral of defeat through conflict. Similar conclusions are
drawn by Proust’s narrator:
In regard to love, our happy rival, which is the same as saying our enemy,
is our benefactor. To a being that only excited in us an insignificant physical desire, he immediately adds an immense and wholly alien value, but

91 - Patterns ofMimetic Interaction
one that we confuse with this being. If we had no rivals, pleasure would
never be transformed into love. If we had none, or if we did not believe

that we had any, for it is not necessary for them to exist in reality. Sufficient to our well-being is that illusory life that our suspicion and jealousy
grant to non-existent rivals.??

To recapitulate, a highly schematic analysis of the interaction
between the disciple and mediator (agent A and agent B) singles
out the following basic elements:
I.

A’s beliefs about self, B, the object of desire, and the rela-

tion between self and model (including model’s opinion
II.
III.

IV.

of A)
A’s desire for the object
B’s resistance (or efforts to realize desire for the same
object)
Avs beliefs about B’s resistance

In these terms, what happens in the type of internal mediation analyzed by Girard is that (I), (III), and (IV) work together to reinforce (II) and to strengthen (I). Also, in those cases where the
mimetic process is fully reciprocal, the same type of process will
be occurring in B, whose desire for the object and efforts to obtain
it will be strengthened by the knowledge of A’s strengthened desire (unless, of course, A manages to succeed in the strategy of
“askesis,” the hiding of one’s desires). The consequence is that
(III) and (IV) are reiterated or strengthened in turn, which refuels
the whole cycle.
According to Girard, this pernicious, cyclical pattern of double
mediation is a basic, dynamic structure underlying any number of
“pathologies” and “syndromes” identified by a range of psychological doctrines. It is in these terms that he discusses “coquetry,”
which he describes as a “particular form of double mediation”
(DDN 105/125). The theory also offers a reinterpretation of the
phenomena typically associated with “sadism” and “masochism”
as well as what clinicians label as “sexual perversions.” I shall not
seek to reconstruct these analyses here, as more basic issues remain
to be taken up. It seems to me that two questions are of particular

92. - Models of Desire
importance. The first has to do with the specific nature of the delusory or self-deceptive processes at work in the scenario of double
mediation. The second concerns Girard’s various claims to the effect that it is possible to explain the genesis of human beings’ violent behavior, or at least some important subset of it, in terms of
his theory.

Double Mediation and Self-Deception
As I have shown above, certain forms of misrepresentation are es-

sential to Girard’s description of the phenomena of double mediation. In terms of my analysis of typical cognitive elements at work
in double mediation, it may be observed that the following items

from the list are pertinent in this regard, for they represent either
erroneous beliefs (marked “+”) or failures to recognize some state
Of affairs (marked);

1.
5.
6.
9.
10.
13.
14.
16.
18.
21.

+ Tutelary belief about other
— Recognition of mimetic basis of own desire for S’
+ Belief in own spontaneity
— Recognition of other’s imitation of self in desire for S
+ Belief in other’s spontaneity in desire for S
— Recognition that other’s resistance is result of mimesis
+ Belief that other’s resistance is sign of object’s value
+ Belief that other’s resistance is sign of model’s prestige
+ Belief in model’s hierarchical superiority to self
— Belief that model believes in selfs (not model’s) hierarchical superiority
22. + Belief that model believes in selfs inferiority

Given this list, it is possible to ask whether any of these items
must be explained in terms of self-deception or related concepts.
More generally, we are interested in explaining how it is that the
agent can systematically fail to know what is the case in regard to
his or her most personal affairs; after all, it is a matter of the agent’s

93 - Patterns ofMimetic Interaction
own motivational states. Items 5 and 6, for example, would seem

to require some explaining: how is it that people can fail to be
aware of their own copying of others, and how can they really believe in their own spontaneity in cases where they are in fact desiring “according to the other”? How is it that the agents can be
aware of their own states of desire but not of the fact that these
desires are linked to the agents’ own beliefs about the model’s desires? Must we invoke paradoxical or mysterious unconscious processes in describing such matters? Must we say, for example, that
mimetic agents lie to themselves in something like the way one
person knowingly deceives another? Girard may seem to move in
this direction when he writes that desire puts what it knows about
itself in the service of “its own untruth” (TH 304/429).
The latter question leads us directly to what has frequently been
discussed as the paradox of self-deception, concerning which there
is a sizable and complex philosophical literature. One author states
the central issue by specifying that self-deception is what happens
when “a person lies to himself, that is to say, persuades himself to

believe what he knows is not so. In short, self-deception entails
that B believes both p and not-p at the same time.”?3 When this
particular analysis of self-deception is adopted, the question that
is usually posed is how it may be brought into line with another
crucial assumption, namely, the basic idea that an agent cannot believe p and not-p in the same sense at the same time. Thus begins
the search for the special senses in which the agent believes p and
not-p, senses that have to do with the specific processes and

modalities whereby such an apparent contradiction could be generated and maintained.”4 For example, the attempt is made to explain the possibility of self-deception in terms of the agent’s awareness and beliefs being partitioned into relatively autonomous
subsystems (either across time or at a single moment). Different
components hold the contradictory beliefs, so that the contradiction (p and not-p) is not actually believed at the same time and in
the same sense by a single subject. Prominent advocates of this approach to self-deception are David Pears and Donald Davidson.
Powerful criticisms of the partitioning strategy, and of the inter-

94 - Models of Desire

personal paradigm upon which it rests, have been presented by
Alfred Mele. In the summary discussion of self-deception and
mimetic desire that follows, I shall adopt Mele’s approach.
Mele argues that the same forms of irrational belief (and behavior) that the partitionists seek to deal with can be explained more
plausibly and economically by an analysis that does not fragment
the agent into a “multiple self.” In particular, he asks whether it is
necessary to have recourse to highly problematic constructs in
which the agent is divided into subsystems that are conceived of as
internal, rational centers of agency and intention. Whereas Mele
recognizes the work of internal processes or subsystems in his explanation of the possibility of the phenomena associated with selfdeception, he critiques any approach that endows the internal subsystem with the intentional capacities that are required in order to
act on reasons and employ a conscious strategy in deceiving another (or the “main”) center of consciousness. In short, Mele seeks
alternatives to those approaches in which the subsystem is said to
act strategically in order to trick a center of consciousness with
which it shares some beliefs but not others.
