Mele proposes that it is not necessary to adopt such strong assumptions; in fact, most self-deception is nonintentional. Mele ex-

plains this point as follows: “To be sure, self-deceivers often do
engage in intentional behavior with the result that they become
deceived with respect to p; but I shall argue that they rarely act
with the mtention of decetving themselves.”> In other words, the
state of deception is an unintended consequence of the agent’s intended acts. We can say that these unintended consequences can
have a systematic character without supposing that they must be
the intended results of some hidden subagent’s strategic actions.
To support this claim, Mele discusses typical sources of consistent
error, such as the confirmation bias, in regard to which there is no

good reason for assuming that the agent or subagent intentionally
seeks to err.
The key to Mele’s approach, then, is his focus on ways in which
data can be consistently manipulated in a motivated yet nonconscious way. Thus he formulates the following characteristic and
sufficient conditions for self-deception:

95 - Patterns ofMimetic Interaction
1. The belief that p which S acquires is false.
2. S’s desiring that p leads $ to manipulate (i.e., to treat inappropriately) a
datum or data relevant, or at least seemingly relevant, to the truth value
of p.
Oo. This manipulation is a cause of S’s acquiring the belief that p.
4. If, in the causal chain between desire and manipulation or in that between manipulation and belief-acquisition, there are any accidental intermediaries (links), or intermediaries intentionally introduced by another agent, these intermediaries do not make S (significantly) less
responsible for acquiring the belief that p than he would otherwise

have been.?¢

Such an analysis is quite adequate to the forms of self-deception
described in Girard’s discussions of double mediation. Although
some of Girard’s wordings may suggest that desire lies to itself,
there is no consistent emphasis on the desiring agent’s having any
conscious intent to deceive himself or herself, and even less em-

phasis on the splitting of the self into a deceiver and a deceived
party. Rather, an alternative and more economical explanation is
available.
The alternative is in a sense analogous to what was said above
about Girard’s analysis of the self-defeating tendencies of a certain
formation of mimetic desire. What does it mean to say that people
sometimes have self-defeating desires? With a little work, one
could elaborate a paradox of self-defeating desire on the model of
the paradox of self-deception: in the case of a self-defeating desire,
if the desire is successfully satisfied, then it is not really a case of
self-defeat, but then the desire is not really satisfied; but if the de-

sire is not successfully satisfied, then there was no real self-defeat,
and so on. Is it necessary, then, to posit a subsystem that desires the

thwarting of the desires of the “main system”? Cases of self-defeating behavior could be described as situations where the desires of
the main system go unsatisfied, while an inner subagent enjoys a
roaring success in thwarting the main system’s wishes. But in fact
the explanation of phenomena associated with self-defeat does not
require such extreme measures, for they need not be described as
cases where the agents have some sort of paradoxical self-referring

desires. Rather, Girard insists that it is the desire for a particular,

96 - Models of Desire

illusory type of success, and not a desire for failure as such, that
leads to the self-defeating consequences. There is no contradiction, then, but merely a sharp contrast between the intended results of a particular kind of desire and the actual consequences of
acting on this desire. Cases in which the agent has no chance of
actually realizing his or her desire and in which the agent is for one
reason or another unaware of this, would correspond to Girard’s
analysis. It is the agent, then, and not just the desire, that is self-

defeating.
Similarly, one could point out that it is not necessary to attribute a desire to deceive themselves to all agents who consistently fall into a certain kind of error. The error may be the result
of a particular manner of misinterpreting data. Moreover, some
kinds of misinterpretations may be motivated by the nature of the
agent’s desires. Mele’s recommendations are useful at this point,
for he points to four general ways in which the self-deceiving
agent’s misinterpretations may work: (1) selective focusing or attending: the agents’ desire that p leads them to fail to attend to
evidence against p and to focus instead on evidence to the contrary; (2) selective gathering of evidence: the agents’ desire that p
gives rise to a dedicated and systematic search for evidence supporting p, whereas the agents will overlook readily available evidence in favor of not-p; (3) negative misinterpretation: the agents’
desire that p leads them to interpret evidence that counts against p
as failing to count against p; (4) positive misinterpretation: as in
the previous case, the agents’ desire that p vitiates the usual evaluation of evidence (here, evidence which in fact counts against p is
read as counting in its favor).?7
Turning now to the cognitive items that may be associated with
the méconnassance at the heart of double mediation, let us see how

these epistemic shortcomings can be justified without our having
recourse to constructs involving intentional subagency. The cognitive items that we have listed fall into four categories: false beliefs about self; missing recognition of aspects of self; false beliefs
about others (the model); missing recognition in regard to others
(the model). The claim is that certain states of mimetic desire are

systematically linked to these cognitive items. Must this imply that

97 - Patterns ofMimetic Interaction

the agent, or some subsystem in the agent, desires not to know the
truth, or to believe in the particular falsehoods in question?
In the case of the agent’s cognitions concerning the model, the
response is negative. The agent’s views of the other may very well
be systematically distorted, but they may nonetheless form a coherent whole, flowing logically not only from the agent’s own erroneous initial assumptions but also from the new data as interpreted in the light of these assumptions. There is no paradox of
self-deception because there is no real cognition of any competing
beliefs and thus no mystery as to how the error could be sustained.
It may be pointed out in this regard that in cultural formations
where the ideology of individualism and personal autonomy is a
central or even dominant framework of belief, the very process of
socialization will tend to inculcate in the agent a viewpoint running contrary to any awareness of the mimetic facets of desire—
especially of desire attributed to others, who may be wrongly
assumed to desire spontaneously and not mimetically. If the disciple’s primordial error is to fail to become aware of the model’s imitation of the disciple, this mistake follows quite coherently from
acceptance of the initial, socially given distinction between the
roles of disciple and master.
We may focus, then, on the items having to do with an agent’s
self-understanding, as these would seem to represent the most
puzzling cases. How could an individualist ideology of spontaneous desire resist the immediate data of the agent’s personal experience? Surely it is wilful self-deception when the agent fails
to notice his or her own glaring lack of spontaneity, somehow
obscuring the active role of the agent’s own beliefs about the
model in the orientation of the agent’s desire. For we have stipulated that the mimetic agent must at some point believe that the
model desires the particular object in question; the agent experiences, at the same moment or perhaps at a later time, a desire for
the same object but does not know that the desire and the belief are
linked (5) or falsely believes that the desire is spontaneous (6).
Now, what, more specifically, would be the grounds for claiming
that (5) or (6) necessarily involves some form of self-deception?
Following Mele’s indications, it must be possible to show that

98 - Models of Desire
the agent necessarily has available good evidence for contrary
states of mind; that is, in the case of (5), which describes an absence of a recognition that p, the agent has good evidence of p but
somehow does not make the inference, while in the case of (6), the

agent falsely believes not-p, whereas there is again good evidence
for p. What might this evidence be? Well, the agent knows that he
or she has certain beliefs about the model, and a bit of introspec-

tion could lead to the conclusion that the model’s prestige could be
at the heart of the agent’s own desire for the same thing. But note
already that this is not “evidence” of some direct and inescapable
variety, like the sharp pain that is evidence that a heavy object has
just fallen on one’s foot.?8 If anything here remotely resembles that
sort of direct, personal evidence, it is the immediacy and perhaps
the urgency of the experienced desire for the object. It should also
be recalled that in many such instances it is a belief in the object’s
intrinsic value that is the focus of the agent’s beliefs: the desire is
experienced as a “pull” exercised on the agent by the object, not as
a projection realized by the lanterns of desire. This fact draws attention away from the whole process whereby the desire was
formed, for the desire 1s experienced as having its source “outside”
the agent, that is, in the object. Similar considerations come into

play in regard to the agent’s thinking about the model’s role, insofar as such thinking in fact occurs. The model’s role may be
obscured because the agent considers it perfectly natural or fitting
that the model should also desire what is believed to be an intrinsically valuable or worthwhile object. In the agent’s mind, the convergence of the two desires on a single object requires no additional explaining and gives rise to no mimetic hypotheses because
the powerful pull of the object itself suffices to explain the
situation.
The more general point here is that the agent’s attention may
focus on any number of aspects of the situation, some of which
may readily be interpreted as evidence of the agent’s or the model’s
spontaneity. These are the elements that would provide the mate-

rial for Mele’s four types of misinterpretations. Given the range of
possible misinterpretations, it becomes clear that nothing requires
the agent to conclude, on the basis of the desire and the belief in

99 - Patterns of Mimetic Interaction

the model’s desire, that the former is linked to the latter: such a link

is not, in any case, a matter of a direct perception but a particular
inference. In short, there is no reason to start with the assumption

that the agent knows a p and must consciously and intentionally
persuade him- or herself to the contrary. Rather, the agent of mimetic desire wrongly believes a not-p in spite of evidence to the
contrary; the agent of mimetic desire would have to know p in
order to be lucid about the workings of his or her own desires, but
the agent of mimetic desire does not believe both p and not-p and
does not have to engage in any form of deliberate or intentional
self-deception in order to be profoundly deluded about self, other,
and their relations.

Triangles ofDesire

I believe that it is worthwhile to conclude this chapter with a few
remarks on the idea of a “triangle of desire” that is often associated
with Girard’s thought. In this regard, my central point is that the
hypothesis of a general “triangularity” of desiring relations should

be dissociated from discussions, in common parlance, of the “romantic triangle,” for the two are really not equivalent. In fact, a
careful analysis of romantic triangles as they appear, for example,
in large numbers of literary works reveals a great deal of complexity.
The “triangular” structure of mimetic desire corresponds quite
simply to the distinction between the model (or mediator) of desire and its object, both being necessary according to the theory.
Girard sometimes speaks, and with good reason, of cases where

the “object” of desire “drops out,” so that what is essential is the
desiring agent’s relation to the model. Following the terminology
that I am proposing, this important insight would be expressed
somewhat differently, for every desire must have an “object” or
content (an S) of some sort, and this S is analytically distinct from
the model, even should the contents essentially involve the model
in some way. In other words, to say that what A desires finally is in
some sense the model is not to say that there is no more specific

100 - Models of Desire

content of A’s desire or that this object of desire is quite simply
“the model.” All such cases need to be described more precisely,
for they in fact involve such desires as A’s wish to be romantically
involved with the model, or A’s desire to defeat a mediator in some

rivalry, and so on. Even if we accept the case of A having a “metaphysical” desire for the appropriation of the substance or being of
some person, this is not reducible to the simple replacement of
the content of desire by the beliefs about the mediator. Thus all
mimetic desires have an “object” in the sense that I have given this
term.
But does this mean that all mimetic desires are “triangular”?
What of a situation where A desires an S, this desire being con-

nected to beliefs about the desires and qualities of two distinct
agents, B and C? In some instances, B and C would conjoin to
form one collective mediator concept, M (e.g., the “happy couple”), but I do not see any reason why the alternate case should be
ruled out in principle. Thus I am led to the heretical conclusion
that all particular instances of mimetic desire need not be triangular.
A more important issue is the analysis of romantic triangles in
mimetic terms. In this regard, I think it necessary to argue for the
view that romantic triangles are frequently more complex than
some analyses would seem to imply. A few summary remarks
along these lines may serve to show that the analysis of such
matters 1s not reducible to the application of a simplistic and schematic “French triangle.”
In its most sexist configuration, the romantic triangle is a matter
of two men engaged in a rivalry for the romantic possession of a
single female, who is spoken of as the “object” of the romantic triangle. But this particular pattern is by no means the only one. A
broad distinction can help to make possible a first approach to this
matter: on the one hand are those rivalries where the conflict between the two parties does not in any important way center on the
beliefs and desires of the “object” in question; on the other hand
are the more prevalent cases where the victory in the conflict is to
an important degree, if not entirely, a matter of the desire of the socalled “object” of rivalry, whose preferences determine the winner.

101 - Patterns ofMimetic Interaction

The former class of rivalries hinges on the interaction between the
two rivalrous parties, and thus its instances are best described as

dyadic interactions, although both rivals’ desires may be triangular
in the sense outlined above.
These two classes of rivalry may now be related to the mimetic
model. In regard to the first, we will focus on only those cases
where the two conflicting parties, say, A and B, serve as each
other’s mediators in their mimetic desires for possession of the
favors of C. The “object” of desire, S, in such affairs can usually be

described in terms of a desire to occupy an exclusive relation of
some sort (€.g., Marriage) to C. Thus A’s relation to B, and B’s

relation to A, is essential to the description of the object of both
agents’ desires. The second type of rivalry is more complex and
embraces different variants. What for shorthand can be called C’s
choice is essential, for even if A’s and B’s deepest concern is win-

ning out over the rival, winning C’s choice is an indispensable part
of the object of desire; thus, the “lines of force” do not run solely
between A and B, even if we focus on cases where A and B are

locked in reciprocal mediation. A distinction between intrinsic
and extrinsic desires may help to tease out some of these different
variations. To construct a case where C has the most minimal role
in the triangle, we may specify not only that C’s choice in no way
determines the outcome but that both rivals desire possession extrinsically as a means to the defeat of the rival, this defeat being the
real object of desire. Quite different would be the case where C’s
choice is determinant and, moreover, desired intrinsically by both
rivals. This is probably by far the most important form of “triangularity” in European literary history, and the theory of mimetic desire should not be taken as insisting dogmatically on the
“priority” of the other forms.
Still other variants of the “triangle” become apparent when we
focus on two other factors: (i) the possibility that C may serve as
mediator for A or B; and (ii) the fact that C may also have A or B as
a mediator for some desires that are relevant to the outcome ofthe
romantic episode. Girard’s analyses of romantic affairs where coquetry is in play would seem to involve (1), for the coquette’s display of self-love is an important piece in the machinery.?? Factors

102 - Models of Desire
(i) and (ii) together raise the issue whether the model should include cases of triple mediation, reciprocal or other.
I shall not linger over these mimetic combinations here, for I
think it more crucial at this stage to clarify and to assess some of
Girard’s other claims. In the next chapter, I turn to Girard’s fundamental anthropology. In that context I shall discuss an important
objection that may be raised in relation to my reconstruction of
the theory of mimetic desire: does not Girard want to link his theory of desire to a larger anthropological hypothesis, one in which
“desire” is no longer the primary and most fundamental type of
mimesis? Does not Girard’s anthropology, with its emphasis on
the generation of cultural phenomena from mimetic mechanisms,
run contrary to an approach in which the explanatory value of mimetic desire depends on a prior identification of cultural and contextual factors?

Chapter Four

Mimesis and the

Origin of Culture

René Girard has stated explicitly that the concept of mimetic desire
does not figure in his theory of hominization, which hinges on a
precultural variety of mimesis called la mimésis @appropriation (appropriative or acquisitive mimesis). The operations of mimetic desire presuppose the existence of human symbolic capacities and a
cultural context, but the primitive forms of mimesis that Girard
discusses do not. Mimetic desire may become central to humanity
later on, he states, but it is not already there at the origin: Girard
expressly comments that at the “elementary level the word desire is
not appropriate” (I 32). He suggests that desire transfigures its objects and that “this process of transfiguration does not correspond
to anything real, and yet it transforms the object into something
that appears superabundantly real. Thus it could be described as
metaphysical in character. We might well decide to use the word
‘desire’ only in circumstances where the misunderstood mechanism of mimetic rivalry has imbued what was previously just an
appetite or need with this metaphysical dimension” (TH 296/
321). And again: “For there to be desire according to our definition, the effects of mimesis must interfere, not directly with ani-

mal instincts and appetites, but in a terrain that has already been
fundamentally modified by the process of hominization: in other
words, the mimetic effects and a wholesale re-processing of symbols must develop in unison” (TH 283—84/308).!
What is at stake, for Girard, in distinguishing between mimetic
103

104 + Models of Desire

desire and a more primitive form of mimesis? The response to that
question hinges on the role that the latter concept of mimesis plays
in Girard’s “fundamental anthropology,” that is, in his hypothesis
about the origin of human culture. Although my main focus in
this study is on mimetic desire and not on Girard’s anthropology,
in the present chapter I shall discuss the distinction between acquisitive mimesis and mimetic desire in order to clarify the nature of
Girard’s different assumptions about agency. To that end I shall
present aspects of Girard’s hypothesis about the origin of human
culture, but it is not my goal to provide a systematic assessment of
it.2 As I stated at the outset of this study, I am not in agreement

with those who believe that one must adopt an all-or-nothing approach to the arguments presented in an author’s writings. In my
view, Girard’s genetic hypothesis is logically distinct from his psychology of mimetic desire, which means that it is coherent to accept the one without the other. Girard’s genetic anthropology conceptually depends on a mimetic model of agency, but as I shall
show in some detail in what follows, that model is not the theory
of mimetic desire. And if, as I argued in the last chapter, Girard’s

psychology of mimetic desire rests on certain broad sociohistorical
assumptions, they are not the ones that are essential to the genetic
hypothesis.

The Many and the One

Girard is widely known for his perceptive descriptions of a social
dynamic having two essential moments: on the one hand, the “crisis of violence,” and on the other, the “scapegoat mechanism,”

which provides a resolution to this violent crisis by uniting the
group in opposition to a single victim. The theory of scapegoating

is at once a description of the basic patterns of interaction characteristic of these two moments and a specification of their sociocultural and psychological conditions and consequences. In regard to
this theory, it is crucial to distinguish carefully between different
“scapegoat mechanisms” in Girard’s analyses, or at the very least,
between different ways in which a similar configuration is man-

105 - Mimesis and the Origin of Culture

ifested. On the one hand, there is the pattern of scapegoating that
figures within Girard’s hypothesis of the origin of human cultures.
This is said to be a mechanism that is activated when crises of violence disturb the order of hominid groups; it produces a collective
murder that results in what Girard calls the first “noninstinctual”
state of awareness. This mechanism and the symbolic effects it produces are the ultimate basis of a properly human social order. On
the other hand, there are the crises and resolutions of violence oc-

curring within historical contexts that are already human and
cultural (i.e., already constituted by the beliefs and institutions of
the sacred). Within the latter category, it is possible to distinguish
between spontaneous instances of the pattern and those that correspond to an institutionalized frame of interaction, such as that of

ritual. The actions and interactions that take place in these two
different frameworks, that is, the ritual and the spontaneous, may

on occasion be structurally isomorphic, and the very boundary
that separates them may in practice be highly unstable, but the distinction between them remains important. My emphasis on such
distinctions is not motivated by a pedantic taxonomic impulse;
rather, these distinctions are at the heart of the explanatory claims

that may be made for Girard’s insights and should be given careful
consideration by anyone who wants to take those claims seriously.
Unfortunately, some of Girard’s readers conflate his various no-

tions of mimesis.
I have quickly distinguished between three different types of
contexts within which certain patterns of interaction—such as the
configuration of the “many and the one” essential to scapegoating—may appear. What is the essential difference between these
different manifestations of an invariant pattern of interaction? The
answer to this question involves the role played by beliefs and symbolic representations in all cultural manifestations of the mechanism. For example, in any instance of scapegoating in a cultural
context, the group’s choice of a victim will be motivated by any
number of beliefs concerning the victim’s “difference,” “guilt,”

and so on. Girard refers to some of these beliefs as the “stereotypes
of persecution” and presents an illuminating categorization of
them.3 He lists the typical signs of “difference” that motivate the

106 + Models of Desire

victim’s selection, a selection that is in fact as “arbitrary” as it is

unjust for the simple reason that the individual chosen could not
possibly have been the cause of the social crisis as a whole. Such,
however, is the mimetically supported delusion of the scapegoat
community, which finds unanimity in opposition to the hapless
victim. It is easy to see how mimetic desire can contribute to this
unanimity: once the accusation has been made and aggressive intent has been expressed, members of the group are strongly motivated to copy the motives of those perceived to be on the side of
order. And this strengthening of social cooperation and consensus
lends a spurious support to the accusatory beliefs that motivated
the selection of the victim. Social order is strengthened or restored
by virtue of the scapegoating process, and this effect is wrongly
interpreted as confirmation of the initial accusation. Scapegoating
“works,” then, but only at the cost of a fundamental misrepresenta-

tion of the victim and of the social process of scapegoating.
The analysis is different in the case of precultural manifestations
of the scapegoat pattern. Girard does not want his genetic hypothesis to be circular, and as a result, in his model of the generation of

cultural beliefs and representations, these beliefs and representations cannot themselves play any role in the functioning of the
very mechanism that is supposed to bring them into existence.
Thus in the description of the mechanism that brings about the
crisis of violence and its resolution by means of an original collective murder, there can be no reference to the hominid group’s beliefs about the victim. This means that the choice cannot be motivated by the stereotypes of persecution or by a belief in the “guilt”
of the victim. Instead, more primitive, nonintentional forms of
mimesis, namely, Girard’s acquisitive and conflictual varieties,
must be responsible for the functioning of the generative mechanism. These same motivational systems may also be operative in
the cultural varieties of the dynamic of crisis and resolution, but
here they may be supplemented and guided by the products of the
mechanism’s own history. Once we are in a historical context, sacred beliefs, notions about the cosmic order, knowledge of kinship
ties, and so on, will play an important role in orienting the agents’
actions. Violence itself, which in the genetic mechanism is but the

107 - Mimesis and the Origin of Culture

product of the most mechanical form of agency, becomes invested
with a cultural signification that in turn shapes the individual’s
motivational states and understandings of the possible and desirable courses of action: “Desire . . . is attracted to violence triumphant and strives desperately to incarnate this ‘irresistible’ force.
Desire clings to violence and stalks it like a shadow because violence is the signifier of the cherished being, the signifier of divinity” (VS 151/211).
In what follows, I shall focus on the conception of agency figur-ing within Girard’s genetic hypothesis, my primary goal being to
elucidate the notions of acquisitive and conflictual mimesis as a
contrast to the theory of mimetic desire. I should say at the outset
that the goal of my analysis is not to convince the reader that
Girard’s genetic hypothesis is correct. I consider that hypothesis to
be a thought-provoking and important speculation, but I do not
believe that the evidence and arguments today warrant any firm
conviction that any one scenario describes the emergence of human culture out of the primate order around a couple of million
years ago. Not only is this theory highly schematic but the available data simply do not dictate its acceptance.*

Primary Mimesis and Hominid Agency

Girard conjectures that it was the mimetic dispositions of hominid
beings that produced the specifically human phenomena that may
be associated with the emergence of culture, beginning with religiosity or the sacred. Mimesis, he claims, is what produces the
sequence of interactions that lead to “the first noninstinctual attention” —also referred to as “the Transcendental Signifier” (TH 99/
139). Girard writes that “mimetic phenomena provide the common ground between animal and human society as well as the first
concrete means to differentiate the two, concrete in the sense that

all observable analogies and differences between the two types of
organization become intelligible” (J 35). What, then, is this mime-

sis? Girard argues that it cannot be adequately grasped in terms of
classical philosophical notions about teleological action guided by

108 - Models of Desire

the agent’s representation of a goal or plan. The animal that engages in primitive mimicry is not an agent of action in this sense.
Presumably, such an agent does not represent to itself the model’s
actions prior to the generation of its own mimetic response (otherwise, there would be a social representation serving as the condition of the origin of social interactions). Girard asserts that this
primary mimesis is a type of imitation that is “prior to gestures,
attitudes, manners, and everything to which mimesis is usually reduced when it is understood only as representation. This mode of
imitation operates with a quasi-osmotic immediacy necessarily betrayed and lost in all the dualities of the modern problematics of
desire, including the conscious and the unconscious” (DBB 89).

Elsewhere Girard writes that “all subsequent mimetic effects are
ultimately brought back to the radical simplicity of the primordial
mimetic interference. When any gesture of appropriation is imitated it simply means that two hands will reach for the same object
simultaneously: conflict cannot fail to result” (I 32).
Although Girard claims that this kind of mimetic rivalry is not
specifically human, he contends that in the human species, such

forms of conflict are no longer checked by an instinctual inhibition
of intraspecific murder and can lead to a “fight to the finish”: “an
increased mimetic drive corresponding to the enlarged human
brain must escalate mimetic rivalry beyond the point of no return”
(I 32—33). In the same context he goes on to claim that the “mechanism of symbolicity must be triggered by the murderous exasperation of mimetic rivalry.” He elaborates on this theme as
follows:
It was Plato who determined once and for all the cultural meaning of imitation, but this meaning is truncated, torn from the essential dimension
of acquisitive behavior, which is also the dimension of conflict. If the be-

havior of certain higher mammals, particularly the apes, seems to foreshadow human behavior, it does so almost exclusively, perhaps, because
the role of acquisitive mimesis is so important in their behavior, although
it is not as central as it is for human beings. If one ape observes another
reach for an object, it is immediately tempted to imitate the gesture. (TH
8/16)

109 - Mimesis and the Origin of Culture

As I have already pointed out, Girard describes the process of
hominization as involving, at least in part, an increase in or inten-

sification of the frequency and role of mimesis, including a mechanical form of mimesis whereby behavior is imitated in an automatic manner. How might this primary form of mimesis, which
may be referred to as “motor mimicry,” be more precisely defined?
As a first and purely preliminary approximation, we may suppose
that a few basic clauses are required, as follows:
“Motor Mimicry

Given organisms O),.. . , O,,, together in a single spatiotemporal
location; given as well a type of behavior, B, having particular
manifestations b, b’, b”, . . . , an episode of motor mimicry re-

quires that:
1. at t, O; perceives that O) , ,,b’s;

2. at t, B belongs to O,’s behavioral repertoire (O, has a disposition or capacity to perform tokens of type B); and
Shatter

O, b4s:

This general definition of motor mimicry will not serve, however, as a characterization of Girard’s notion of acquisitive mimesis. He remarks that the objects sought after are essential to acquisitive mimesis and that he cannot insist too much on their
importance in any adequate analysis of the phenomenon: “Our
point of departure is the object; we cannot stress this enough even
though no one understands it, apparently” (TH 16/27). There are
good reasons for this emphasis. For example, it allows the theorist
to get the mimetic process started without having recourse to the
imitative agent’s beliefs or representations (the “spectacle” of another desire, be it real or illusory one): acquisitive mimesis finds its
roots in very basic, biologically explicable dispositions that are already present, such as eating. Girard’s privileged example—that of
two apes reaching for the same banana— implies that acquisitive
mimesis is a matter of behavior that is based in the object to the

extent that the organism’s disposition to grasp and eat this object
arises from a basic and necessary motivational system in the ani-

110 - Models ofDesire

mal. Although it is wrong to adopt any simple depletion-repletion
model of animal motivation—even in the case of needs as basic as
hunger and organisms as lowly as the rat—there is strong evidence
for positing motivational systems that direct organisms toward
specific forms of “appropriative” or “acquisitive” behavior. Such
motivational systems as hunger and thirst effectively guide the animal in relation to types of objects in the environment; these objects serve as primary incentives insofar as the animal is motivated
to make or maintain contact with, to escape from or to avoid, these
objects. It would be misleading, however, to see these incentives

purely in terms of external objects defined by their intrinsic properties; rather, these objects should be understood as external stim-

uli that are contextualized by the nervous system of the organism,
a system that has its own particular history. The ingestion of beneficial substances, for example, is in part guided by the organism’s
previously favorable experiences with a particular type of object.
(For example, there may be some evidence that kittens raised by
rats refuse to kill them, even when hungry.)® Even so, it remains
valid to observe that given this history, and other conditions being
equal, certain motivational sequences are effectively set in motion
by the presence of incentive objects (e.g., foodstuffs), given the
satisfaction of certain conditions relative to internal states (e.g.,
energy depletion). This kind of system gives behavior a teleological orientation because a given incentive object helps to bring
about the motivational state and to define the direction of the behavior motivated by the state in question.”
Girard’s acquisitive mimesis, then, can be said to involve a

matching of behavior in instances where the kind of behavior in
question is generated by a basic motivational system of the sort
just discussed. Hunger stands as the paradigmatic example. Talk of
acquisitive mimesis could refer, then, to cases where the behavior

being matched or imitated is of a type corresponding to the animal’s basic needs or wants (e.g., there is a strong incentive for the
ingestion of this type of object). This manner of filling in the hypothesis has several consequences. First of all, there is no problem
of getting the interactional sequence going because the opening

111 - Mimesis and the Origin of Culture
move at t is a nonmimetic gesture of appropriation directly perceived by an observing organism. (There is no need to invoke, as in
the case of mimetic desire, a sequence that begins with one agent’s
belief about a model’s desire.) Grounding the sequence in basic
incentive objects that are identified by basic motivational systems
also plausibly fills in the second clause in our schematic definition
of motor mimicry (the behavior must be part of the imitating organism’s behavioral repertoire). Finally, the hypothesis in question
naturalizes the model. With the exception of cases of apes being
-manipulated by ethologists, there is something unrealistic about a
scenario in which a banana suddenly materializes between two
philosophical apes, who, finding their contemplation disturbed,
must decide what to do about the puzzling intrusion. Having
posed the problem in this manner, one wonders how it is possible
for Desire to begin; must not Desire be schon immer da in order to
get started? But it is not necessary to pose the problem in this manner at all. It is, on the contrary, highly likely that higher primates
and hominids will already be engaged, during significant portions
of their time, in diverse activities involving the pursuit of edible
objects, and this in each other’s presence. Not only is the basic situation described likely to arise but the agents already have an array
of behavioral dispositions.
Yet another stipulation needs to be made if we are to grasp the
specificity of the acquisitive mimesis isolated by Girard. As the
word “acquisitive” suggests, there must be something “selfish”
about the gestures belonging to this class of behavior that needs to
be specified: the banana is grasped in order to be held, kept, possessed, ingested. If the gesture happens to be opposed by another
grasping hand, the object is not simply parceled out or enjoyed in
common. By definition, the class of acquisitive behaviors includes
only those behaviors that have an implicit goal of appropriating
and maintaining exclusive possession or use of some object; one
could perhaps generalize this to include the sole exploitation or
occupation of a place or situation. Insofar as objective possibilities
are concerned, the objects sought after in such behavior might
very well be subject to division or to some form of cooperative use,

112 - Models of Desire

but the organism’s relation to them is such that these possibilities
are excluded from the implicit aims of acquisitive behavior. It is
crucial to understand that the class of behaviors in question here is
not defined or dictated by the intrinsic nature of any class of real
objects in the world. Objects that cannot possibly be shared by two
organisms at the same time do exist; for example, two people cannot both wear the same shoe at once (although one can imagine
them taking turns). But facts of this sort are not in any case what
determine the category of acquisitive behavior. Instead, this category is constituted by the nature of the agent’s relation to a state of
affairs in reality. To spell this out, we may stipulate that the implicit goal realized by any successful example of this kind of behavior is the occupation of a particular position in relation to some
thing or situation, without any other agent occupying this same
position at the same time.
In short, if we refer to the acquisitive type of behavior as ACQ
and to instances of it as acq, acq’, acq’’, . . . , we may propose the

following schematic formulation:
Basic Acquisitive Mimesis

Given organisms O,,. . . ,O,, together in a single spatiotemporal
location; given as well a type of acquisitive behavior engaged in by
them, ACQ, manifested in particular behaviors acq, acq’, acq”’,

. .

,a basic episode of acquisitive mimesis requires that:

1. at t, O, perceives that O),,, acq’s;

2. at t, O, has a disposition to perform tokens of ACQ, this
disposition being supported by a basic motivational system
in O,; and
3. att + 1, (ust after t) O, acq’’s.

Let us now turn to some of the claims that may be made for
Girard’s notion of a primitive acquisitive mimicry, focusing in particular on Girard’s theorizing about human violence and its role in
the origin of human culture.

113 - Mimesis and the Origin of Culture

Generating Violent Interaction

How does acquisitive mimesis generate the violent crises that are
essential elements in Girard’s genetic scenario and, in a somewhat
different form, in his analysis of a variety of cultural practices? In
the theory of motor mimicry just evoked, it is specified that the
perception of another organism’s behavior triggers preestablished
behavioral dispositions: no beliefs or representational processes
are required; the disposition is already in place; nothing is learned;
no decisions are involved. If this kind of “facilitation” is what is at
stake in Girard’s primitive mimesis, then this might imply that
Girard must posit an inherent, preexisting aggressive “instinct”
that tends to be “released” more and more frequently as the “instinctual checks” are “eroded” by an increased mimesis.’ But in
spite of his occasional use of these “safety-valve” metaphors and
“quantum of wantum” psychodynamic expressions, Girard rejects
this approach and explicitly states that it is mistaken to attribute
the increased tendency toward violence in mankind to the work of
some kind of instinct; on the contrary, the increase in violence is

“part of [7d ne fait qu’un avec| the growth of mimetic activity linked
to the increase in brain size” (TH 95/133).
Now, Girard’s approach to the genesis of violence among human beings differs interestingly from two typical positions on the
subject. The first attributes to the hominids a “desire to dominate”

or some sort of aggressive instinct. Here we refer to the many
pseudobiological versions of the will to power. The second typical
claim amounts to an attempt to generate violence, rivalry, and con-

flict out of an objective scarcity of the goods that are needed by the
organisms, this scarcity being the bread and butter of the discourse
of political economy in its classical and neoclassical forms.? A

lengthy critique of these two stances is unnecessary in the present
context. Suffice it to say in passing that the latter is contradicted by
the multiplicity of cases in which human conflict simply cannot
plausibly be linked to an objectively imposed scarcity, because no
such scarcity exists. The former approach is inadequate because it
is characterized by a dogmatic and imprecise manipulation of in-

114 - Models of Desire

stinct or drive constructs, usually supported by an unexamined
and self-serving exploitation of functionalist evolutionary pseudoexplanations.!° What needs to be explained is how the ape became
a killer; positing a gene and the killer ape’s supposed fitness does
not constitute such an explanation. But is Girard’s alternative explanation any more cogent?
Clearly, a number of questions arise at this point, for the mimetic theory’s manner of generating violence needs to be specified
in much greater detail. Girard is no doubt right in rejecting a
depth-psychodynamic theory of the origin of aggression, that is,
one that “explains” observed violence by positing an instinct or
drive, which is in turn “evidenced” by observed violence. The question remains, however, as to how the basic mimesis that Girard

posits is able to produce an overall increase in violence. What conditions must be in place for this to happen, or rather, what assumptions must be made for the theory to be plausible? It is crucial to
note, first of all, that Girard does not explain humankind’s tendency to engage in conflict simply in terms of an enhanced mimesis, but in terms of a more particular form of mimesis— acquisitive
mimesis. But is this explanation sufficient?
It should be clear that the basic variety of motor mimicry described above is too general to play the role required by Girard’s
hypothesis. As the term suggests, what the theory requires is a tendency to imitate “acquisitive” gestures or behavior. Several hypotheses may be identified: (1) that a generalized tendency toward

motor mimicry, as sketched above, leads to the automatic matching of behavior that happens to be acquisitive, just as it leads to the
matching of other kinds of behavior; (2) that there is an increasing
tendency for a selective matching of only the acquisitive behaviors
displayed by other organisms; (3) that the organism selectively
matches perceived instances of behavior from both the acquisitive
and nonacquisitive categories.
The first hypothesis should be ruled out, for it seems highly implausible that the mimetic organism imitates every behavior of
which it is capable whenever it perceives the enacting of such behavior in the environment. Such a claim is at odds with observed

115 - Mimesis and the Origin of Culture

cases of social facilitation and primate mimicry. It would seem to
follow that the behavioral system instantiating the mimetic disposition must include a selective device or mechanism of some sort
to serve as a discriminatory gate between the perception of behavior and the setting in motion of effective motivational states. The
idea that motor mimicry is in this manner selective should not be
taken to imply the operation of fairly sophisticated forms of cognition, that is, not only a basic perceptual linkage but some form of
interpretive categorization of what other creatures do. In this
. manner one might imagine that the hypothesis would be pushed
toward a rather different form of mimetic process than the “quasiosmotic” and immediate variety to which Girard refers. It may be
recalled that some fairly sophisticated discriminatory capacities
may be given functional explanations in terms of the nonrepresentational variety of cognition.!! Thus we will specify the necessary
assumption roughly as follows: the disposition to engage in motor
mimicry is a motivational subsystem that operates nonconsciously
in the organism’s selective responses to the perception of behavior
in the environment. The organism’s overall behavioral system includes a switching component that mediates between the perceptual states determined by stimulus inputs and the different motivational subsystems, so that the mimetic subsystem is at times
inhibited, at times activated. Clearly this is not a trivial assumption, and an adequate model must provide further specifications.
What of the remaining two hypotheses? The third hypothesis
clearly seems preferable insofar as (a) motor mimicry of nonacquisitive behavior is a widely observed fact and (4) we do not (yet)
have any theoretical motivation for postulating wholly separate
systems for the mimicry of different types of behavior. Apparently,
the second hypothesis’s only advantage 1s that it promises to generate quite directly the increase in acquisitive behavior that the overall theory requires, but it is not in fact clear that it is the only or the
best way to generate this increase. The third hypothesis, then,
seems best: a general disposition for motor mimicry engenders the
special case of acquisitive mimicry.
What must be dealt with in any case is the more general prob-

116 - Models of Desire

lem of what can and cannot be derived from the class of acquisitive
behaviors that, in one way or another, is promoted by the enhanced mimesis of the hominids. How does acquisitive mimicry
produce violence? It seems obvious that conflict, at least in some
loose sense of the term, is already inscribed within the situation of
acquisitive mimesis. By definition, an instance of acquisitive mimesis has an implicit goal of acquiring or maintaining exclusive
possession or use of some object or situation. When two organisms seek exclusive use of a single object, it is impossible for both
gestures to succeed, and hence there is a potential conflict between
them. It is clear that the disposition for acquisitive mimesis as I
have defined it leads to a situation where two hominids reach for
the same object, but why do they fight? Surely it is not the objective nature of the object that rules out the alternatives (compare
the various speculations that emphasize the importance of food
sharing among primates and the earliest hominids).!2 Why does
the convergence of behavior lead to conflict and not, for example,
to what ethologists call “scrambling,” a situation in which one of
the creatures takes possession of the object and flees with it? Also,
how is it that this same mimesis gives rise to a certain type of resolution of crises of violence within a hominid group? A more detailed account of this model of agency and interaction must be
provided.
A first approach to answering these questions runs as follows.
The progression to rivalry requires that both organisms persist in
their efforts to appropriate the object. Scrambling could be followed by pursuit, which could lead to a confrontation between
two parties strongly motivated to possess the object. At this point
behavior directly related to retaining or seizing the object might be
replaced by gestures aimed at discouraging or incapacitating the
opponent. Here we need to assume that certain forms of violent
behavior appropriate to both attack and defense are already part of
the animal’s behavioral system. That this kind of behavior should
occur in such a situation is wholly plausible. It is not adequate to
say that this kind of sequence is realized when the “instinctual
bonds” are loosened, which implies that some powerful violent

117 - Mimesis and the Origin of Culture

urge was already lurking in the breast of the beast. A more positive
characterization is required; for example, it could be argued that
the key element in the progression just sketched is the organisms’
persistence in wanting and seeking to acquire the disputed object.
The “strength” or intensity of the motivational state that incites
the organisms to want the object in the first place, along with the
appropriative nature of the want, leads to a situation in which the
organisms are drawn into a conflict.
It may be noted that in the scenario of acquisitive mimesis de_ veloped above, violent conflict does not appear to be the direct or
simple result of only an enhanced tendency to match the behavior
of models. There is nothing inherently aggressive or violent about
the hypothetical propensity for a generalized motor mimicry. It is
only in conjunction with the organisms’ tendency to engage in acquisitive behavior that the mimesis leads to situations of conflict,
and it is only the intensity and persistence of the impulse that leads
in turn to overt rivalry and violence. Thus a key feature of this
model is the hominids’ tendency to adopt acquisitive relations to
various objects in the environment. This tendency is said to operate independently of the propensity for motor mimicry. It is crucial to note that there is nonmimetic acquisitive behavior and that
not all matched behavior is acquisitive. A further stipulation is that
the organism’s acquisitive behavior is guided by basic motivational
systems productive of motivational states that are sufficiently

strong to engage the organisms in a persistent effort to overcome
obstacles in acquiring the incentive objects.
Now, the clauses I have been sketching in may not seem wholly
implausible, but are they sufficient to lead to the kind of effects
required in Girard’s generative hypothesis? A thought experiment
may help us to pose this question more precisely. Let us consider
what would happen in the following situation: While foraging,
two of our hominids happen to find a bunch of bananas. Both have
eaten bananas before and are hungry. In keeping with our little
scenario, one of them “appropriates” a banana. What does the
other one see? Before her stands the first ape, banana in hand; on
the ground lies the bunch of bananas. Now is the moment for our

118 - Models ofDesire

mimetic animal to experience the urge incited by the “quasi-osmotic” mimesis with which the theory equips her. So what does the
ape do? Clearly, she must reach for the banana held by her fellow
creature, which suggests that she must (a) ignore the bunch of
bananas on the ground, although she has seen this fellow take one
of them and could imitate that gesture; and (4) get embroiled in
some unpleasant business with a hungry and dangerous ape, an
ape that is, moreover, intent on keeping his banana. The inherent
preference for acquiring bananas, which is by our postulation
quite strong, is overcome, indeed interfered with. Does this imply
that the creature has an inherent preference for eating bananas that
other apes want to keep for themselves? But that seems absurd. In
relation to this example, it begins to look as if the tendency to imitate the other’s acquisitive gesture must arise from some kind of
motivational subsystem having a high degree of priority within
the overall motivational system of the animal; the key postulation
is that cases in which acquisitive and mimetic motivations converge are most likely to generate the prevailing motivational state.
The lesson of this thought experiment can be generalized: to
keep the particular nature of this aspect of Girard’s generative hypothesis clearly in view, it suffices to imagine the variety of fully
acquisitive behavior the mimetic system of motivation passes over
in favor of an imitative and conflictual variety. In a similar vein,
Girard himself has frequently evoked a kindergarten scene where
children fight to possess one toy singled out from among a wealth
of playthings, including ones identical to the toy being fought over
(TH 9/17). Yet it is important not to let these considerations lead
one to assume that the “object” is inessential in cases of acquisitive
mimesis: Hamlet’s Poles who fight over a plot of land not big
enough to bury them in belong to a different context of analysis.
In short, the specific effects produced by acquisitive mimesis are a
matter of emergent properties not produced independently by
either the system of generalized motor mimicry or the system of
acquisitive behavior. Instead, it is their combination that generates
the unique effects associated in the theory with la mimésis
appropriation.

119 - Mimesis and the Origin of Culture

Mimesis and the Crisis of Violence

Remaining still within the precultural context of analysis that is
the framework of Girard’s genetic anthropology, we must now
confront two questions: first, what is the nature of the “violent
crisis” generated by primitive mimesis? and second, how does mimesis resolve this crisis? Girard’s critics have always been puzzled
about how the theory explains the transition from a situation
where apes struggle over a banana to one where a whole band of
hominids is engaged in a convulsive struggle, a struggle myste~ riously resolved when the group unanimously murders one of its
own members. To some readers it may all seem like a surrealist’s
rewriting of Totem and Taboo. The skeptic is indeed entitled to a
more detailed account, particularly when it is an unspecified mimetic fluid that seems to allow the argument to slide from step to
step. André Orléan has provided an elegant formal model of how a
generalized tendency to imitate can, under certain conditions, lead
to a situation where the members of the group all choose the same
object. But it is not obvious at first glance that the conditions specified in this model, which deals with the stock market, are satisfied

in any plausible versions of the original anthropological scene involving hominids committing a primordial murder (and Orléan
makes no such claim).}8
In the case of the original murder, an initial situation in which
the various members of the group are engaged in typical activities,
including in some cases appropriative behavior, somehow must
give way to a situation in which aggression has become the predominant or even the sole impulse. In other words, there must be
what Girard calls a “murderous exasperation of mimetic rivalry”
(I 33). This generalized rivalry must then crystallize into the form
of the many and the one, where a group stands united in opposition to a single party. While the kind of acquisitive mimesis already defined seems quite likely to generate episodes of dyadic aggression, what has not been rigorously demonstrated is how
motor mimicry is related to the larger interactional patterns of aggression that figure in the theory. The kind of violent crisis that

120 - Models of Desire

Girard evokes could hardly be a matter of a band of hominids all
trying to possess a single banana and getting in a brawl over it as a
result; rather, what must be postulated is a tendency to match violent or aggressive behavior when it is enacted by a model, and this
independently of any context of acquisitive behavior based on
basic needs. Only then would talk of “spreading” and “contagious”
violence make sense.
Clearly, the Girardian genetic scenario requires both a “crisis of
violence” and its “violent resolution” in the unanimous, collective

murder, with mimesis serving as the connecting link. Yet before
we examine some different ways of conceptualizing the precise operation of this link, it may be useful to examine the nature of the
two situations that it is supposed to connect. The meaning of the
“resolution of the crisis in violent unanimity” 1s pretty clear: all, or
almost all, of the members of the group partake in the collective
murder of a member of the group. But what is the meaning of the
notion of “crisis of violence” that describes the stage just prior to
this resolution? Globally, it may make some sense to speak of a
“crisis” of violence, but locally, it cannot literally be the case that
each and every ape is violently attacking all of the other apes at
once.!4 It may be better to assume that “crisis” refers to a situation
in which all or most of the members of the group are aroused and
are experiencing some violent form of anger or rage, a situation in
which there has been a lot of fighting and the majority of the members have been, and are still, involved in attack, defense, and coun-

terattack, so that the overall situation is one of insecurity. This is
how “generalized violence” may roughly be understood. More
precise sequences of behavior and interaction can be imagined. It
could be a matter of positing a number of dyadic struggles going
on at once. Or perhaps individual hominids charge through the
group, attacking now one, now another, in a series of disconnected assaults that become more and more frequent.
If this model is to be developed, the role of mimesis in the genesis and resolution of the crisis needs to be made more precise.
What, for example, is the place of mimesis in the initial situation

and in the outburst and dissemination of violence? Perhaps we
need only stipulate that among the kinds of behavior that may be

121 - Mimesis and the Origin of Culture
imitated, there are various types of aggressive or violent gestures. I
have already argued that it is not necessary to posit an abstract will
to dominate or an aggressive drive in order to make an eruption of
violence plausible. Let us assume, then, that it is not wholly un-

likely that acquisitive mimesis could lead to situations in which
there is some fighting in the hominid group. I think that we also
want to assume that not many attacks would spontaneously occur
at the very same moment in a peaceful group of hominids. It seems
plausible to assume that we are talking about a relatively small
group (one hesitates to speculate on precise figures), but in any
~ case, it would be wild to imagine that all of a sudden a majority of
the members of the group spontaneously would engage in violent
attacks on each other. If one or a small number of violent incidents
is to be disseminated and to lead to a generalized crisis, some
mechanism must bring this about. This role is to be played by mimesis in any Girardian model, but it is important to note that insofar as there is aggressive behavior to be imitated, it is initially isolated; it also seems most realistic to say that this behavior would be
engaged in by a small subsection of the group.

So far, we have the group together, and some fighting is taking
place. For the scenario to work, this fighting has to be generalized
throughout the group by means of mimesis. Now, what does motor mimicry of violent behavior entail in terms of the others’ next
behaviors? In other words, how do we get from here to the crisis?

Several distinct scenarios may be imagined. One would follow Orléan’s model. If our simian scenario is to follow the logic of the
model proposed by Orléan in relation to the stock market, we have

to specify something like the following: at t, each animal is either
fighting with another one or doing something else (e.g., looking
on, foraging for food, etc.); at t + 1, each animal imitates one of
these behaviors; and so on. Now, as I argued above, it seems most
reasonable to assume that in the initial situation, most members of

the group are displaying pacific behavior at any given moment.
Given the probabilistic setup of Orléan’s model, it follows that

most of the time the group as a whole would imitate some form of
peaceful behavior. It is true that given the same setup, there would
also be some cases of a convergence on the initial violent behavior,

122,» Models of Desire

so that crises would also be generated. Now, it may be that this
latter consideration is satisfactory: the model should not generate
the crisis of violence all or even most of the time; what matters is

that the scenario be realized and give rise to its particular effects.
The scenario just proposed is still not very precise, for we have

not yet said what it means for an animal to adopt, at t + 1, the same
“strategy” or behavior that another animal manifested at t. In other
words, what does it mean to imitate an observed instance of aggression? In the absence of a more precise and clearer sense of this
expression, all talk about the link between aggression and mimesis
is in danger of being far too ambiguous. But here we encounter a
basic and unavoidable problem, namely, that it is difficult to provide anything like a precise definition of “aggression” or “violence.”!5 These notions are notoriously diffuse. It may be possible
to define “aggression” as behavior causing physical injury to another organism, yet such a definition hardly corresponds to the
scope of our basic intuitions about the problem of aggression.
Three considerations are typically used to drive this point home.
First, an instance of behavior that is perceived as having been oriented toward causing physical harm but failed to achieve this goal
will still be classified as a serious instance of aggression. Second,
some behavior that actually causes physical injury is not classified
by either party as an instance of aggression. And third, many forms
of nonphysical harm are frequently recognized as instances of aggression. All three of these remarks underscore the necessity of
moving away from a simplistic approach to the problem. This
means that the analysis must shift to a level of description involving references to the agents’ explicit or implicit intents, as well as
to such issues as the agents’ judgments concerning the morality of
the action.
But in the context of Girard’s precultural crisis, such fine distinctions must be held to a minimum. As I suggested above, what
is at stake is an initial situation in which, as a result of acquisitive
mimesis, at least two organisms have effectively engaged in physical conflict or fighting with each other, causing at the very least
violent affective states of fright and rage and perhaps some physical injury. Suppose now that another animal has observed this

123 - Mimesis and the Origin of Culture
course of events. What does it mean to say that this observer imitates the violence? To facilitate the discussion, let us assume that A,
is an animal that physically fought with another animal, A,, at t,
and A; is an onlooker at t. What does A; do at t + 1] should it turn
out that A3 behaves imitatively? The following analysis will show
that even this very simple situation contains a number of possibilities that are different in important respects.
First of all, the model of A;’s imitative behavior must be determined. Does A; imitate A), A), or both of them? Two hypotheses,
Hj, and H,, can be defined by stipulating that either A, or A, was

the aggressor in the fight. Given this distinction between the aggressor and the target of the aggression, we can develop a first hypothesis, H), that stipulates that the imitator takes the aggressor as
model and attacks the same animal that the aggressor just attacked.
Another hypothesis, H), inverts the former one: the hominids always imitate the defender and rush in to fight against the aggressor. A third hypothesis, H3, states that neither A; nor A; was

distinguished as the aggressor and allows that A; takes either one
as model with roughly equal probability, the orientation being
“unmotivated.” Following H;3, to say that A; imitates the observed
violence is simply to say that A; attacks either one of the two parties involved, taking the other one as model. Still another hypothesis, H,, runs as follows: the matching of A,’s or A,’s behavior

could simply mean that A, in turn engages in “similar” activity, an
activity that is “the same” as the first behavior only in a general
sense. What is imitated is the “type” of behavior, “fighting,” but
the particular partner in this relation is not necessarily the same:
A; does not necessarily attack A, or A; att + 1, but A; does attack

someone at t + 1. We could generate another hypothesis, H;, by
stipulating that the animal cannot attack either of the two animals
whose violent behavior was just observed: if at t + 1, A; imitates
A, or A,, then A; can attack any animal except A, or A,. (Note that

all of these hypotheses assume unrealistically that the animals’ potential targets of aggressivity are not limited by such categorizations as physical size, sex, age, or memory of prior encounters with
the aggressor.)
Let us now discuss these different hypotheses in terms of their

124 - Models of Desire

potential to generate the crisis of generalized rivalry and its resolution. H, is a scenario that has the advantage of leading very quickly
to unanimity. Here is how. Suppose A, and A, fight. Let A, be the
aggressor. Imitating this first behavior, A3, Ay, .. . , A,, each attack A,; imitating A3, Ay,..., A,, others still in turn attack the

hapless A), and so on. We suddenly get the violent unanimity that
is so important to a theory of social structure based on the pattern
of the many and the one. Yet there are problems with this model.
First, the solution is reached without passing through a stage of
“generalized crisis” or widespread violent disorder; instead, it

looks like a hunting party in which one of the hunters was mistaken for the prey. Second, this is a model of social life where violent aggression is guaranteed to be a successful strategy within the
group and where the target of any act of aggression is made the
object of collective violence. Such a society could quickly take the
form of a game of musical chairs, where the number of players

dwindles with each round! H, is not uninteresting, but it clearly
does not satisfy the Girardian criteria. H, is even less successful in
this regard. Members of the group tend to rush to the aid of victims of an attack and violently pursue the aggressor. The result
could well be a collective murder, but again, no generalized crisis
precedes it, so it is disqualified.
What pattern would H; follow should it happen to lead to the
generalized crisis and its resolution? The imitators can attack either
one of the parties in a fight, and we assume that they do so with
roughly equal probability. Thus, for every dyadic fight at t, there is

a subgroup at t + 1 in which several imitators attack each of the
two parties of the initial dyad. Where does this lead? At t + 2,
some of the other formerly pacific animals join in, and the odds are
that they will attack A, or A,—or one of the initial parties in another dyad at t. So in this model we appear to have a tendency
toward the formation of several coalitions, each having its own
victim. The number of these coalitions roughly depends on the
number of dyadic fights that were going on at t or shortly thereafter. Now, if the tendency to imitate any one of the fighting parties
is roughly equal, it is true that at t + 2 the victims of the coalitions
formed at t + 1 will get some reinforcements; for example, some

125 - Mimesis and the Origin of Culture
of the bystanders will join A, in fighting one of his or her opponents. But in the long run the evolution of the violent scenarios
would be toward the formation of several distinct scapegoat parties. What about H,? This hypothesis means roughly that the animal has a disposition to behave following the schema “fight with
somebody” and that mimicry of a model can set an instance of this
general kind of behavior in motion. Hypothesis H; is a more extreme version: the imitator is incited to engage in violence by the
sight of a model but never attacks the same animal that the model
just fought with. In both H, and Hs, whether the model is an aggressor or a defender does not matter, since the direction of the

matching behavior is not determined by that of the model.
Both H, and H; would seem to satisfy the conditions of a

model in which mimesis leads a group to converge on the same
strategy. Would they not lead to cases where more and more animals engaged in fighting? I will focus on H, because H; seems

strange in its exclusion of a literal form of imitative matching.
Now, according to Hy, when A; has seen A, and A, fight, A; goes

into a kind of rage and more or less randomly launches him- or
herself at someone else. And if the “more or less random” selection
of targets is indeed the case, then there can be (a) generalization of
violence, so that all or most of the members get involved in some
fighting; and (4) an eventual convergence on one target. This is
precisely what the hypothesis requires, and thus H, is the most
promising hypothesis for filling in Girard’s scenario. But what are
its assumptions?
The essential difference between H; and Hy, involves what is

meant by matching the behavior of an animal that 1s fighting. One
form of matching is more literal than the other, for it involves
fighting with the same organism that the model fought with. The
other type of matching is more abstractive and schematic: what
the imitator takes from the model is the general category of behavior—fighting. Which of these two interpretations of the relationship between motor mimicry and aggressive behavior should
be given prominence in the model? Girard tends to write as if he
assumed the more “generalized” form of mimesis, that implied in
H,, were operative. But it should be noted that such an assump-

126 + Models of Desire
tion places a certain strain on the conception of a motor mimicry
with its “quasi-osmotic immediacy.” How does it work? In a case
of motor mimicry, the organism does not perceive an abstract
event; what it observes is a particular model’s particular assault on
a particular creature. That this observation could result in a
motivation to attack an unspecified someone is anything but farfetched, but it does imply a certain transformational process
within the imitative agent, whose cognitive/motivational system
in some sense abstracts from the case at hand, singles out another
target, and engages, right away, in a new bit of violence. The
motivational sequence’s relation to particular incentives and observations is drastically loosened here, and we seem to be positing
something like a general disposition toward a type of behavior
vaguely defined as “behaving violently impulsively” or “attacking
at the sight of aggression.” The point, once more, is not that this is
psychologically implausible or incoherent; what could seem incoherent is requiring that this be the effect of our most basic variety of motor mimicry. On the other hand, that the mimetic
motivational state should engage a basic behavioral schema, perhaps in conjunction with strong affective states (violent arousal,
anger, etc.), that propels the animal headlong into a fight is not
really an exotic hypothesis.
It should be recalled that the conflict and aggression generated
by appropriative or acquisitive mimesis is object-related and involves animals fighting over something they wish to appropriate.
A more rapid and more certain spread of violence throughout the
group would be generated by a different mimetic tendency. For
example, when violence is perceived, it becomes a privileged object
of imitation. The innocent bystanders will be likely to imitate the
violence and join in the fray, even though this mimesis is not supported by any basic appropriative form of behavior. Perhaps this is
what Girard has in mind in the following passage:
If the object is excluded there can no longer be any acquisitive mimesis as
we have defined it. There is no longer any support for mimesis but the
antagonists themselves. What will occur at the heart of the crisis will
therefore be the mimetic substitution of antagonists.

127 - Mimesis and the Origin of Culture
If acquisitive mimesis divides by leading two or more individuals to converge on one and the same object with a view to appropriating it, conflictual mimesis [la mimeésis de Pantagoniste] will inevitably unify by leading
two or more individuals to converge on one and the same adversary that
all wish to strike down. (TH 26/41, Girard’s emphases)

It would seem that we need to posit the existence of another
subcategory of mimesis alongside acquisitive mimesis: “conflictual” or “antagonistic” mimesis. Oddly, Girard takes this step in
the passage cited, yet he rarely employs the new term in other contexts. Nor does he really address himself to the implications that
this new concept has for his attempt to ground hominid rivalry
and the violent crisis “in the object” (an apparently misunderstood
emphasis stridently asserted only a few pages earlier). It could be
argued that if it was necessary to introduce conflictual mimesis
into the scenario, this was because the patterns of an object-oriented acquisitive mimesis cannot alone generate a crisis of generalized violence within a hominid group, and then its resolution in

a collective murder. The generalized crisis of violence cannot be a
matter of isolated dyads engaged in a reciprocal struggle fueled by
imitation; rather, the link between imitation and aggression must
be more plastic and more volatile. In order for Girard’s mechanism
to work, the agents’ dispositions to behave violently must include
a crucial element of substitution allowing the orientation or target
of the imitated aggression to be transformed, first, to produce a
generalized crisis that no longer has anything to do with appropriation, and second, to allow the agents’ rage to focalize on a substitute victim. Such a hypothesis is coherent, but whether it is empirically correct (or could ever be warranted in such terms) is quite
another matter.

Durkheim and Girard

It may be useful in this context to compare an aspect of Girard’s
thought to Emile Durkheim’s sociology, which Girard has called a
“precious legacy.”!6 Girard’s analysis of the cognitive and motiva-

128 - Models of Desire

tional dimensions of scapegoating is analogous to, but differs significantly from, Durkheim’s perspective on religion. Durkheim
contends that religious beliefs in sacred entities all involve a fundamental misrepresentation (une méconnatssance) of the social group
or totality. In gatherings where individuals experience the powerful emotions of “social effervescence,” it is somehow impossible
for them to believe that such an exaltation could have its source in
the purely profane fact of a social occasion, and hence the members
of the group project that force outside of themselves, identifying
it as the manifestation of some sacred entity. The social totality
is systematically misrepresented, then, and the social system’s
emergent properties are mistaken for transcendent and supernatural powers. Although the concept of méconnaussance is a fundamental part of Durkheim’s analysis of religion and society, he provides only a feeble account of the causes of this systematic error,
basically proposing that the members of the group cannot correctly understand the powerful emotions that they feel because the
social action is “too circuitous and obscure” to grasp.!”7 Durkheim
is not committed, as is sometimes claimed, to the idea that religious

ideology is a necessary consequence of some basic fact of human
psychology, for such an assumption cannot be squared with his
obvious belief that it is possible for some people to understand that
the divinity is but a transfigured image of society. Durkheim also
believed that the historical process of enlightenment was contributing to the demystification of the religious error. Yet he offers no
systematic and plausible account of the social and psychological
conditions that produce either the error or its demystification.
As I have already suggested, Girard also works with a notion of
méconnaissance, but it is not the same as Durkheim’s. What Girard’s

mimetic agents misrepresent is not the “force” that the social totality exercises on the individual but the actions, causal powers, and

identities of mediators and scapegoats. The archetypal supernatural error 1s an exaggeration of some human individual’s ability
to cause a generalized social crisis, and this basic error is motivated
by a perception that the group’s violence against this individual
indeed puts an end to the crisis. Like Durkheim, Girard must deal

with the question of the source of this kind of error; and his claim

129 - Mimesis and the Origin of Culture

that the error is not universal calls for an explanation of the conditions under which it has been understood.
Let us start with Girard’s views on the origin of the sacrificial
error. Girard’s explanation of the genesis and persistence of that
error is couched, we have seen, in terms of a theory of hominid

mimesis. A conjunction of motor, acquisitive, and conflictual
mimicry produced the configuration of crisis and resolution outlined above, the result being an unprecedented event accompanied
by an unprecedented type of awareness—the sacrificial misrepresentation. But there is a problem here. How can a primitive and

mechanical form of mimesis produce such prodigious cognitive effects as a false belief in the supernatural powers of a particular entity who 1s no longer recognized even by its fellows as a member of
the same species? What Girard calls the first noninstinctual state of
awareness comes on the heels of a crisis having purely mechanical
determinations, those of acquisitive and conflictual mimesis; yet
this crisis and its resolution are said to produce rather prodigious
representational effects insofar as they lead to the group’s misrepresentation of the victim, to its belief in his or her “guilt,” and

to an amazed recognition (reconnaissance émerveillée) of the peace

following the act of collective murder. To the extent that one
stresses the nonrepresentational and nonintentional aspects of the

primordial mimesis, a mechanical theory of hominid agency 1s embraced; yet this analysis is conjoined to a description, in “high”
