cognitive language, of the results of the scapegoat mechanism,
results amounting to an ensemble of beliefs, attitudes, symbolic
practices, and actions that are at once cultural and representational. But if the kinds of representational capacities that can be
associated with human culture are in place, why would the sacrificial error be inevitable? Why are the initial beliefs about the victim
always marvelous and erroneous? We must remember that only in
later situations, after the emergence of culture, can a belief in the

victim’s guilt actually motivate a unanimous turning of violence
against one party: no such beliefs are at work in a unanimity generated by a nonrepresentational mimesis. Beliefs in the victim’s
responsibility come later as a representational effect of this more
mechanical form of mimesis.

130 + Models of Desire
Here is Girard’s dilemma. On the one hand, it would seem that

creatures driven by fairly primitive drives and by mimetic facilitations of these drives suddenly acquire higher cognitive capacities
(beliefs, memory, etc.). On the other, it would seem that the first

noninstinctual attention must have been preceded by some fairly

complicated representational capacities. The former option entails
an inexplicable rupture, but the latter makes the creatures’ system-

atic cognitive error a mystery. Girard’s lengthiest explicit statement
on this issue, in which he responds to the question whether the
first victims were already sacrificial victims, reads as follows:
To the extent that the new type of attention is awakened, the victim will be
imbued with the emotions provoked by the crisis and its resolution. The
powerful experience crystallizes around the victim. As weak as it might
be, the “consciousness” the participants have of the victim 1s linked structurally to the prodigious effects produced by its passage from life to death,
by the spectacular and liberating reversal that has occurred at that instant.
The double transference will determine the only possible meaning to take
shape under the circumstances, and this will constitute the sacred and confer total responsibility for the event on to the victim. It is necessary to
conceive of stages, however, which were perhaps the longest in all human
history, in which the signifying effects have still not truly taken shape.
One would have to answer your question by saying that once the victim
has appeared, however dimly, the process leading to the sacred has begun,

although concepts and representations are not yet part of it.
There is no need to assume that the mechanism of awakening attention
works right away; one can imagine that for a considerable period it produced nothing at all, or next to nothing. Nonetheless, the most rudimentary signifying effects result from the necessity of controlling excessive
mimesis; as soon as we grant that these effects can be in the slightest degree cumulative, we will have recognized them as forerunners of forms of
human culture. (TH 100/140)

The passage suggests that we cannot plausibly postulate a sudden leap from a purely mechanical agency (a quasi-osmotic copying of behavior), to the fully fledged cultural significations of the
sacred. Girard does not want a model based on a mysterious “rup-

ture.” Nor does he want a gradualist model where only continuity

131 - Mimesis and the Origin of Culture

is stressed. Yet the passage seems to move in the latter direction.
When does the hominid ever arrive at the sacred? When do concepts, representations, and symbolic misrepresentations finally follow on an act of collective murder? It is not clear how reference to
“a considerable period” matters, given that the murders that finally
do generate the representational meanings and concepts of the sacred must also be the result of the nonrepresentational mimesis if
the latter is to be truly “originary,” that is, if the anthropology is to
remain “rooted in the animal.” The only rupture for Girard is the
collective murder, which he finds missing in those animal rituals
that display the same mechanisms that, when carried one step
further, produce the generative events. Thus, the creature that is

driven to participate in a collective murder without representational guidance must also be the creature who will experience the
significations of the earliest sacred representations. But how is this
possible?
One way to respond to this question is to point to a concurrent
development of representational capacities alongside the increase
in more mechanical forms of mimesis. On its own, the theory of an
externally prompted and rather mechanical mimesis offers an incomplete explanation of complex beliefs and delayed, symbolic

forms of modeling because different processes are at work in
them.!8 In other words, the increase in hominid brain size may
very well have resulted in an increase in acquisitive mimesis, but
not in it alone, if this increase is to have produced organisms capable of sacred beliefs as well as actions motivated by these beliefs.
The error would be to think that Girard’s stress on the increase in
mimesis must be taken as an exhaustive characterization of the impact of the increase in brain size on the hominids in question—as
if the system of motivation determined by mimesis were the only
behavioral system in the organism.!? As the tendency toward mimesis in certain types of situations increases, an increased capacity
for simulation or modeling of events in the environment emerges.
What we need to imagine is a creature who is in many ways
already very intelligent and inventive. This is a creature whose interaction with the physical environment does not comprise a very
limited repertory of motor responses generated by a purely me-

132 - Models of Desire

chanical form of agency. As Bandura puts it, “A theory that denies
that thoughts can regulate actions does not lend itself readily to the
explanation of complex human behavior.”?° The hominid in question, then, already has well-developed capacities for memory, abstraction, forethought, and intentional and purposive action. It

probably has a tool-making capacity as well as a complex ability to
orient itself in its natural environment, to undertake prolonged activities requiring planning and memory. There is evidence, for example, that Homo erectus (Lower Pleistocene, one million years

ago) transported raw materials for stone axes from sites where they
were available to the base camps where they would be used, the
distances ranging up to sixty kilometers. Gowlett infers from these
data that these creatures must have had “mental maps” of fairly
extensive geographical areas, as well as a capacity for planning and
foresight over at least a day or more.?! Such capacities outstrip a
stimulus-driven form of mechanical agency. Yet we may at the
same time imagine this creature as being prone to what may appear to us as a form of motivational rigidity in certain of its interactions with its fellow creatures; the rigid patterns that Girard attributes to acquisitive and conflictual mimesis at times totally
govern its interaction with its fellows. One way to put this would
be to say that what comes last is a capacity for higher-level social
representations, such as beliefs about the nature of the relations in
which the organism is engaged, and of course a reflexive concept of
self and a representation of the group as a whole. After all, the
most intelligent creatures, the inventors of extremely sophisticated
representational systems, frequently rely, in a highly unreflective
manner, on very basic and repetitive patterns of social interaction
(examples would be the rigid and automatic nature of certain fairly
common forms of joking behavior or the interactions prompted
by such emotions as anger). Why should the earliest hominids not
also combine a complex, instrumental knowledge of features of
the environment with a social order that is in some ways highly
rigid and involves little self-knowledge? Would we not in this regard often resemble them? I recommend, then, a modified ap-

proach to the development of cognitive capacities in the process of
hominization, namely, one that does not view the higher cognitive

133 + Mimesis and the Origin of Culture

faculties as being generated or determined by primitive and mechanical forms of motivational mimesis. Instead, behavior emerges
from the complex interplay of different cognitive, motivational,
and affective subsystems.
These remarks have important implications for Girard’s ap-

proach to the second of Durkheim’s two problems, namely, that of
explaining how any human being could ever escape from the kind
of méconnaissance that is fatally produced by the mimetic mechanism. Girard does not believe in a deus ex machina: the mimetic
machine cannot produce a genuine and comprehensive knowledge
of its own workings. Sacrificial cultures could never arrive at a lucid self-reflection without the influence, from without, of a truly

divine revelation, because the system of mimetic interaction functions as a closed system: “No system of thought can truly think the
thought that is capable of destroying it” (BE 289). The revelation
of the innocence of the scapegoat transcends the purely human and
social domain, for it is a divine being—the Christ—who announces, in word and deed, the falsehood of the archaic form of

the sacred. Girard does not claim that myth and ritual were effectively demystified from that moment on, and he admits that Christendom has in many ways functioned as a sacrificial institution—
beginning with a sacrificial misinterpretation of Christ’s murder.
But he claims that in principle the mythical illusions were debunked by Christ’s message, which has continued to exercise a
“subterranean influence” in spite of the sacrificial distortions:
“Men did not stop hunting witches because they invented science;
they invented science because they stopped hunting witches. The
scientific spirit, like the spirit of economic enterprise, is a by-product of the deep subterranean influence that has been exercised by
the evangelical text” (BE 285).
Girard seems to be arguing that his very strong hypothesis
about divine intervention in history is in part warranted because it
is the only way to explain how such a tremendous historical transformation could have been possible. Given the closed and selfreproducing system of mimesis, 7 follows that the cause of the change
had to come from outside the system. But at least two powerful
objections to this argument must be entertained: (1) the mimetic

134 - Models of Desire

system of thought and behavior was not in fact a closed system
incapable of generating significant changes in belief and social apperception; and (2) the historical transformation of human mimesis has not been as radical as Girard contends. Objection 1 may be
supported, for example, by noting that the fundamental méconnasssance of scapegoating was from the start only a contingent fact
about human mimesis, even if it was highly prevalent. It certainly
has not been established that such an error was a universal feature
of human cognition until the advent of Jesus Christ.?* Objection 2
may be supported by the observation that many instances of the
very same méconnaissance can be identified in the contemporary
social world, beginning with the economic institutions that are
supposed to be a by-product of the Christian revelation.?3 In
short, mimesis never determined the sacred as a universal and

closed system of belief, and no extra-human cause was required to
make it possible for some people to recognize some scapegoats as
scapegoats.
To sum up, I would like to dissociate the recognition of important, recurrent patterns of mimetic motivation and interaction
from the idea that human thought and history have been driven by
a closed and binding mimetic mechanism. I do not think that we
require theological posits to explain the complexity, openness, and
indeterminacy of human evolution and history. In the next chapter, I further develop my argument concerning the explanatory
status of mimetic patterns, focusing in particular on claims about
the determination of cycles of violent retribution.

Chapter Five

Desire, Violence,
and Revenge

In the foregoing chapters I have sought to elucidate and develop
some of the concepts that may be associated with Girard’s analyses
of mimesis. In this final chapter I shall take up in more detail what
is perhaps one of Girard’s most significant and controversial
claims, namely, that mimetic desire is the source of at least the
largest part of violent and aggressive forms of interaction. Although chapter 4 includes a discussion of some of Girard’s hypotheses about the links between acquisitive and conflictual mimesis in
precultural settings, the following analysis will focus on the relations between mimetic desire and violence within cultural contexts.
Girard has on occasion asserted quite explicitly that mimetic desire necessarily involves rivalry and conflict. Thus we read that
“mimesis coupled with desire leads automatically to conflict” (VS
146/205). Girard also states that “mimetism is a source of continual conflict. By making one man’s desire into a replica of another man’s desire, it invariably leads to rivalry; and rivalry in turn
transforms desire into violence” (VS 169/235). And elsewhere he
makes the same point: “To imitate the desires of someone else is to

turn this someone else into a rival as well as a model. From the
convergence of two or more desires on the same object, conflict
must necessarily arise” (DBB 140).
These very general assertions are contradicted, however, by the
possibility of cases in which an agent imitates a model’s desire
135

136 - Models ofDesire

without there being the slightest element of rivalry or conflict between them. As I argued in chapter 2, a general model of mimetic
desire embraces both “imitative” and “emulative” types of desire,
or in other words, both external and internal forms of mediation.

Only the latter is essentially conflictual. The category of nonconflictual mimetic desire is constituted by those situations where
two or more agents’ desires are of a certain sort, namely, those sit-

uations in relation to which the satisfaction of one agent’s desire
does not exclude the simultaneous success of the other’s desire.
Two subcategories of nonconflictual, imitative desiring may be
distinguished: on the one hand, cases where two or more desires

may be successfully satisfied by means of a cooperative venture; on
the other hand, instances that we have described as hierarchical.

Here it is the desiring agent’s beliefs about the model, and about
the agent’s own relation to the model, that preclude any form of
emulative desire in relation to that model. Moreover, in a hier-

archical mimetic relation, although there is a difference between
the two agents’ desires, the objects sought by them partake of a
single schema in which the agents take hierarchically different
roles.
The fact is that Girard recognizes the existence of nonconflictual instances of mimetic desire in his discussion of external mediation, which he explicitly characterizes in terms of the impossibility
of there being any rivalry between model and imitator. Insofar as
external mediation is indeed a form of mimetic desire, it follows

that not all mimetic desire “leads necessarily” to conflict. How,
then, should we understand Girard’s statements to the contrary?
The intimate link that Girard insists exists between mimetic desire
and rivalry should perhaps be understood as a state of affairs holding within a more restricted context. In this regard it is significant
that one of his most insistent statements about the competitive nature of mimetic desire figures in a passage that assigns the entirety
of desire to a particular sociohistorical domain:
Desire . . . corresponds not so much to mimetic crises as they occur in
primitive societies but to something at once similar and very different,
which is linked to the lasting enfeeblement of founding violence within

137

- Desire, Violence, and Revenge

our own world. The Judeo-Christian texts have produced a disintegration, whose effect has, however, been slowed down and moderated by the
churches’ sacrificial reading. . . .

Desire is what happens to human relationships when there is no longer
any resolution through the victim, and consequently no form of polarization that is genuinely unanimous and can trigger such a resolution. But
human relationships are mimetic none the less. We shall be able to discover, beneath the ‘underground? (in the Dostoevskyan sense) and always
deceptive form of individual symptoms, the dynamic style of the sacrificial
crisis. In this instance, however, there can be no ritualistic or victimary
resolution, and, if and when it becomes acute, the crisis ensues— what we

call psychosis.
Desire is the mimetic crisis in itself, it is the acute mimetic rivalry with
the other that occurs in all the circumstances we call ‘private’, ranging
from eroticism to professional or intellectual ambition. (TH 288/407—8)

The passage suggests that Girard’s analysis of mimetic desire, as
well as his claims about its intimate link to crisis, conflict, and psychopathology, rest on any number of broad anthropological and
historical theses. Desire is said to be a phenomenon constituted at
a particular moment of human history, and it is clear from Girard’s
terminology that the specification of this cultural moment requires
reference to the tenets of Girard’s other hypotheses. For example,
desire is analyzed in reference to a particular stage in the dissolution of a prior cultural order that was organized by cycles of crises
and by their resolution through victimization. Thus it could seem
that if we do not already assume along with Girard that the development of cultural life in modern civilization is essentially a story
of the modalities of the decline of the sacrificial institutions of
archaic cultures, then we will not be able to approach the phenom-

ena of desire and psychopathology within the right context.
Such a conclusion would be necessary if our goal here were
merely to interpret Girard faithfully; it would also be necessary in
that case to try to flesh out his story about the subterranean influence of the evangelical text, which is said to be responsible for undermining the sacrificial institutions in spite of the churches’ tendency to give a sacrificial reading of Christ’s message. It would be

138 - Models ofDesire

necessary as well to say how this undermining is conjoined with
the characteristically modern appearance of “underground” forms
of mimetic crisis.1 But my goal here is not simply to interpret
Girard faithfully; rather, I want to see how certain aspects of his
intuitions about mimesis can be reconstructed in a way that makes
them logically independent in relation to certain of his substantive
claims about the overarching dynamics of History and Revelation.
Thus I do not restrict the application of the term “desire” to any
particular period of human history, and I include in the model of
mimetic desire the assumption that this motivational state could
meaningfully be attributed to the members of archaic societies living long before the emergence of Christianity. Moreover, I do not
assume that all forms of mimetic desire that involve external mediation are necessarily culpable because they occur within the context of a “violent, sacrificial culture.” Here I have in mind the idea

that external mediation is not a solution to the problem of mimetic
violence because its condition of possibility is scapegoating. I am
certainly in agreement with the idea that there is too much violence and injustice in the present world order, but I am not sure
that this proposition entails the notion that all forms of external
mediation are suspect. I think, on the contrary, that it is a good
idea to hold open the possibility that some of the beliefs that can
motivate a nonemulative form of imitation are neither delusory
nor reprehensible. For example, it would be reductive to castigate
the broad range of interactions and values that one may associate
with “education” because they are necessarily forms of “deviated
transcendence.”
Girard may be right to stress certain particular ways in which
Christian beliefs have brought about a fundamental change in the
sociocultural institutions of the Western world; indeed, to deny
this very basic point would seem foolish. It is another thing entirely to argue for the truth of some subset of these beliefs (e.g., the
divinity of Christ), and I am unpersuaded by Girard’s efforts along
these lines. Moreover, it seems quite reductive to think that the key
to sociohistorical modernity lies primarily in the influence of some
true beliefs about victimization, a claim that is further complicated
by the obscurity of the very idea of a “subterranean” influence,

139

- Desire, Violence, and Revenge

which suggests that it is not a matter of the influence of beliefs at
all. Girard is to be congratulated when he employs the language of
“hypothesis,” but a hypothesis is not a bold idea, or a guess, or a
seemingly plausible speculation; even less is it a leap of faith. A
rigorous use of the language of hypothesis requires some epistemological and methodological considerations having to do with
whether the views in question could ever be meaningfully brought
into confrontation with something like “evidence” and made the
object of some process of rational and empirical justification.
Herein resides my fundamental divergence from a certain tendency in Girard’s thought, for I do not think it methodologically
prudent to link the model of mimetic desire to articles of faith or
to vast generalizations about the course of human history. Even if
these statements happened to be true, they are not justified in the
sense that there is not today any reasonable warrant for believing
in them.
Yet I do not mean to imply that the model of mimetic desire can
be totally separated from any and all assumptions of a sociohistor1cal nature. Rather, the point is that at the present time the theory
of mimetic desire should only be linked to a much broader and
much looser sociohistorical framework, one allowing for any
number of adjustments and specifications. Thus I think it possible
to state that all the mimetic hypothesis requires is that we adopt
whatever sociohistorical assumptions are necessary for it to make
sense to speak of there being social conditions (e.g., institutions
and systems of belief) under which external mediation and internal mediation are possible (but not necessarily in the same contexts). It may very well make sense to think that in truly archaic
cultures, external mediation was the rule, whereas in modern so-

cieties of “equality in principle” and “competition in fact,” internal mediation has become the most commonplace mimetic configuration. This kind of broad hypothesis seems perfectly warranted
as long as one is talking about broad tendencies or trends, which
could easily be supported by reference to many important examples. But there is a tendency in Girard’s rhetoric to speak about
transitions from external to internal forms of mediation as if they
were the manifestations of some kind of fundamental nomic pat-

140 + Models of Desire

tern, and this at both the global historical and personal levels.
Thus, the passage from external to internal mediation becomes the
overarching and ineluctable tendency of History, a tendency that
can only be halted by the “choice” of a truly vertical form of transcendence. On other occasions, Girard speaks as if mimesis were a

disease that moved through a series of pathological stages, progressing from external to increasingly internal forms of mediation,

until finally the desiring agent would go mad. The assumption in
both cases is that external forms of mediation are inherently unstable and necessarily transform themselves into internal mediation
by means of some kind of internal dynamic. Yet this seers an inadequate hypothesis concerning the nature of social and individual
change. In the cases of some societies and some individuals, in-

stances of external mediation may last a very long time, and thus
one needs to look for those conditions that do effectively destabilize hierarchical beliefs.
My central point here is that the very real interest of Girard’s
own insights is sometimes obscured by some unnecessarily ambitious, second-order claims about their explanatory range. And
these claims are too ambitious for two major reasons: first, because

the theory of the dynamics and mechanisms of mimetic desire has
never been accompanied by an actual, detailed mechanistic model
of the psychic stages and transitional laws in question and hence
the nomic rhetoric is a blank check; and second, because there are

ample reasons to assume that the actual manifestations of the putative mechanism are massively overdetermined by factors extra-

neous to the model. The latter cannot be presented, then, as the
determining or autonomous mechanism producing phenomena
as complex as personal and world-historical change, even if it
may indeed constitute an important subset of their conditions.
In short, if mimetic desire produces conflict, rivalry, and violence,

it only does so under certain conditions and in conjunction with
factors that are the product of histories and contingencies that
themselves are not fully explicable in terms of a single mimetic
mechanism. At both the personal and historical levels, it is an unwarranted reduction to point to a single mechanism as the cause
of some overarching tendency or movement. Girard reproaches

141

- Desire, Violence, and Revenge

Freud and modern psychiatry for their “Platonism,” by which he
means a tendency to posit the existence of abstract psychic forms
that bear no dynamic relation to each other or to the pragmatic
conditions of intersubjective relations. This is very good, but
Girard himself sometimes flirts with a kind of “Augustinianism,”
by which I mean a tendency to indulge in eschatological psychohistories.?

Mimetic Desire and Revenge
Girard’s analyses of the links between mimetic desire and violence
specify that certain typical interactive patterns are generated by
mimesis. Of particular interest in the present context are his statements about the patterns that may be associated with revenge and
with what he refers to as the “imitative cycle of violence.” We may
ask how, more specifically, these typical interactive sequences are
explained in terms of mimetic desire. Taking up this question at
some length here will serve a dual function: on the one hand, we
may consider whether it is coherent and plausible to think that the
hypothesis of mimetic desire promises to shed light on an important type of social phenomenon; and on the other, we will see how

the model may be inflected and fleshed out in the discussion of a
particular context of interaction. My point of focus in what follows will not be the empirical validity of Girard’s emphasis on the
importance of cycles of violence (or blood feud) in archaic (and
modern) cultural formations nor the conclusions he draws con-

cerning the historical destiny of the various institutions that function to channel or control aggression. Implicit in my decision to
focus on Girard’s analysis of revenge is the assumption that Girard’s emphasis on this issue is well justified. What I shall be discussing in the present context, however, is not simply the emphasis
on cycles of violence but the attempt to forge an explanatory link
between revenge and the theory of mimetic motivation.

The link between mimesis and vengeance is only implicit in the
long discussion of revenge at the beginning of Violence and the Sacred; nor is it developed at any great length elsewhere in Girard’s

142 + Models of Desire

writings, in spite of the fact that the claim that mimesis can produce extremely disruptive cycles of revenge is crucial to his more
general sociohistorical claims. Let us look at one of Girard’s
lengthier and more explicit statements in this regard:
If the transcendence of the judicial institution is no longer there, if the
institution loses its efficacy or becomes incapable of commanding respect,
the imitative and repetitious character of violence becomes manifest once
more; the imitative character of violence is in fact most manifest in explicit
violence, where it acquires a formal perfection it had not previously possessed. At the level of the blood feud, in fact, there is always only one act,
murder, which is performed in the same way for the same reasons, in

vengeful imitation of the preceding murder. And this imitation propagates itself by degrees. It becomes a duty for distant relatives who had
nothing to do with the original act, if in fact an original act can be identified; it surpasses limits in space and time and leaves destruction everywhere in its wake; 1t moves from generation to generation. In such cases,
in its perfection and paroxysm mimesis becomes a chain reaction of vengeance, in which human beings are constrained to the monotonous repetition of homicide. Vengeance turns them into doubles. (TH 12/21—22)

How, then, is revenge generated by mimetic desire? In a typical
sequence of violent revenge, who is imitating whom? How, exactly, does the murderous

repetition and imitation to which

Girard refers work? To approach such questions, even with the
goal of arriving at a purely schematic and hypothetical model, it
would be useful to refer to something like a typical or at least
somewhat exemplary cycle of revenge or blood feud. To that end, I
will follow Girard’s lead by focusing the analysis on a fictional representation of a feud, my assumption being, not that the analysis of
a literary text can serve as an empirical proof or refutation of the
mimetic theory, but that such a reference to a text can serve as a
useful heuristic in the clarification and formation of the hypothetical model.
My example comes from Brennu-Nyals Saga, perhaps one of the
most well known of the so-called “family sagas” from medieval
Iceland.* Written around the year 1285, the saga situates the events
that it recounts at a much earlier time—roughly a few decades be-

143

- Desire, Violence, and Revenge

fore and after the formal adoption of Christianity by the Althing,
or general assembly, in 999. In its depiction of the earlier period,
the saga is at times strictly anachronistic, yet it also seems that
some of the events it relates may have had some basis in the history

of the island. Such commentators as Rolf Heller lay great stress on
the literary and artificial nature of the events recounted in the saga,
but others believe that important aspects of medieval Icelandic culture do shine through. Jesse Byock writes that “it would be incorrect to assume that the events narrated in the family sagas really
_ happened, but accepting the sagas as revealing the tensions and
dynamics of a rural medieval society is a different matter.” That the
sagas are the product of a society where feuds were an essential
part of the social dynamics seems undeniable. As Byock has argued, “Iceland organized its judicial apparatus, indeed its entire
society, to assist and expedite the resolution of feud.”5 Moreover,

Byock and other specialists, such as Lars Lonnroth, argue convincingly that the actions and motifs related to feuding provide some
of the most essential narrative elements of the family sagas, which
makes these texts particularly relevant to any reader interested in
the structures of revenge. Another scholar who takes the sagas’ depictions of feuding quite seriously is William Miller, who, following Carol Clover, presents detailed arguments for what he calls a
“balance-sheet model” of feuding.® In the present context, my interest in an example of a saga’s depiction of feuds lies in its pertinence for a hypothetical model: what we are after is conceptual
clarifications, not empirical evidence or justification, and thus the
question of the accuracy of the text’s references to historical Iceland need not delay us any further.”
The sequence of feuding to be discussed occupies eleven chapters of Njala (35—45) and hinges upon a prolonged conflict between two women, Bergbora, the wife of Njall, and Hallgerdr, the
wife of Gunnarr. I shall recapitulate the main points of the action
before proceeding to my analysis. The women’s husbands are close
friends and allies; for example, in the episodes leading up to the
feud, Njall’s advice proves of great service to Gunnarr. The two
families share land, and at the time when the feud begins, they
have a tradition of taking turns in staging a yearly autumn banquet

144 - Models of Desire

for each other. It is precisely on such an occasion that the enmity
between the two wives breaks out. (We are given no indication of
any earlier friction or tension between

them.) Bergbora,

the

hostess of the feast, orders Hallgerdr to make room for the wife of
Helgi, Bergbora’s son, who has just arrived. It should be noted that

this gesture occurs in a culture where the seating arrangements at
banquets were highly charged with meaning, being understood as
direct expressions of social rank and priority (a fact that is evident
in the chapter preceding the one in which the quarrel begins,
where we are given a careful description of the seating at another
gathering). Hallgerdr is likely to interpret Bergbora’s gesture,

then, as implying that Bergbora grants the younger woman precedence over Hallgerdr in Bergbora’s home. Hallgerdr indeed responds that she will not move because she does not want to be
made into a hornkerling, or, basically, one of the old women who

have to sit in a corner to make room for others. Bergbora angrily
retorts that she is in charge in her own home. Hallgerdr then directly insults Bergbora, saying that Bergbora and Njall are a good

match because she has a man’s fingernails and he is beardless.
Bergbora answers with yet another insult: Hallgerdr’s former husband may not have been beardless, but this did not stop Hallgerdr

from causing his death (Hallgerdr had indeed incited her foster
father to kill her first husband following a quarrel she had with
him). Hallgerdr then calls upon her husband, Gunnarr, to avenge
the insult that Bergbora has just made, but he angrily refuses, and
the two guests leave, both women swearing that the affair is any-

thing but settled.
The next incident in the feud takes place months later, while the
two husbands are away at the Althing. The two families jointly
own a forest, each family cutting wood there when necessary.
Bergbora orders one of her servants, Svartr, to go there and cut

wood, and some beggars who learn of this happen to tell HalIgerdr. Saying that Bergbora intends to rob her, Hallgerdr has one
of her servants, Kolr, kill Svartr. Hallgerdr herself sends a messenger to the Althing to tell her husband about the murder. But
the two husbands quickly reach an agreement about the necessary
retribution: Gunnarr grants Njall sja/fdemi, the right to make a

145

- Desire, Violence, and Revenge

judgment on the matter without outside arbitration. Njall then
awards himself the modest sum of twelve ounces of silver, a stan-

dard payment in retribution for a slave. Njall stipulates that if Gunnarr should ever be in a similar position, he should judge just as
leniently.
This is precisely what happens, one year later at the next Althing. Hallgerdr has been boasting all year about the murder of
Svartr, which infuriates Bergbora, who hires a freedman, Atli, to

kill Kolr. When the news of this murder reaches the Thing, Njall
~ approaches his friend Gunnarr and offers him sja/fdemie. Gunnarr
keeps his promise, awarding himself the same amount as he paid
out the year before. He notes that Njall pays him with the very
same coins that he had given Njall. Returning home, the men,
who remain good friends, argue with their wives. Bergbora says
she will never let Hallgerdr get the upper hand, and Hallgerdr
berates her husband for making a settlement with Njall.
A year later, Hallgerdr makes the next move. She gets Brynjolfr,
a relative, to come and serve as overseer of her farm and goads him
into killing Atl. Once more, Gunnarr keeps the peace by offering

Njall the right to name the price, and Njall sets the amount at one
hundred ounces of silver, a much higher amount because Atli was

not a slave. The husbands and wives again disagree over the settlement. Another year passes, and Bergbora goads bordr, another

man in the Njall household (the foster father of Njall’s own sons),
into killing Brynjolfr. Again the men reach an agreement at the

Thing, and the same amount changes hands, Gunnarr awarding
himself one hundred silvers.
Anticipating his wife’s next move, Gunnarr sends warnings
to bdordr, but in vain: pbdérdr sees his fetch; he is ambushed

and killed by three men who have been goaded into action by
Hallgerdr. The killers are Sigmundr, one of Gunnarr’s relatives
(his mother’s cousin), and his Swedish friend Skgldr. With them 1s
prainn, another of Gunnarr’s relatives, who looks on as the others

fight (an action which, following the written law of the period,
amounted to full complicity in the murder). At the Thing, Njall
grants himself two hundred silvers for the murder of his sons’ foster father. The men pledge friendship once more.

146 - Models of Desire

After a period of calm, some nomadic beggar women happen to
visit the farm of Gunnarr and Hallgerdr. Claiming that they have
just passed by Njall’s farm, they inflame Hallgerdr by making fun
of Njall and saying that his sons are preparing for battle. At Hallgerdr’s instigation, Sigmundr composes an insulting poem about
Njall and his sons. The beggars next visit Njall’s place, and without
being asked, they tell Bergbéra about the insulting poem. She stirs
up the wrath of her four sons, who, angered by the poem, go out
and kill Sigmundr and Skgldr. But Gunnarr refuses to seek any
compensation, in spite of his wife’s goadings, and three years pass.
Njall then takes the initiative and proposes compensation to Gunnarr in the form of two hundred silvers, and the two men pledge
friendship. This friendship is not broken in the events that follow
in the saga; indeed, Njall and his sons support Gunnarr in the next
bloody feud in which Hallgerdr embroils him, a feud that leads to
his death.

The Dynamics ofRivalry

The feud described by the saga certainly presents some of the general characteristics emphasized by Girard. The story moves back
and forth between essentially three types of settings, which may be
taken to represent three categories of action: first, the respective
households, where, in scenes of /vet (from hvetja, “to whet”), the
women incite their servants and then their relatives to seek violent
revenge; second, the various places where the bloody conflicts occur, always ending in murder; and finally, the annual Althing,
where the two husbands make peace with each other by paying
retribution. That the agreements between the men occur at the
Thing is suggestive ofthe judiciary system’s function in constraining the destructive conflict brought by blood feud. Njall and Gunnarr have a firm vindtta, that is, a bond of friendship that allies
them despite the feud that rages between their households. Although at the end of the sequence of events equal sums of money
have been given and received, the payments in retribution serve to
stabilize the interaction, allowing a nonviolent means of measur-

147 - Desire, Violence, and Revenge

ing the terms of the relationship. The wives’ machinations stand in
a functional opposition to the Thing and to the dealings between
the husbands that occur there: whereas the women’s actions serve
to carry the violence one step forward at each point, the transactions occurring at the Thing serve to stabilize the relation between
the two families by means of financial repayments and pledges of
good will. It is important to insist on this functional opposition
between the judiciary system and the feud. Clover (1986), for example, who focuses her analysis primarily on an incident occur. ring later in the same saga— Hildigunnr’s lament— insists that the
women in the saga are alienated from the men’s agreements and
juridical system. This point may be underscored, for there is a tendency in the sociological and anthropological literature on revenge and feuding to make violent reprisals into a fully codified
and systemic social process, an uninterrupted manifestation of a
structure of exchange.® Thus Byock suggests 1n passing that feuding 1s a “socially stabilizing process.”? Vengeance is seen as an orderly relationship of exchange instead of as a form of reciprocity
that, if unchecked, could put an end to any nonviolent interaction
between groups. In the case under discussion, while it is true that
the actions of Bergbora and Hallgerdr are in many ways guided by
the norms and codes of their culture, their desires for revenge represent a serious threat to the order that Njall and others like him
seek to protect. It is important to remember that it was prolonged,
violent feuding between powerful clans that had totally destabilized Icelandic society by the thirteenth century, leading the
way to the island’s domination by Norway." Finally, in spite of the
impression that may be given by the example under discussion,
neither Njala nor the Icelandic sagas in general consistently depict
women as the primary instigators of revenge.!!
After ten chapters and seven murders ranging over a period of
about eight years, the agreement between Njall and Gunnarr manages to stabilize the situation, temporarily putting an end to the
feud between Bergpora and Hallgerdr at a stage where the monies
paid and received by both sides are equal. But the feud is far from
over. If Hallgerdr stops inciting her people to attack Bergbora’s,
her attention turns elsewhere, and Gunnarr’s entanglements in

148 - Models of Desire

feuding have only just begun. Moreover, Njall’s sons have not yet
fully settled accounts, and a murder occurring fifteen years later
may be linked back to prainn’s role in the murder of their foster

father.!
Roughly speaking, the cycle of violence is made possible, as
Girard suggests, by the inefficiency of the judiciary process, which
is not fully “transcendent” in relation to the family quarrel. The
two women do not respect any of the agreements made at the
Thing, nor are the formal judgments and pledges respected by
those men who let themselves be egged on to acts of murder, often
against their own wishes and better judgment. It is also true that
remote members of the family are dragged into a quarrel that,

from another perspective, hardly seems to concern them: they are
in a sense total strangers to the dispute between the two women,

but this does not stop the conflict from leading to their death. It is
also the case that the violence moves closer and closer to the immediate relatives of the parties concerned. Girard emphasizes the repetitive character of the murders, the symmetrical structure of the
action, and the tendency of the cycle to escalate from relation to
relation, all of which correspond to features of the feud described

in this text.
The series of violent actions that take place in the saga support
Girard’s stress on the extent to which rivalry, which may or may
not have a starting point in some material object, can become
wholly detached from any quest for material advantage, turning
instead on notions of honor, personal priority, and prestige. Indeed, the “original action” in the feud is an insult that concerns the
social ranking of two women at a banquet, yet such a small incident leads to no fewer than seven murders! What is it, in fact, that

gets the conflict going? Given the social norms and relations that
are already in place at the time, Bergbora’s initial request that Hal-

lgerdr move over for a younger woman, who is also Bergb6ra’s
own relative, can only be understood by Hallgerdr as an affront to
her pride—a pride that is, according to both the narrator of the

saga and some of the other characters, quite exaggerated. Whence
the violence of Hallgerdr’s refusal and her escalation of the quarrel

149 - Desire, Violence, and Revenge

by insulting Bergbora and her husband. The “object” of the quarrel is nothing other than the women’s rank or status in relation to
each other, and the quarrel is fueled by the great importance both
women attach to this issue.
The significance of this kind of rivalry to the patterns of feud in
medieval Iceland has been usefully acknowledged by Byock, in

spite of his occasional tendency to emphasize those cases in the
sagas where the material goods at stake provide a powerful incentive to fighting (and it is certainly important to recognize along
. with Byock that there are many such cases in the texts). Byock at
one point argues that the frequency of feuding in this culture is
best explained by the scarcity of material goods and most of all, by
the “need” for land.!3 Yet some interesting cases do not exactly correspond to Byock’s idea, for the Landnamabok (a text depicting the
“land taking” during the settlement of Iceland) refers to several
instances where individuals preferred to fight for land rather than
receive it as a gift. The motivation behind such an unfortunate
preference was not a “need” but a number of far-reaching beliefs
about the social and personal implications of gifts: it was thought
that in certain contexts, to receive a gift was to be indebted, sometimes even humiliated, and the proud man preferred to fight. For
example, in one text a newcomer in Iceland named Halkel spends
his first winter with a relative, Ketilbjarn, who then offers him land

of his own. Considering it unmanly (ltdmannlight) to accept such
an offer, Halkel instead challenges his host to a duel, kills him, and

takes over his estate.!* It is clear that many of the most important
feuds described in the sagas have no basis 1n the objective scarcity
of land or any other commodity. For example, Njall and Gunnarr
seem to have no problem in peacefully sharing a commodity that is
usually said to have been quite scarce on the island— wood— until
the two wives’ pride comes into play. But the point is perhaps already implicitly granted by Byock, who notes that “conflicts that
started over material goods often turned into feuds involving issues of power and honor, while ongoing feuds between powerful
men were often fueled by smaller conflicts over material goods between less important persons. Insult, killing, horse fighting, and

150 - Models ofDesire

harboring an outlaw were frequent causes for violent clashes.
Honor, in all such cases, demanded that a man preserve his reputa-

tion and guard his family name.”!

Feuding and Imitation

We are now in a position to raise our central question: if the feud
described in the saga is characterized by some of the main features
stipulated in Girard’s theory of cycles of violence, how does this
description stand in relation to his insistence on the role of mimesis in generating such patterns? Are the two women and the other
agents involved behaving mimetically, and if so, who 1s imitating
whom at each stage of the action? Please note that as I flesh out
aspects of the story’s content, I am asking, not whether the saga’s
author thought about the characters in terms of mimesis, but how
Girard’s model is to be concretized if the actions directly reported
by the text are to be given a plausible mimetic reading. Nor am I
asking whether my interpretation of the story, which is of course
not the only one that is compatible with the text’s explicit indications, somehow confirms or disproves the mimetic theory.
Bergbora insults Hallgerdr, telling her to move over to make
room for a younger woman in the household, expressing a definition of their social ranks that does not correspond to Hallgerdr’s
sense of her own place in the scheme of things. The narrative gives
us a glimpse into Hallgerdr’s reasoning when she decides to launch
the first attack, for we read that she tells herself that Bergbora is
trying to “rob” her. The object of this “theft,” however, is not any
particular thing at all—uit is certainly not the wood that the luckless woodcutter would have taken from the forest shared by the
two families. Rather, Hallgerdr’s accusation is more diffuse and
general: Bergbora, she complains, thinks that she is going to rob
her “in everything.”!6 The literal forms of acquisitive desire are in
the background here, but they are not the primary motives.
Whom 1s Bergpbora imitating, then, when she first insults Hallgerdr? What is her desire, and who is her model? The text tells us

nothing about any events in their previous dealings that could

151 - Desire, Violence, and Revenge
have conditioned or otherwise motivated Bergbora’s action, and

we could only speculate about any antecedents of the initial insult.
Whatever the motives, Bergb6ra’s statement manifests an implicit
comment on Hallgerdr’s status, a desire to “put her in her place,” a
place that Hallgerdr clearly does not recognize as being her own.
Let us move on then, to Hallgerdr’s response, which amounts to
publicly belittling her host and hostess. What is Hallgerdr’s desire
here? Is she imitating Bergbdra? Does her action match Bergp6ra’s
or surpass it?
It could be argued that there is no question of imitation here,
since the women’s actions at each step in the rivalry display significant differences. Bergbora asks Hallgerdr to move over for someone, and the response is an extremely violent piece of slander.
(Assailing Njall’s manliness by referring to his lack of a beard was
a particularly aggressive action in Icelandic society.)!”? Bergbéra
then insults Hallgerdr, and Hallgerdr responds by having a servant murder one of Bergpora’s thralls, no small escalation. At a

later stage there would seem to be yet another major case of a significant difference between action and reaction. When Hallgerdr
gets one of her relatives to kill Atli, Bergbora’s next move is to call
on pordr, the foster father of Njall’s sons, and she can hardly fail to
see that should Hallgerdr act against bordr, her sons will get involved in the feud—and everything she knows about Hallgerdr
tells us that p6rdr is the next man on the list. For this reason,

Bergbora’s choice of bordr amounts to a serious escalation of the
feud. Can we speak of imitation, then, when the response to an
antecedent action is qualitatively different from that action? Are
these really instances of matching the other’s action or of doing the
same thing? One could reasonably be skeptical on this score.
Perhaps it is better to say that each action matches the previous
one precisely because it surpasses it. For example, from Bergpb6ra’s
perspective, Hallgerdr has carried things one step further with her
first insult, but from Hallgerdr’s point of view, the situation is
more or less even only after she has made mock of Bergbora’s nails
and Njall’s lack of a beard. Her reasoning? Basically, she reckons
that Bergbora has tried to belittle her, to assert priority over her;
with her insult Hallgerdr has “merely” turned the tables, inverting

152 - Models of Desire

their places within the same schema of ranking. In expressing herself in terms implying that she is superior to Bergbora, Hallgerdr is
“doing the same thing” that Bergb6ra just did to her, because only
a truly inferior guest could be shuffled around and told to move
over. The motivations—a desire to get the upper hand—are
highly similar.
The same analysis may be applied to the next move, and perhaps
to many of the subsequent ones as well: Bergb6ora makes an insult
that is even more extreme, for she publicly refers to the fact that
Hallgerdr had goaded her foster father into killing her first husband. In her view, she has to respond very strongly to this if she
does not want to lose face. Had Bergbora chosen an action that
stressed the equality of the two women’s positions, this would not
have been perceived as a matching of the rival’s last action, which

was an implicit bid for first place, not equality; thus from Bergpora’s ’s perspective, she must try to top Hallgerdr, for anything

else amounts to having allowed Hallgerdr to humiliate her. In this
context, then, matching the other’s action means escalating the
rivalry by repeating the bid for first place.
Thus there is a clear sense in which we can say that although the
women’s actions are different, and increasingly more extreme, at
each step, they desire similar things. The similarity in question exists at the level of the implicit goals and preferences subtending
both women’s behavior. Each woman thinks that she cannot afford
to lose face, and to allow the other to make a bid for priority without being punished for it is indeed to lose face; the only way to
punish someone properly for having made such a bid is to claim, in
turn, priority over that person. The only parity is disparity, for a
response that simply affirmed one’s literal equality to the other
would be understood as letting a slight go unpunished. Both of
the rivals, then, must strive to occupy a position of superiority, and
it is this position that is the implicit goal of their actions. The
structure of the relationship desired by each of them is the same;
they merely invert their respective places in the ranking. Thus it is
possible to say that the interactional sequence of rivalry can be analyzed in terms of the rivals’ similar desires, in spite of the very real
differences between their particular actions. What they desire can

153

- Desire, Violence, and Revenge

be described in terms of an abstract situation whose essential feature is a particular type of relationship between the two parties in
the dispute. This relationship is “hierarchical” in that it defines a
situation where some significant form of superiority over the other
is enjoyed by one of the parties; the terms and forms of expression
of this superiority may vary greatly, but the essential state of affairs
that is desired remains the same.
This said, we still have not established that Hallgerdr and Bergbora are engaged in double mediation. They may desire the same
~ thing, but are they also each other’s models in these desires? If so,

what tutelary beliefs support the mimetic desiring in question?
And what about the other agents in the feud, the men who do the
fighting? Are they, too, animated by mimetic desire? The story

does not tell us about any previous imitative interaction that
would be relevant to Bergp6ra’s initial insult, but we do know that

this initial action is a spur to Hallgerdr’s response, and from that
moment on, the story is structured in terms of a back-and-forth
dynamic in which each move is strongly conditioned by the other
party's last move. In this sense the feud 1s strongly interactive and
certainly has the kind of cybernetic, runaway quality that Girard
likes to stress in his discussions of double mediation. It does make
some sense to suppose that Bergbora could be serving as the mediator of Hallgerdr’s competitive desire: if Hallgerdr was not suffering from a desire to dominate her hostess prior to the moment
of the first insult, she certainly burns with this and only this desire
a few moments later and will go on burning with it for years. The
source of this desire surely is the challenge that the insult represents to her self-image and to her social standing, so the duration
and personal significance of the desire qualify it as an instance of
personal mediation. Both women will henceforth desire one
thing, victory over the rival, and the knowledge that the rival de-

sires this thing makes the desire all the more intense. The kind of
self-sustaining reciprocal interaction described in chapter 4 is certainly in place here.
But saying that Bergbora is Hallgerdr’s model, and as the feud
progresses, her mediator, does not amount to a full explanation of

why Hallgerdr is driven to insult her hostess, nor does it fully ex-

154 + Models of Desire
plain the complex sequence of events that follows. Why, for example, does Hallgerdr not accept the insult and seek to placate her
hostess? Or again, why does she not consider herself above any
petty rivalry with her hostess? Why does Bergbora prefer risking
her people’s lives to accepting a slight from the other woman?
Here we return to the topic of the tutelary beliefs that establish
Bergbora as a particular type of model for Hallgerdr, and vice
versa. Crucial to this topic is the equality of the two women’s social
ranks, or at the very least, the absence of any stable, socially recognized ranking of their respective statuses. The narrative is clear on
this point: both women are said to possess exceptional beauty, and
although Hallgerdr has a reputation for her violent temper, this
does not exactly mean that she has no status in the “Viking” community of the sagas (many of the men, for example, seem to think

of her as an attractive but dangerous match). In the cultural context of the story, the women’s status is intimately linked to that of
their husbands. Njall and Gunnarr are both influential, powerful,

and attractive men; they are bendr, free and independent landowners living in a society that knows no kingship and no “nobility,” if by this is meant a system of social classification based on
hereditary orders. Yet theirs is at the same time a society where
distinctions of prestige are central to the social fabric. These distinctions are attached to the relative status and glory of both families and individuals, and the typical symbols of this status are
wealth, courage in combat, knowledge of the law, personal influ-

ence and leadership, and an honor that allows no affront without
retribution.
The essential point, then, is that the ranking of the two families
and of its members is both an open and very significant question
for the two women: public recognition of one’s high social status is
believed by them to be an intrinsic end to which many other factors should be subordinated. And this important desire for social
recognition and distinction must express itself in a context where
distinction is never fully certain. Insofar as the one perceives any
sign of real or pretended superiority in the other, this distinction is
not believed to be a necessary or lasting mark of hierarchy, but
temporary and changeable, a strategic contingency that may be re-

155

- Desire, Violence, and Revenge

moved with effort and a little luck. Hallgerdr may, when reeling
from the other woman’s last blow, admire her force or cleverness,

but this beliefin the other’s superiority only fuels her desire to win
out in the next round. Thus the mimesis in question is emulative,
not hierarchical, and the mediator is an obstacle, not someone be-

lieved to be out of one’s own range. Although the text does not
offer any evidence on this issue, it seems likely that neither HalIgerdr nor Bergb6ora would acknowledge the other as her mediator
or model: the investment in a self-concept of personal priority is
simply too strong.

The Mimetic Background

Yet this last observation points to another dimension of the mimetic modeling that may be at work in this sort of affair, for the
kind of internal, emulative mediation that I have just discussed still
does not adequately explain the women’s massive investment in
their rivalry with each other. What we have established is that the
two women’s sense of their relative standing is not stabilized by
any socially given hierarchy, and thus their standing is in question.
But why do they each believe it so important not to lose face—so
important indeed as to warrant getting entangled in murderous
intrigues that flatly contradict their husbands’ wishes? Why do
they think that so much is at stake here, and why, once more, does
their rivalry take the particular form of expression that it does,

namely, arranging to have men kill members of the other household? An adequate response to these questions would require a
lengthy examination of the two women’s personal histories, which
is to say, in Girardian terms, the history of their relations to past
models and mediators. And such an analysis in turn requires detailed reference to the norms and beliefs prevalent in the cultural
context in question, beliefs that serve as articles of faith for those
involved in the feud. Here the Girardian analysis of the reciprocal
modeling that begins with the insults would require amplification
by reference to a longer-term developmental scenario, perhaps one
quite similar to a social learning approach, for in both cases it

156 - Models of Desire

would amount to showing how Hallgerdr and Bergbora have
learned from others the attitudes and capacities central to the personal dispositions that make them prone to engage in a protracted
and murderous feud.
The evidence provided by the saga is clearly insufficient to make
such an analysis possible, but the narrator does offer a few telling
clues in relation to Hallgerdr.!8 In the folk wisdom related by the
narrator of the saga, the headstrong woman’s character is traced

back directly to the propensities and deeds of the man who raised
her, a foster father named pjostolfr: “He was strong and skilled in

arms; he had killed many men and paid compensation for none of
them. It was said that he did little to temper Hallgerdr’s character.”!9 In the Old West Norse, the last phrase reads “at hann veri

engi skapboetir Hallgerdi,” which means literally that he was not a
“better-maker” for Hallgerdr, an expression suggestive of an in-

sight into the importance of social influence in that the noun
skapbetir picks out those people whose example improves others’
dispositions. Indeed, Hallgerdr’s foster father does nothing to
make her life more peaceful. He is the man who murders HalIgerdr’s first husband at her request and gets away with it, and who

appears at her second wedding brandishing an axe and looking
hostile. The guests pretend not to notice, probably because to do
so would be to risk one’s life. The same bjéstolfr later murders HalIgerdr’s second husband (against her will this time) and finally gets
killed.
Hallgerdr’s headstrong nature and her concern with social rank
and revenge could plausibly have been conditioned by her growing up under the influence of such models. She says as much herself in an interesting exchange with her real father, who has
wounded her pride by arranging her first marriage behind her back
(which practice was not the least bit uncommon). Her complaint is
twofold: first of all, her father has not cared about her enough to
discuss the matter with her before concluding a deal, and second,
the marriage does not give her as high a rank as her father had
promised. When he responds that he does not prize her arrogance
enough to break a deal that has already been made, she responds
that her attitude should not surprise him, because it is an attitude

157 - Desire, Violence, and Revenge
prevalent in the family. The textual details are interesting here.
Hallgerdr’s phrase begins with the words “Mikill er metnadr
ydvarr frenda” (roughly, “Great is the metnadr of you and your
Kinsmen”), and she goes on to say that it is hardly surprising that
she too should have the same trait in her somewhere.?° The attitude Hallgerdr admits to having, which she attributes to her relatives in the same breath—metnadr—is variously translated as
“ambition,” “pride,”

0

“arrogance,” and so on. More literally, the

word evokes a gnawing or passion for “weighing” or “measuring”
self and other.
~
In the world evoked by the saga, aggressive actions are a common enough way of expressing one’s “ambition” or lust for measuring oneself against others: it suffices to attack and to win, to
defend oneself successfully against those who seek to make one
pay. The social modeling of aggressive behavior finds many examples in the Icelandic family sagas. A particularly extreme example is
Bera’s reaction to her son Egill’s first murder in Egils saga. At age
seven, Egill gets in a scrap with an older playmate during a ball
game, and with the encouragement and help of an older friend,

Egill finishes his young rival off with an axe. A battle ensues, but
Egill’s people overpower their adversaries and Egill comes home
safely. Hearing about her precocious son’s exploit, Egill’s mother
says that he has everything it takes to become a real Viking and
that he should obviously be given a fighting-ship as soon as he 1s
old enough.?!
The foregoing remarks suggest the plausibility of a mimetic
analysis of the motivations that could lead to the sequence of reprisals described in the saga. Both of the rivals have a history of
mediation and modeling inculcating in them those attitudes and
inclinations needed to fuel a protracted and escalating dispute.
Given these beliefs and motivational dispositions, it is possible

that a challenger could become a highly salient model, one whose
actions and attitudes would become the focal point of a significant
period of an individual’s personal history. Indeed, both Hallgerdr
and Bergbora invest heavily in their competitive relation to each
other for eight years or more, and both engage in the extended
plotting and manipulations necessary to get a number of different

158 + Models of Desire

men to take up arms. Their reciprocal mediation leads to a situation where getting the upper hand over the other woman becomes
a central, intrinsically desirable end in life, and various other priorities are subordinated to this project.

Mimesis and Murder

In my analysis of the possible mimetic motivations of the feud, I
have left out a rather crucial consideration, namely, the motives
and beliefs of the men who actually do the killing and dying. Are
they driven by mimetic desires? What of the actions of the two
husbands, who annually bargain over the price to be paid for
murder? Is there anything mimetic about these transactions? I
shall take up these two issues in turn.
It is not an exaggeration to say that the murderers and victims
fall into two categories, the enthusiastic and the unenthusiastic.
The former have an appetite for battle and require little encouragement from the women to go out and attack the designated party.
They do this fully aware that if successful, they will most likely be
the next target. But as argued above, this is a world where there is
honor and prestige to be had through successful combat, a world
where cowardice is to be shunned at any cost. The latter clause is
what preoccupies the men I have labeled unenthusiastic. They
know that they cannot afford not to be guided by the women’s
goading, but at the same time they have little belief that anything
good can come of it. In the feud under discussion, eleven men are

motivated to engage in five violent attacks. Four of these men are
explicitly unenthusiastic about getting involved in the violence but
let themselves be persuaded to do so anyway, most frequently
when a woman raises the question of cowardice. An example is
Kolr, who first responds to Hallgerdr’s request by pointing out
that he will get killed if he murders Svartr; but as soon as she
suggests he may lack courage, he angrily agrees to do her bidding.
Atli is the best example of the opposite: he introduces himself to
Bergb6ra as someone who has caused many wounds and is eager to
attack the man she singles out.

159 - Desire, Violence, and Revenge

In what sense are these two types of motivation mimetic? It is
accurate to say that Atli desires something similar to what Bergbora desires to the extent that both want him to succeed in killing
Kolr, and it is also accurate to say that it is Bergb6ra’s desire that
orients Atli’s action. At the same time, however, to say that they

“desire the same thing” would amount to obscuring important differences. We have already established that what Bergbora really
wants is primacy over her rival and model in this feud, Hallgerdr,
and it is far-fetched to ascribe any such desire to Atli, who seems to
be after a more diffuse form of honor and glory. Perhaps both
~ Bergpora and Atli desire the same thing in that both follow a more
abstract social model that tells them that to struggle to win first
place is a highly desirable course of action. Yet such was Atli’s disposition long before he met the woman. But this point should not
rule out consideration of the more immediate relation between
them: in following the attractive woman’s directives, Atli may also
be motivated by a strong desire for her admiration.
A similar type of motivation is hinted at in the narration of HalIgerdr’s goading of Sigmundr, for the text refers to the great
strength of their sympathy and to the fact that she served him no
less well than she served her husband, which made people talk.
Reference is also made to her influencing him with money. Yet
Hallgerdr takes quite a different approach with pbrainn; she evokes
the fact that he is her son-in-law (by her former marriage) and uses
this familial relation to pressure him into taking up her cause. Fi-

nally, there are the motives of the four sons of Njall, who go out
into the night to encounter Sigmundr and his Swedish friend.
When Bergbora tells them about the insulting poem and goads
them into action, they pretend not to be moved. But in fact they
are furious. Their desire for revenge is not at all a copy of their
mother’s desire to win out over Hallgerdr. The law specifies that
the kinds of insults figuring in the poem constitute a legal warrant
for murder.?? Thus their anger is not a copy of Bergbdéra’s emotions; from their perspective, it is the direct expression of their

own honor as well as of their loyalty to their beloved foster father,
bordr, who was the last victim in the feud, and they have with reluctance been observing their father’s truce with the other side. If

160 - Models of Desire
there is a mimetic element in their motivation, it is not a matter of
their imitating the recent desires of their mother, father, or the
men who killed their foster father. Rather, what they desire is to

preserve their personal honor by repaying those who insulted
them and who killed their close relative. It would be inaccurate to
think that such a desire is taken from the murderers of their foster
father, and thus the rivalry in which they are involved, although it
may partake of the symmetrical and reciprocal features of the feud
as a whole, is not motivated by any reciprocal mediation or mod-

eling. Their motivation, if not their action, is not isomorphic to
the kind of direct cycle of attack and counterattack in which Bergbora and Hallgerdr are engaged. The only way to make that argument would be to suppose that they identify strongly with their
mother’s perspective, but in fact they overtly refuse the idea that it
is her anger that moves them. Consequently, it may be observed
that if they are moved by mimetic desire, it is again of a less proximal variety: they could be said to be acting on beliefs and attitudes
acquired in a long personal history of social learning, a more distal
variety of modeling. In short, the motives of the different men
who do the killing and dying are varied. A central thread in the
motivational processes is a number of generalized beliefs pertaining to personal honor, courage, and retribution, and the different
men act in function of these beliefs—in some cases being pulled
forward by their attractive appeal, in others being pushed along
with reluctance and a kind of grim resolve: one must kill and be
killed in order not to suffer a worse punishment, the public accusation of cowardice hurled by a woman.
What of the role played by the two husbands in the affair? To
what extent are their motivations mimetic? The two men’s actions
throughout the feud are guided by an explicit desire to maintain
their pact of friendship, and this externalized concept of their relationship provides the stable anchor for their various decisions: it is
clear that each believes preserving their alliance to be in his best
interest and actively seeks to attain this goal. Thus each man is willing to sacrifice something desired by everyone—the silver paid in
a ritual of retribution—in order to deal with a situation rendered
unstable by the last act of aggression. Each man is careful to main-

161

- Desire, Violence, and Revenge

tain the symmetry in the payments, a symmetry that symbolizes
their parity in each other’s eyes. It could be said that they imitate
each other in this regard, each one’s prudence and caution serving
as a model to the other, but it remains necessary to say how such an
imitation is possible. We can only speculate as to the genesis of this
alliance; the text presents them as great friends from the beginning, and there 1s a kind of symbolic complementarity to the virtues that the narrative has them heroically embody: Gunnarr is the
superlative warrior, while Njall’s wisdom and prophetic capacities
are unmatched. At the same time, the alliance should probably be
~ understood as a highly political one: even a powerful man cannot
stand entirely alone in this society; both men have much to lose by
letting the other become an enemy, and much to gain by maintaining the alliance. As both nourish self-concepts where honor and
probity have an important place, neither wishes to incur the dishonor of breaking their bond, so that the purely pragmatic bases of
the alliance are reinforced by personal self-evaluations. Moreover,
to break this pact is to be in debt, to have to pay retribution in one

form or another. Thus at a point in the action when it is Njall’s
turn to seek retribution, Nyall tells Gunnarr that he does not want
the break in their pact of friendship (vimdtta) to be caused by him
(Njall). He sets a price much lower than what his wife and sons
would agree to, and he makes the agreement in his sons’ absence,

knowing that they would not accept money for the death of their
foster father but will have to abide by this arrangement once it is
made. Similarly, at the next stage in the cycle, Gunnarr forgoes
seeking retribution, in spite of his wife’s frequent goadings. Three
years later, it is Njall who spontaneously offers compensation, a
gesture that strongly stabilizes the situation.
But the text does not present this delicate balance as the product
of some kind of unselfish devotion and affection. Subtle details
suggest that the heads of the two households, in spite of an overriding desire to keep the peace, cannot help but experience a
strong desire for revenge when confronted with the damage just
done by the other side, particularly when the person lost is a relative. Gunnarr thus knows that Njall will be severely tried when he
learns of the murder of his sons’ foster father, and indeed, when

162 + Models of Desire

Njall’s sons finally go out and kill the murderers, Njall spontaneously praises their action. Njall is confronted with contrasting
desires, for his wish to keep the peace with Gunnarr is threatened

by a genuine desire to see his sons avenge the murder. The former
desire is to some extent an imitation of Gunnarr’s motives but also
derives from a strong sense of virtue; the latter desire, far from
being a punctual imitation of his wife’s or his sons’ violent emotions, arises from the background beliefs having to do with propriety and honor: Njall simply cannot let the murder of his sons’
foster father go lightly.

Rivalry and Doubling
Another feature of Girard’s account of the general effects of a cycle
of revenge generated by mimesis should be discussed at this point,
namely, his suggestion that the process turns the adversaries into
“doubles.” Thus Girard contends that mimetic rivalry tends to
eliminate the differences between people’s desires (VS 159/222).
In regard to our example, this is a notion that runs directly against
the grain of most of the commentaries, for a whole tradition surrounds the saga’s depiction of Hallgerdr as an archetypally treacherous and evil female. Hallgerdr, then, must be read as a singular

and striking character, not as an agent whose dispositions are
shaped by her environment and interactions. Boyer points out that
the name of the infamous female is no longer given to Icelandic
girls and labels Hallgerdr “a kind of fury and femme fatale.”23
Heller links the character to a literary and mythical tradition in
which she stands as a quasi-supernatural incarnation of evil and
calamity.** Girard’s stress on symmetry, then, would be contradicted by the narrator’s asymmetrical characterization of the two
women and of their respective allies.2° Lonnroth underscores the
extent to which the narrative is structured in terms of a simple dichotomy between good and evil, a dichotomy that may have had
a very real pragmatic function for both the storyteller and the
audience.?6
In regard to the differences between the parties in the feud, I

163 - Desire, Violence, and Revenge

think it necessary to recognize that the text contains elements of
asymmetry and symmetry in an interesting mixture. The point is
certainly not that one should revive the tradition that tried to
whitewash Hallgerdr.2” Such a rehabilitation attempt is a sterile
and hopeless exercise in regard to a text that literally “fates” HalIgerdr from childhood to behave badly (as a very young girl she
is told that she already has the eyes of a thief, and she indeed goes
on to involve herself in a despicable piece of larceny). Rather, what
is important 1s to show the error of eliminating the important
_ elements of symmetry in the text. If the infamous Hallgerdr is
explicitly labeled as having a bad disposition, her opponent
Bergbora is no princess of grace and humility. The narrator characterizes Njall’s mate as an “excellent woman” but adds that she is
somewhat “harsh-natured” (skaphord, the same term used to describe Hallgerdr).?8 Her subsequent actions reveal her to be stub-

born and vindictive. If Hallgerdr is supposed to be the source of
all evil, the bringer of calamity, why is it that the insult that starts

the feud is made by the “sympathetic” Bergbdéra? Heller, a critic
who is exuberant in blackening Hallgerdr, at least acknowledges

this question but begs it by attributing to Bergbora the very
“knowledge” for which more evidence should be provided. Thus
Heller simply asserts that Bergbora “acts on the knowledge” that
Hallgerdr represents evil and calamity: how could Bergbora be expected to befriend such a character??? In fact, the hypothesis that

the text presents the two women as having radically different
characters prior to the feud should serve to underscore the necessity of recognizing the powerful influence exercised on them by
their rivalry. If in the beginning Bergb6ora does not have HalIgerdr’s essentially evil disposition, if she is only “somewhat” harsh
as opposed to totally hardhearted, is it not interesting that she too
is led to incite men to murder each other over a petty rivalry? If it is
no surprise that the headstrong and evil Hallgerdr goads men to
their deaths and quarrels with her husband whenever he makes
peace, why is it that Bergbora does exactly the same things?
Thus the initial differences between the women are eroded by
the history of the interaction itself, and the “excellent” Bergbora
becomes more and more like her opponent. Herein resides the

164 + Models of Desire

kind of symmetry to which Girard points, a symmetry that is situated at a level deeper than that of moral judgments of individual
