characters inasmuch as it concerns the powerful role of intersubjective relations and social influences in determining what the
agents do. In this regard, the rich texture of the actions reported
by the text militates against reducing everything to a single asymmetrical relation between good and evil. This is, I think, the key

insight behind Girard’s talk of “the doubles.” It should also be
pointed out that the feud between the two women does not go far
enough to involve the kind of total social crisis to which Girard
refers when he evokes the radical nondifferentiation of “doubling.” It is already quite significant that the moral differences between Bergbora and Hallgerdr for which some readers argue so
vehemently are already put in question by the similarity of their
competitive desires and actions.

Mimetic Dynamics

Although there is a quasi-cybernetic flavor to the runaway cycle of
rivalry between Hallgerdr and Bergbora, it is only in a very loose
sense that one could speak here of a single mechanism governing
their motives and actions. This is the case because the pattern of
double mediation is not manifested in a pure and autonomous
form but is immersed in a complex texture of other factors, such as
the other kinds of social and psychological determinations introduced above, of which some are a matter of the past history of
personal and intersubjective mimesis, while others can hardly be
explained in terms of a mimetic psychology. Thus it would appear
that even in regard to a highly stylized and schematic textual example, feuding is not reducible to the single dynamic of reciprocal
interaction described in terms of double mediation. This is not to
say that such a dynamic is not at the heart of the example; on the
contrary: Bergpora’s and Hallgerdr’s emotions take the form of a
highly mimetic runaway cycle of hatred and aggression. Yet this
motivational dynamic does not function autonomously. The cycle
of violent actions finds some of the conditions necessary to its

165

- Desire, Violence, and Revenge

functioning in another form of mimesis, the more distal and more
longstanding type of mediation that we may associate with developmental processes and social learning. The latter point should be
underscored: Bergbdra and Hallgerdr can only fascinate each
other reciprocally in a runaway process because of the kind of people they are when their rivalry begins, and many complex factors,
including histories of social modeling, are responsible for their dispositions and actions. Similarly, the other actors in the drama are
not motivated solely by the direct, mimetic influence of the two
. women, or even by the actions and desires of their opponents; although these factors are in some instances important, personal histories and socially instituted practices and beliefs are always crucial
in motivating the men to fight or keep the peace.
Mention must also be made of the role of contingent factors in
the sequence of events recounted by the saga. The saga presents a
highly symmetrical and stylized sequence of actions, one already
purged of the messiness and detail that is encountered in actual
human affairs. It should be noted, for example, that the storyteller

decides the outcome of each combat in the manner that makes possible the most efficient presentation of a cycle of feuding: those
who attack, seeking violent retribution, always win. This is in a
sense a wholly trivial detail because it in no way diminishes the
interest of the narrative, which is simply more streamlined as a result of this convenience. But another way in which the narrative
fuels the cycle of violence is of independent interest. At a key point
in the sequence, the point at which Njall has accepted two hundred
silvers for b6rdr’s murder and has enjoined his sons not to avenge
him, it is a group of outsiders, and not the members of the feud-

ing groups, who play a crucial role in advancing the conflict. By
chance, some nomadic beggar women happen to visit Hallgerdr,

and it is their provocative talk that leads to the composition and
communication of the insulting poem. This event occurs at a moment when the feud seems to have calmed down, and it leads to a

dramatic escalation of the action.
How is the action of these beggars to be related to the dynamics
of mimetic interaction at work in the feud? One can only speculate
about the motives of these devious beggars (who may appear to

166 + Models ofDesire
some critics as a mere prop used to advance the narrative), but it

should be clear that they cannot plausibly be said to be driven by
the same desires as the parties who are more immediately involved
in the conflict: the beggars do not share Hallgerdr’s hatred of
Bergbora, nor are they moved by Bergbora’s desire for revenge
against Hallgerdr. Perhaps as beggars they thrive by telling their
hostesses (and benefactors?) what they think they want to hear and
thus strategically model their statements on what they know, from
hearsay, about the desires of Hallgerdr and Bergbora. But in any
case it is not the system of mimetic desire defining the interactions
of Hallgerdr and Bergbora that causes the beggars to appear when
they do, and thus we may conclude that the system of mimetic
rivalry in question here does not function in perfect autonomy.
The element of contingency that the arrival and actions of the beggars introduces into the sequence of events of the story contradicts
the idea that the particular course of action is wholly determined
by any single psychological or interactional mechanism or set of
laws: the dynamics of interpersonal mimesis are “open” to the play
of circumstance, and it is only by looking at the interaction of the
two that we can understand what happens.%°
In conclusion, then, we emphasize the fact that the functioning
of reciprocal imitation, its effective generation of a bloody feud, is
only possible given any number of complex conditions. In pointing to these conditions, we trace the limits of a psychological and
dispositional explanation of actions and interactions and emphasize the need to take into account various immediate and more distant physical and social circumstances. These circumstances must
figure in any explanation of the transition from one social situation
to the next. We may contrast different kinds of conditions and factors that conspire to reinforce the dynamic of feuding. Some of the
conditions are part of a psychology of mimesis; others, such as
contingent intersections of human actions, are not. Among the
mimetic factors, one may distinguish between proximal and distal
types of mimetic factors, the former having to do with the immediate genesis of the agents’ motivational states, the latter with the
prior circumstances and events that have helped to create a situ-

167 - Desire, Violence, and Revenge

ation in which a Hallgerdr and a Bergbdra may become each
other’s mediators in a lengthy and highly destructive feud.
Among these background conditions, one may point, first of
all, to a particular form of socialization of the actors involved, for

only reference to such a socialization can explain the genesis of the
determinant mimetic dispositions. In regard to the example under
discussion, what is at stake is a frequent modeling and glorification
of aggressive and competitive action as well as a fixation of beliefs
hinging on the importance of personal ambition and distinction.
_ Second, it is necessary to evoke, along with Girard, the crucial role
of certain institutional facts, in particular the absence of any central executive authority capable of deterring violence or at least of
preventing its perpetuation between neighbors. Quite obviously,
the existence of a particular institution of kinship is also determinant in making the feud possible. In relation to the example, one
may point specifically to the role of fosterage, an institution by
means of which familial bonds were artificially extended so as to
reinforce the potential power of a given group.
Finally, it may be noted that the agents’ beliefs about their own
social context 1s another crucial element in the drama, and the rela-

tions between these beliefs and interpersonal dynamics are highly
complex. While being driven by their mimetic fascination with
each other, both Bergbdéra and Hallgerdr employ strategies that
imply an awareness of the social landscape within which their respective strategies are situated. The assumptions about social relations at stake here are complicated. For example, when Bergbora
gets pordr, the foster father of Njall’s sons, to kill Brynjolfr, she
engages in a strategy that promises to aggravate the conflict considerably. She knows that her husband has received one hundred
silvers for the death of Atli, a free man who was not their relative;

she knows that up to this point in the affair, the two husbands have
kept their relations stable by maintaining a careful balance of accounts: after Gunnarr asks for twelve silver coins, Njall in turn asks
for only twelve. Should pordr be killed in turn, Njall and his sons

would be required to seek an even greater retribution, which
would greatly unsettle the precarious balance between the two

168 + Models of Desire

groups. When Hallgerdr gets one of her husband’s relatives to kill
pordr, her strategy is similar in that it too is a matter of exploiting
her knowledge of the familial bonds and obligations in an effort to
accelerate the conflict. Knowledge of the obligations involved in
the kinship relations is thus an essential part of the women’s strategies. But this knowledge and these strategies are not copied as
part of an immediate mimetic reciprocity; hence they must be explained in terms of a more distal and more longstanding form of
social learning, as well as in terms of an immediate engagement in
forms of strategic calculation that are not immediately determined
by states of desire.
An awareness of the infernal logic of the social drama being
played out by their wives and associates also plays a crucial role in
the actions of Njall and Gunnarr. If there is a great deal of supernaturalism in Njall’s prophetic foretelling of future events, there is
at the same time an important element of sociological insight:
given a knowledge of certain relations, motivations, and prior ac-

tions of others, it 1s sometimes possible to predict quite accurately
what their next move has to be. Thus the supernatural themes of
fatality and destiny find an echo 1n a purely social and much more
plausible kind of foresight, one having its basis in the fact that a
person’s range of possible actions can be horribly narrow at a certain stage in a social situation. At the same time, however, the
story suggests that a knowledge of social interaction can sometimes help the actors to avoid disaster.
Thus there intervenes in the system of feuding, with its mimetic
and other motivations, a different and to some extent counteract-

ing element, namely, a knowledge of the dynamics of the feud itself. This knowledge is by no means total, nor does it govern or
control the action, replacing a mythical realm of total mimetic
puppetry with an equally mythical realm of absolute autonomy
and freedom. Nevertheless, Gunnarr and Njall’s temporary forestalling of the conflict is an excellent example of how such a knowledge can under some conditions manage to vitiate a mimetic system of rivalry and violence, replacing it with a self-governance
serving to maintain the peace: Gunnarr refuses to seek compensation, thereby temporarily putting a halt to the cycle, and Njall

169 - Desire, Violence, and Revenge

takes it upon himself to offer compensation so as to reestablish
parity with his friend. Here we have a cooperative form of mimesis
that is not based on any form of delusion or violence. In such moments, which are far too rare in the world of the sagas, the agents
act on their knowledge of the ultimate consequences of being
driven by an emulative mimesis. This is a knowledge exemplified
by the folk wisdom repeated by Rannveig, Gunnarr’s mother, in a
prophetic phrase with which it may be appropriate to conclude
the present chapter: “It is said that the hand is soon sorry that it
Sstruck.”3}

Conclusion

In this book I have not reported on the results of any systematic
empirical studies, and I am consequently not in a position to make
any scientific claims about the frequency or significance of mimetic
desire in people’s lives. Nor can I present any evidence concerning
the role such a phenomenon may have played in directing the
course of human history. Instead, the results of this study are interpretive and conceptual. In a sense I have asked the reader to engage
in a game of make-believe in which we try to imagine, with some

detail and rigor, what the world would have to be like if we were to
generalize and to extend René Girard’s readings of literary and
cultural documents. Thus I have asked how some of his claims
about mimetic desire could be reconciled with the way we use “desire” and related terms in our everyday understandings of ourselves and others, relying on my own intuitions and on those of
some philosophers and psychologists who have written on the
topic. One of my conclusions is that the idea of “desire according
to the other” is not as simple and univocal as it may seem, but I also
conclude that once it has been unpacked a bit, the notion identifies
some types ofsituations that seem plausible and important.
The idea of mimetic desire has a basic, intuitive appeal. It seems
right to conjecture that when the appetites have been sated, people
often do not know what they want. Yet this uncertainty may be
coupled with the perception that the actions of others are guided
by unswerving and confident attitudes. Some of these persons
stand out as salient models to be followed, and when the right attitudes about such a model are in place, a mimetic adoption of that
model’s desires can follow. Today’s noisy public discourses of stardom, personality, and persuasion are designed to give rise to this
sort of modeling. Persons designated as our superiors invite us to
170

171 + Models of Desire

desire what they desire, even when their prestige and pleasures are
nonexistent or well out of our reach. Yet an impersonal and transient form of modeling is probably not the most significant and
effective variety of mimetic desire. Parents, friends, lovers, and

educators serve as long-term personal mediators whose desires,
real or imagined, inspire longings patterned after them. It is hard
to imagine a personal history devoid of such relationships, and
Girard has ably demonstrated that many of our best narratives
and dramas systematically evoke them. Girard also claims that the
~“great texts” trace some of the typical patterns that emerge when
these mimetic relations go wrong. Threatened by the successes of
the disciple, the mediator withholds encouragement and begins to
compete, vitiating cooperation and learning. In a world that prizes
individual talent and success, strategies that disguise imitation and
cooperation unfold and are encouraged, but often at the cost of
delusion and deceit. A runaway system of competition metes out
its bitter rewards of failure and envy.
The mimetic hypothesis has an intuitive appeal. But is it also
plausible to speculate that our disposition to desire “according to
others” has a hidden and systematic orientation, a latent structure
or dynamic, a “logic” that would warrant a rigorous use of the
term “mechanism”? Can a mechanism of mimesis be used to explain the genesis of patterns of culture and its institutions? Perhaps it can, and I do not claim to have established the contrary. I
am not persuaded by sweeping neo-Kantian arguments in favor of
a purely idiographic approach in psychology and anthropology.
But when it is a matter ofa claim that some particular mechanism
generates and explains some complex phenomenon, the burden of
proof lies on the shoulders of those who make this claim. They
should show us a blueprint of the mechanism and describe how a
working model of it can be constructed. In my view, the very best
descriptions of mimetic desire that we have do not specify anything like a mechanism, if by this is meant a finite and closed system of factors that function together to produce certain types of
effects. “Subject, mediator, and the spectacle of the mediator’s de-

sire yield mimetic desire” does not define a mechanism in this
sense. I have tried to show that this basic mimetic formula plausi-

172 « Conclusion

bly yields at least two very different kinds of motivational states
and that “mimetic desire” therefore divides into two significantly
different kinds, the imitative and the emulative. The attitudes that

characterize these two sorts of mimetic episodes display only a
highly schematic similarity, and I have not been able to describe
any laws or patterns governing transitions between them. Under
what general conditions does a tutelary belief in the hierarchical
distance and superiority of a mediator transform itself into an envious vision of the other’s temporary advantage? This is an outstanding problem within the mimetic hypothesis.
Other outstanding problems have to do with the hypothesis
that mimetic mechanisms explain certain social dynamics. Even if
we were to suppose that a mimetic machine functioned to produce
a single type of motivational state in agents, we would still need to
refer to a host of other factors to determine whether— and how—
these agents might then act on those states. Given the agent’s other
attitudes, the mimetic desire need not turn out to be the preponderant motivational state, and even should it do so, it could moti-

vate a number of significantly different types of behavior. Suppose
I am overwhelmed by a mimetic desire to win the prize that I think
my obstacle-model covets. I may believe there is nothing I can do
to enhance my chances of winning the prize, or I may choose one
among several schemes that emerge from the tangled network of
my background beliefs about the ways and means of the world.
But which of the many possible options will emerge? Applying a
very broad rationality heuristic, we can anticipate that in such a
situation an agent always chooses a course of action that is subjectively appropriate. In other words, the agent will select means that
he or she believes may bring about the desired end.! But even if
this clause is granted, we still do not have an effective functional
specification of the type of behavior that is to ensue.
To evoke a literary example, the reader (or listener) who is
about halfway into Njal’s Saga should have a pretty good idea that
Hallgerdr is driven by a terrible form of envy and that she is willing to inflict any sort of harm she can on her enemies. A more
reflective reader may even have a keen sense of her tactics and may
have observed that she always tries to find a way to get her hus-

173 - Models of Desire

band to have to seek retribution from someone related to her
rivals. But it still comes as a surprise when we learn of her attempt
to create trouble by having a slave steal some food, even if we take
the line about her “thief’s” seriously. The theft seems ineffectual
and lowly, and it is unclear how she can believe that it will advance
her cause. But this unanticipated machination begins to make
more sense when we learn that by openly displaying the stolen
goods and making the theft known, Hallgerdr can get her husband further embroiled in the feud. Retrospectively, the action fits
-well within a plan that flows coherently from her motives, but in a
devious and sordid way. The action makes sense, then, but we do

not have a theoretical mechanism that generates or determines the
link between her desire and the scheme that is devised to satisfy it.
More generally, when I try to make sense of the sequence of actions that figure within the cycle of violent retributions, I must
include such a diversity of factors that it is hard to believe that the
mimetic motivations specify the “system” of those interactions as a
whole. My conclusion is that the mimetic concepts describe a pattern of pertinent and typical motivational factors that may play a
role in such affairs but that they should not be mistaken for an
explanatory device that literally generates a functional description
of the types of actions and interactions that make up the cycle of
revenge.
Girard once responded to this sort of objection by saying that
he only uses the term “mechanism” to mean that many crucial aspects of the mimetic process unfold in the absence of “conscious
choice.” But reframing the issue in this manner cannot help in the
absence of a solution to the enormous problem of saying what
consciousness is and does. The psychology of attitudes discussed
in this book, the psychology with which Girard and the rest of us
implicitly and explicitly operate in making sense of ourselves and
others, does not stand or fall with a solution to the problem of
consciousness. We assume instead that conscious introspection 1s
fallible and that beliefs and desires can be functionally modeled as
dispositions, and not only as sensations or states of awareness. For
example, in everyday usage, the belief ascription “the reader believes his or her eyes are focused on the book right now” does not

174 + Conclusion

entail the ascription “the reader presently has a conscious, focal
belief that his or her eyes are focused on the book.” To say that the
mimetic selection of a model or a scapegoat is often— perhaps
most often—not the product of a reflective decision does not support the conclusion that perceptions, beliefs, and motives having
semantic content do not contribute to its orientation. Once we observe that the selection of victims converges on individuals who
bear typical signs of difference—the “stereotypes of persecution”
that Girard so brilliantly describes—we are no longer dealing
with a “blind” or “purely arbitrary” process. That process not only
is unjust but is based on a cognitive error about the causal powers
and responsibilities of the victims, who are wrongly identified as
the source of a vast social crisis that they could not possibly have
brought about. To situate that error beneath the level of “consciousness” or “self-reflection” is one step; to deny the role of meaningful
attitudes in a mimetic process is another. Both are steps into the
dark, the former because we have no real understanding of consciousness, the latter because it cancels the descriptive and explanatory program of a psychology of meaningful attitudes, situating
“the action” at a level of description that has never been specified.
Taking that second step would deprive us of our means of critiquing and trying to forestall the kind of sacrificial error that the theory identifies.
It is my conviction that the Girardian critique of scapegoating
works very well. It identifies with an unprecedented degree of precision some patterns of interaction and attitudes that appear across
a wide range of contexts and domains. The bread and butter of the
movie industry is a ritualistic fictional evocation of the resolution
of a violent crisis by means of the heroic extermination of a monstrous scapegoat. Similar fantasies also provide the scenarios for
some of our most earnest political dramas. Girard is right to point
out that many aspects of our social order of norms and cooperation
are achieved and maintained through a basic pattern of exclusion.
Yet I have on several occasions stated my reservations about some
of the second-order claims that Girard makes for the explanatory
scope of these descriptions. What was said about the putative
autonomous causal mechanisms of mimetic desire holds equally

175 + Models of Desire

well for the notion that a single “scapegoat mechanism” could
serve as the motor of societal dynamics. Even less am I persuaded
by Girard’s claim that this cultural pattern was generated by a
mechanism of nonrepresentational mimicry. I find the kinds of scenarios explored in chapter 4 ambitious and interesting, but only
the most reckless gambler would wager that any one of them is the
right story about the emergence of human culture. In regard to
some topics, one has no choice but to place one’s bets on some
risky belief, but surely the origin of human culture is not one of
_ them.
One last word about René Girard’s achievement. He often castigates our academic “literary culture” for its intellectual nihilism
and sterility. No doubt he exaggerates, but in my view his criticisms do single out some prevalent (but by no means omnipresent) aspects of contemporary literary scholarship. Girard complains that critics often fail to demonstrate, when they do not flatly
betray, the cognitive value of literature and of literary research.
His own work, he adds, has convinced him that “the most per-

spicacious texts from the standpoint of human relationships are
the great texts of Western literature.” Instead of just applying
ready-made theories such as psychoanalysis to literary works,
critics should try to articulate the valuable insights that the works
themselves have to offer. I totally agree, and it seems to me that
Girard deserves to be recognized as one of a tiny minority ofcritics
who have achieved this goal. If we are to follow the example he has
set, our task is not to apply the mimetic hypotheses but to see how
creative readings of literary and other texts can help us to make
them stronger.

6. Such an explanatory deflation is typically the artifact of unrealistic
models of scientific explanation. Sources on explanation that I have found

7. Here and in what follows I am selectively adopting the terminology
of Barwise and Perry (1983, 1985). Roughly speaking, these authors use
the term “situation” to pick out a collection of particulars, relations (and
types thereof) in a single space-time location, while a “course of events”
refers to a similar collection defined across more than one location.
“Types” of situations or courses of events are ones having at least one variable in them (instead of only particulars), and “abstract” types of situations or courses of events are ones that do not correspond to, or classify,
real world situations (although they may be the object of an intentional
attitude). In order to avoid using too much jargon, I shall speak of “situations” only, including under that term what Barwise and Perry have referred to as “courses of events.” In the present context I do not require the
strong claim that these concepts, and the associated notation, can ade-

quately characterize all possible contents of desire; indeed, if the contents
of desire included all possible events (including, for example, unlocated
exemplifications of a property), such a claim would be indefensible. On
the metaphysics of events, see Bennett 1988 and Cleland 1991. On the
more general question of the individuation of semantic contents, see, for a

start, Richard 1990. I cannot enter here into the burgeoning “broad
versus narrow content” debate; see, e.g., Heil and Mele 1991, Jackson and
Pettit 1990, and Patterson 1990.

14. I have heard the argument that “X desires to help Y keep his seat”
cannot be the object of an instance of mimetic desire because X’s and Y’s

are not. The argument also would have the consequence of excluding all
cases of external mediation from the category of mimetic desire. There is
no law against such a stipulation, but it should be kept in mind that such a
move still does not answer the question why agents have acquisitive desires and why they sometimes imitate others’ desires in an acquisitive and
rivalrous fashion.

1. A similar point has been expressed as follows by Perry and Bussey
in a survey of social psychological studies on imitation in children: “Much
of the early work on children’s imitation focused on factors that cause
children to adopt specific actions they have seen displayed by a model.
What makes a girl scold or nurture her doll in ways that resemble how her
mother has treated her? Why does a boy imitate aggressive acts he has seen
in a television cartoon? But focusing on children’s imitation of specific
acts was soon recognized to be an insufficient approach to the study of
modeling influences. Children learn far more than specific actions
through modeling” (1984, 111).

well-founded epistemic notion they are willing to allow in such instances.

28. My point here could perhaps find some support in Neisser’s
(1988) distinctions between different sources of self-knowledge.
29. Girard’s claim 1s that all of the positions in the structures described

185 - Notes to Pages 101—127
by his theory of mimetic desire can be occupied by both men and women.
(He does not make the empirical claim, however, that in all cultures these

positions are occupied by men and women in equal proportions or with
equal facility). Girard has been called a sexist thinker who fails to notice
the essential differences between masculine and feminine desire. For example, see Kofman 1980, where we are told that Freud was not wrong
when he expressed his belief in a specifically female libidinal formation
associated with a concept of “narcissism.” Girard’s criticisms of Freud are
supposed to reveal a lack of attention to “the text of Freud.” Fortunately
for the important project known as feminism, it is not the case that all
. feminists must believe in narcissism and in an essential difference between
masculine and feminine desire; nor is it the case that Freudianism is the

only, or even the best, psychological framework for elaborating feminist
insights. It seems most prudent and interesting to recognize that it is presently an open question whether the theory of mimetic desire can provide
accurate descriptions of women’s—and men’s— motivational processes.

1. Such an analysis is currently being pursued in a creative and informed manner by Cesareo Bandera.
2. Girard’s anthropology, then, bears many of the features discussed in

3. Fora more general statement of the rationale behind such a literary
reading, see Livingston 1986, 1988b. David Lewis (1983) has couched
the key idea as follows: “We who have lived in the world for a while have
plenty of evidence, but we may not have learned as much from it as we
could have done. This evidence bears on a certain proposition. If only that
proposition is formulated, straightway [sic] it will be apparent that we
have very good evidence for it. If not, we will continue not to know it.

187 - Notes to Pages 142—157
Here, fiction can help us. If we are given a fiction such that the proposition
is obviously true in it, we are led to ask: and is it also true simpliciter? And
sometimes, when we have plenty of unappreciated evidence, to ask the
question is to know the answer. Then the author of the fiction has made a
discovery, and he gives his readers the means to make that same discovery
for themselves.”
